THE RED PAGE.
There exists no adequate biography of Nelson.
Southey’s is the best, chiefly because it is
most free from the patriotic bias which insists
that home-grown geese are inevitably swans; and
from the mental incapacity which attributes all-
round perfection to anyone brilliant on a single
line. There are immense numbers of people who,
because Dickens wrote successful novels, would
hear with respect his judgment on horseflesh. Or,
conversely, many valiant Australian riders are
assured of Gordon’s poetical quality because he
leaped a dangerous fence at Mount Gambier.
• * * *
But Southey’s life of Nelson is in many respects
a mere ssetch ; you have to get a picture of the
hero more from what he hints than what he states.
His style, though lucid and strong, is neither
very vehement nor very fascinating. Yet, mak-
ing allowance for the anti-Gallic feeling from
which an Englishman of Southey’s day cor.’ 1
hardly escape, his judgment is singularly good.
He may glide over Nelson’s weaknesses, but he
does not gloss them over. He omits much, but
in strong contrast to other of Nelson’s biographers
—Laughton, for example—he perverts nothing.
Southey saw that Nelson’s greatness offered
magnificent excuse for his failings and that there
was no need of special pleading to justify them.
* * » *
As Southey’s life of Nelson is the best life, so
the-new edition of it which David Hannay edits
is the best edition. The editor is able and im-
partial. Occasionally his phrases concede a trifle
to British prejudice : he is hypothetical where he
might justly be assertive: but his obeisance to
national pride is never so marked as to be servile.
Hannay’s notes and appendices condense infor-
mation only otherwise to be obtained by study of
many half - forgotten volumes. Supplementing
Southey’s story, they make that story as com-
plete as it is possible to make it. But the nature
and extent of them prove it time for a full and
philosophic life of Nelson. Southey’s musket
can be furbished into a decent weapon : it can
never be the equal of a modern rifle.
* • * *
To no man more truly than to Nelson can be
applied the word “ hero ’’—either in its ordinary
or in a more recherehb and enlightened significa-
tion. He compelled and dominated by nature :
success was an instinct with him. The flash of
his eye bred a nervous exaltation in whomsoever
it lighted on. His mere presence was thrilling.
Whether in the fleet or with the Portsmouth
populace this personal stimulus was the same.
Like Napoleon, he seemed a man apart, made for
the common herd to admire, and cherish,
and follow without judgment or hesitation. Yet
physically Nelson was almost contemptible. To-
wards the end of his career that small, shrivelled,
mutilated, weather-beaten carcase was the last
shrine one would choose for a popular idol. Intel-
lectually, outside his profession, his powers were
barely mediocre. Even in his profession he had
little more than a good seaman’s strategy, dis-
played few resources of generalship. But he could
fight, and make others fight, incomparably. It is
hardly an exaggeration to say that after the Nile
his name was worth a dozen line-of-battle ships :
so glorious was the confidence he inspired in his
own men, so signal the fear he struck into those of
the enemy. The worst of his biographers has not
failed to catch from the fire of his spirit a glow
which warms one through the dullest and most
halting pages.
But Nelson’s worshippers are rarely satisfied
with saying all this ■ they seem to think it duty
to try and wash out with weak apologies the blots
upon their hero’s scutcheon. Even the disgraces
of Naples have found bigoted defenders. Naples
was Nelson’s Oapuu. He came thither after
Abonkir, and for twenty months lay, actually or
metaphorically, in.the arms of Emma Hamilton,
a coarse, vigorous, enticing prostitute to whom
Nelson in turn prostituted his own honor and
that of the fleet which he commanded. For twenty
months he devoted his energy and employed his
vessels in propping the throne of a loutish, ignoble
tyrant, time and again disobeying the direct
orders of his superior officer to go in chase
of the hostile fleet. With a head turned by
flattery and female seductions, he was so com-
plaisant to the cruel Neapolitan queen —a sister
of Marie Antoinette, and even more superstitious
and vengeful—that he tore up a capitulation
signed by the English, Russian, and Neapolitan
representatives, and sacrificed the wretched
“rebels” against the worst government in Europe
to the worst cruelties which that government
could invent. When Cardinal Ruffo himself,
whose character was black even for a priest,
wished to keep trust with the men to whom lie on
h.-half of the Neapolitan monarch, and Captain
Foote on behalf of England, had pledged liberty
and life, it was left to Nelson to insist on a
scandalous breach of faith, and to hang at a ship’s
yard-arm, after an infamous mockery of a trial
which in any case Nelson had no right to conduct,
Admiral Garacciolo, who had served with the de-
feated party prior to the capitulation.
* * * *
Southey has said rather less about this conduct
than Nelson deserves, but the little he has said
gives reason for every undiseriminating loyalist to
denounce his incompetency. But Nelson’s actions
speak for themselves to every honest man, and
would shout his folly and barbarity if not a com-
mentator had expressed honest indignation
regarding them. It is charitable to suppose,
what no doubt approximates to the truth, that
Nelson was never quite sane after his head was
wounded at Aboukir, and on that ground, while
justly denouncing his deeds at Naples, to absolve
him as far as maybe from complete responsibility.
Harry Morant, otherwise “ The Breaker,
came out of the sun-smitten West for a Sydney
New Year, and was farewelled in this fashion by
another bright Bulletin bard—W. H. Ogilvie,
otherwise “ Glenrovvan,” who partners him_ in
horse-swapping, and droving, and verse-making,
and whatever of love and iiquor ihe gay gods
send—
I Then “The Breaker” came to Sydney and
planked all the money of his “ crowd ” on a horse
which ran second, instead of first, in the Rand-
wick Steeplechase, and “ the crowd ” went broke
gaiiy. „ „
Of all the bush poets now writing, Ogilvie in
style and capacity approaches nearest to the
lamented Boake. (Boake’s collected work, with
a memoir by A. G. Stephens, is shortly
to be published by Angus and Robertson and per-
mission of The Bulletin for the benefit of his
family.) The author of the thrilling “StoreCattle
from Nelanjie,” and other fine Bulletin verse, is a
pleasant-faced young Scotchman midway in his
twenties, who has almost acquired the air of a
bushman born. He does not brood quite so much
as Boake did, and touches hardly such deep
springs of feeling ; but he has all Boake’s rhyming
faculty and quick-pulsed sympathy. Boake,
indeed, used often to weave his lines much in the
manner with which a Quarterly Reviewer up-
braided Keats, letting the thought follow the
rhyme instead of leading it. So, for example, in
these hitherto unprinted lines —which have, alas !
a note of the personal and prophetic in them—
addressed to one who lightly said that “bush-
men cannot love.”
Where waters thread —doth bid us stay :
Our passions for a month, a week,
Flare out and then they die away—
For separation, like the creek
That stays the fire, bars the way.
You say we bushmen cannot love:
Well, have it so ! but this I swear—
That she possessed a power to move
The dullest boor to do or dare.
But I, as being somewhat shy,
Became the target for her wit . • •
How oft in wantonness she'd pit
The blazing lances of her eye
And keener rapier of her tongue,
That carelessly made lightning play,
Until to action I was stung,
And, like a dumb beast, stood at bay. ...
* * * *
Poor Boake ! shy, sensitive, a dreamer to whom
Rogers’ tine saying of Byron applies truly and
wholly— , . ,
. . . his heart, methinks,
Was generous, noble—noble in its scorn
Of all things low or littll- nothing there
Sordid or servile —
the sweet influences of sex swayed him strongly
—his heart leapt, as all bushmen’s hearts, to the
rustle of a petticoat. And then ... to end so
—a suicide at 25. But, unless it be suicide at
24, what better ending is left for the baffled
idealist ?
—4. ?
Noticing W. Garrett Border’s recently-issued
“Treasury of Hymns,” SATURDAY Review says
things which needed re-saying :
This is a collection of 1042 hymns by about 450 writers,
and is evidence enough of the immense popularity of the
religious ballad. It will excite no dismay or surprise in
most folk who behold it; and yet how surprising it really
is that religious emotion should express itself no better
than in miles of doggerel ballads. Hymns began with
the Arians; they have flourished almost in inverse pro-
portion to the health of the Church, and they have par
ticularly abounded in the decadent and unpoetic periods
of thought. Saints tike Anatolius, Clement, Ambrose,
Hilary, and Augustine are, it is true, the fathers of
hymn-writing, and both they and many writers since
have summed up their deepest feelings in lofty, severe,
and restrained verse, and some of these noble poems have
survived and been interpreted for English people by
Chandler, Neale, and others; but, unfortunately, the
vision of spiritual things, which struck great saints all
but silent in the face of great mysteries, has been denied
to the voluble and gushing grocers who now retail false
■sentiment in halting verses. If we take a single verse of
an ancient hymn and examine it by the side of a modern
verse we shall immediately discover the root reason for
the vapidness of modern jingle. Let us place together a
verse of St. Ambrose and one by Mr. Bubier: —
“ Splendor Paternse Glori® “ I would commune with
Thee, my God—
De luce lucem proferens, E’en to Thy seat, 1 come;
Lux Lucis et fons luminis, I leave my joys, I leave my
sins,
Dies diem illuminans.” And seek in Thee my
home.”
• * * *
The one author has forgotten himself. He does not
even remember that he is weary or a wastrel, or has lost
angel faces or anything of the sort: he does not descant
upon his diseases. He does not congratulate himself, like
Phoebe Cary, that he is “nearer home,” or “beat the
void ” with Eliza Scudder’s “ restless pinion,” or with
Monsell assure the Almighty that he bears Him in mind,
or with Faber wish to have no wishes left, or with the
little Halls chirrup “I'm thy little birdie,” or remind
the Holy Ghost, as the excited Mr. Bonar did, that he
needed calming, or with Mr. J. Sharp explain “I cannot
bear the thought that I may start, Away from Thee,” or
with smug Thomas Hornblower Gill sing “ My God ! I do
not flee from Thee." St. Ambrose would have regarded
it as profane to perk his personality into the inaccessible
light. There is no personal element at all in his deep and
passionate lines. We are on a lower level the moment
the personal element comes in, even when it is intro-
duced by a master singer, even when it is as delicately in-
troduced as in the fine lines “ Quaerens me sedisti lussus,”
or in Cowper’s “ Sometimes a light surprises.” In Mr.
Bubier's vulgar verses we have every imaginable hymn-
vice concentrated—impertinence, inexact use of language,
baldness, unreality, irreverence, lack of the musical sense.
It is a far cry from Heaven to Beckham, Mr. Bubier's real
home.
* * • *
No doubt there are a few hymns which are also noble
poems, and Mr. Horder has thrust into his volume, to
plump it out, some noble poems vyhieh are in no sense
hymns; hut to get a synoptical view of what is false,
mawkish, profane, and illegitimate, a modern hymnal is
the best coign of vantage. It is, indeed, an index of how
sharp-set, and how starved, the religious appetite must be
that it will endure to banquet upon such husks. The
editor of the present collection, however, is very phased
with himself, and he offers his collection hopefully and
cheerfully to the “widely-spread culture rf our day.”
If that sprawling nebulous abstraction were healthy and
well-knit it would he anything but grateful. Ilow can
any one, forsooth, but a spiritual surgeon he grateful for
a swollen parcel of religious dropsies ? If we cannot stay
I the plague ofhjmns, nt *a.t it might be reduced to a
minimum by a sever, h careful hymu;b<K,fe
pruned and pared relentlessly, and issued by the bench
bishops.
The fine fleshly fascination of “ Victoria Cross
makes “Paula” a pleasant yarn for a lazy Sun-
day morning. There is no great skill in'the book,.
but a good deal of sentimenUl stmculence for the
literary bon vivcur. Anti Paula bt-rself ,ui a
belated Greek girl, who believes in wrtl.mR: but
Art and Love, and kisses qmte naturally, witho t
being asked, the second time you meet her. A t
at all music-hall, but thoroughly marsim, 1-hall.
As, for example. ...
“Good-bye,- he said, and she put her hand in his. it
was held there, and she looked up. She was drawn a
little nearer, and then somehow in some soft but irre-
sistible way she found herself folded into his arms, close
against his breast, and his lips against hers. Swayed iy
some power that s-emed quite new to her and beyond
herself, she linked her arms suddenly round his neck and
kissed him back with her warm, smooth lips in a quick,
responsive, passionate fervor. All the gratitude to him
for the pleasure of that happy afternoon, all the appre-
ciation of his charm and the sense of violent attraction
towards him, found relief in that impulsive kiss. It was
not the kiss of passion, nor even love. These had not
been stirred as yet; it was rather of enthusiastic admira-
tion, as she might have bent suddenly over the page of
a book that stirred her and kissed it with vehement
delight. As he had said ‘Good-bye,’ words had been
leaping to her lips and striving in her brain- words to
tell him how she admired him, how great her pleasure
with him had been. Then suddenly in his arms, so near
his heart, there had seemed no. way to express it all hut
this no way so simple, so natural, so utterly satisfying,
so necessaiy as this, and in a pure spontaneous enthu-
siasm she let all the strength of her fervid soul rush out
upon her lips as they met his. It ran like quick fire
through the man’s whole nervous, excitable being, but
he gave no outward sign.
Query: If she had had “large, curled lips,”
like Irene, instead of merely 44 warm, smooth
ditto, would he have given any outward sign?

WHEN “THE BREAKER" IS BOOKED FOR THE
SOUTH.
I He will leave, when his ticket is tendered,
A bundle of debts, I'm afraid —
Accounts that were many times rendered
And bills that will never be paid ;
Whilst the tailor and riding-boot maker
Will stand with their thumbs in their mouth
With a three-cornered curse at “ The Breaker ”
When “ The Breaker ” has booked for the South !
There’s a fence at the back of Small’s stable —
Five feet—and a bristle of spikfs !
But a rider who’s willing and able
Will make the horse jump, if he lues.
They will say we are mad, or have dreamed it,
For there’s no one from Lachlan to Louth
Will cross in the dark where the cream did—
When "The Breaker” has booked for the South !
There’s a girl—when the white moonbeams glisten
And the stars shine out over the roofs—
Will stand by the railings and listen
For the beat of that cream horse’s hoofs!
Will stand by the rails and remember
The last kiss that was press’d on her mouth,
On a certain sad day in December-
On the day when “ The Breaker” went South!
There are comrades who’ll miss him at polo
And look for his hard phiz, in vain,
And it’s twenty to one that they’ll follow,
And “ shout ” for “ The Breaker ” again ;
Whilst a rest shall come after the riot,
And drinking shall lapse into drouth,
For the town will go back to its quiet—
When “ The Breaker ” has booked for the South !
You say we bushmen cannot love:
Our live* are too prosaic ; hence
We lose or lack that finer sense
That raises some lew men above
Their fellows, setting them apart
As vessels of a finer make,
The acme of the potter’s art.
Are placed apart upon the shelf:
So he is more than common delf,
And more than brute in human guise,
Who, seeking, finds his nobler self
Twin-mirrored in a woman’s e>es.
Yet these things bring their penalty :
For oft the merest touch will break
These vessels of a finer make ;
And throats attuned to noblest key
A draught of air will set awry,
And stifle in an ulcerous sore
That voice that floated to the sky,
And silence it for evermore.
You say we bushmen cannot love—
That, like our foe, the fire-fiend,
We blaze, until a river-bend—
Nay, less, a pebble-paven groove
PRIMROSES.
They shine upon my table there,
A constellation mimic sweet:
No stars in heaven could shine more fair,
Nor earth has beauty more complete ;
And on my table there they shin",
And spea>- to me of things divine.
In Heaven at 8rst they grew, and when
God could no fairer make tin m, He
Did plant them by the ways of men
For all the pure in heart to see,
That each might shine upon its stem
And be a light from Him to them.
They speak of things above iny verse,
Of thoughts no earthly language knows,
That loftiest hard could ne’er rehearse,
Nor holiest proph»t e’er disclose,
Which God Himself no other way
Than by a primrose could convey.

n . Was everyone a liar?
Ur could it only be the spook of Dunn, of Never-

Then I smiled (though somewhat sadly) as I

ol 'their papers, and, according to
There’s not a place called Nevertire in all of New

And Jack looked at me blankly, and his eyebrows
rose up higher,
Till he gasped in rank amazement: “No such
place as Nevertire ?
Well, you’re the sort of liar
I really do admire :
And p r’aps I’m dead, and not Jack Dunn, and
not from Nevertire ! ”
I picked the morning paper from the floor beside
my feet,
And I said: “I’ve got some news for you that
isn t very sweet.
Ihey have started newer fashions there, where
once you used to dwell,
And they ve just invented cyclones, and the town
is blown to Hell.
Perhaps you’ll find some gutters, and a bit of
tencmg-wire—
But there’s nothing else but sandheaps where
there once was Nevertire ;
No, there ain’t no Nevertire,
t For it's been lifted higher ;
It s dead as you were thought to be, Jack Dunn
of Nevertire ! ”
Vic.
THE PAGAN.
ODE: TO THE “SOUTHERLY BUSTER.”
Wind of the South, all hail !
Oh, wind that comest roaring' from the sea,
That comest grand and glorious, fresh and free !
All day we longed and listened for thy feet,
We drooped and sickened in the stifling heat.
Night came. The darkness came and still no
breath,
No breeze to stir the air, and still as death !
At midnight hour from restless couch I rise
And leaning from the casement, scan the skies.
No speck, no cloud—no hope of gentle rain !
The cruel stars smile down upon our pain.
But hark ! a low, dull murmur from the South—
It comes, O drooping hearts ! O land of drouth !
The good South wind, the strong South wind up-
springs.
I feel its breath ! I hear its rushing wings !
And louder still, and plainer and more plain,
The breeze blows in upon my burning brain.
Oh, roaring wind that o’er the land dost sweep,
Oh, rushing wings that bring us longed-for sleep,
Blow on, blow swift and strong, blow peace and
rest
To weary thousands, faint and heat-oppressed,
Who list with joy the fury of the gale,
Who rise up in the night and cry, “ All hail! ”
Wind of the South, all hail!
JESEIE PATERSON.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable Ms. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humwous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 16, 1897.
The Past Year’s Boom
The Westralian boom has got to the end of
another year of its existence, and it is still
alive, though there are signs that it is getting
near to the brink of disaster. Many outside
circumstances have combined to keep the
boom afloat so long. Money in England has
of late been cheap and very abundant, while
promising investments have been scarce,
therefore unpromising ones have had a better
chance than usual. Gold has always a
fascination for the speculator, and Aus-
tralia’s past repute as a gold-producer helped
the boom along. To J. Bull, Westralia
was a field that was green by reason of its
remoteness, just as Paraguay looked to
Lane’s pilgrims like a land of extraordinary
verdure. The fact that a very large
proportion of the scanty gold-output was
obtained in three immense masses from
Bayley’s Reward, the disastrous Lon-
donderry and the Great Boulder added
a beautiful glamour to a total which
was comparatively small in mere ounces,
and which, if it had been a little more
evenly divided among the mines actually
working, would have looked very poor
and barren. Also, the British boomer
loves big things to float and boodle and lie
about, and Westralia, with its 975,000 square
miles of territory, mostly empty and with
their possibilities not exploded by too much
exploration, was a splendid field for romance.
The province came to the front, too, just
when the great Rand boom had revived the
faith of the stolid Briton in big gold dis-
coveries, and when the men who grabbed
too late at the South African fields were
eager to grab in good time at the next
chance which came along. Therefore, as the
natural consequence, they grabbed altogether
too soon, and Westralia got the benefit of
their mistake. All these and other circum-
stances helped to make W.A. the great
bubble land it has been. But though the
arrival of the boqm can be fully accounted
for, its continuance, considering the great
scarcity of solid results, remains a good deal
of a mystery.
* * * *
Westralia lives, to all intents and pur-
poses, on gold alone —or, to be more accu-
rate, it booms on gold alone. For 1896 its
yield was about 281,2630 z. ; the figures may
not be absolutely accurate, but that is the
official record at time of writing. In 1892
the yield was 59,5000 z. In 1893 it was
110,800 oz. ; in ’94, 207,0000 z. ; and in ’95,
231,0000 z. Consequently, this year there is a
reported increase of 50,0000 z., equal to an im-
provement of £189,000. This would be a
good increase in any country that wasn’t
staggering under so stupendous a load of
British capital, but even if the gold-yield
was all profit it wouldn’t pay quite ljj per
cent, on the capital of the various mining
companies, and even the reconstructed Colo-
nial Bank pays 2| per cent, to its very much
fixed depositors. Moreover, the Colonial
Bank pays back the principal when the
transaction is closed ; a mine never does.
When its business is finished there is no-
thing left to represent the capital save a hole
in the ground and a small amount of old
machinery, which is either abandoned as not
worth removing or sold for a fraction of its
original cost. Therefore, it certainly is not
the dividends, so far as they have gone,
which keep the Westralian boom alive. If
1| per cent, interest and no return of the
principal could make a boom, then the rush
for deposits in the Colonial Bank, where the
interest is nearly double and the principal is
returnable, would be so intense that cus-
tomers would tread each other under foot in
their struggle to get their money into the
reconstructed institution.
* * * •
Moreover, after allowing for working ex-
penses, it is more than doubtful if the Wes-
tralian goldfields, as a whole, pay any
dividend whatever. There is very solid
reason for believing that the gold actually
costs more to produce than it is worth. There
have been years when calls exceeded divi-
dends in Victoria, and Victoria produces far
more metal under conditions which make the
cost of production a great deal less. As
another curious circumstance, it may be men-
tioned that Victoria, in 1896, produced
84.000 ounces more than in 1895, so it had a
60 per cent, larger increase than Westralia.
Yet there was no aggravated boom in the
Melbourne mining market ; no great rush of
outside investors, no large influx of popula-
tion as in W.A., and, though the revenue
improved, it didn’t rise with the sensational
whoop of the western province. This seems
to show that a gold-boom and gold produc-
tion have no essential relationship to each
other ; there may be the production without
the boom or vice-versa. But the gold-boom
which is not based on solid ounces and dwt.
can’t be of an enduring character. The
Westralian mines have to pay dividends on a
capital which is probably more than four
times as great as that of the mines of Vic-
toria. To do so on the Victorian scale—
which, taking matters all round, is now a very
moderate one—the output needs to be fully
ten times what it is, provided that expenses
can be brought down to the Victorian level.
If they can’t be brought down to that level,
then the production needs to be twelve or
fifteen times as large as at present. And
when it reaches that point, of which there is no
present prospect, the only reasonable infer-
ence is that the industry willmaintain the same
steady - going position as that of Victoria,
and seeing that the latter province can’t raise
even the shadow of a gold-boom, and merely
plods along with its mineral industry in
much the same fashion as it does with its
wheat and butter and other unemotional
products, even this glorious consummation,
if it can be reached, which seems hopelessly
improbable, holds out no prospect of the
boom’s continuance.
* * * *
The present situation of Westralia is one
vast anomaly, and another especially anomal-
ous feature is shown in the public revenue.
As already mentioned, the gold-production,
in 1896, increased to the extent of £189,000
as compared with ’95. There was no notable
improvement in any other industry. Com-
paring 1890 with 1894—these being the
earliest and latest years for which the W.A.
Statistician supplies any information—and
dealing with all products except gold, the
exports show a decline in nearly every item.
Even the mysterious trade in dogs dropped
from £l5O to £2 ; fish went down from £25
to 20s. ; the total wine exports fell from £29
to £l9 ; and half-a-dozen items vanished
altogether. Yet, on the strength of this
increase of £189,000 in gold the public
revenue rose by a little over £1,000,000. It
was a great and wonderful rise—almost the
biggest in Australian history, and as a
million couldn’t come out of £189,000 the
only inference is that so many extra millions
were spent in looking for that comparatively
small sum that the Treasury managed to
pick up one of them in passing. The state
of things where the finding of £189,000 leads
to the expenditure of so much money that
the State can annex fjve times the total
visible cause of the riot is truly extraor-
dinary, and is much too glorious to last.
Either the cause must get a great deal larger
or the effect must get smaller. The Westra-
lian revenue can’t continue booming at its
present rate on so infinitesimal a basis- of
solid inducement, and unless its mineral in-
dustry looks up to a very remarkable extent,
something serious must happen.
* * * *
The Forrest Government has been run-
ning riot on the strength of this transient
prosperity. Within the last 12 months it
has committed itself, or partially committed
itself, to undertakings that will swallow
£6,000,000 or so of borrowed money. Hit
gets the money at 3 per cent, the additional
interest burden will be £lBO,OOO a year ;
and £189,000 extra extracted once out of the
province’s one big industry is a very slender
justification for a load of £lBO,OOO a year in
perpetuity. When there is hardly any
notable sign of improvement in any industry
save the precarious gold-mining business
alone, and even its improvement is nothing
very large or extraordinary, the future pro-
spects of the Westralian creditor look far
from brilliant. Yet, in the meantime,, and
despite the fact that the share-market is in
an advanced state of decomposition, and the
floating of new wild-cats is practically im-
possible, and the country is full of idle
batteries and exempted mines and liquida-
tions, the boom, so far as the State is con-
cerned, keeps up bravely. The increase of
revenue is spread over almost every depart-
ment. On the strength of the extra £189,000
of gold the railways earned £330,000 more
than in 1895, by carrying the men who
found the above amount, and the men
who didn’t find it, and by conveying
machinery to extract it, and provisions
and liquors and other things for the
people who were looking for it, or were
selling drinks and various goods to the people
who were looking for it, or were washing
their clothes, or otherwise living upon them.
And the Customs raised £380,000 more in
duties on the goods imported for the use of
the men who found that £189,000 and for
the people directly or indirectly dependent
on them. Still, though the outward pro-
sperity continues, the character of things is
changing. The excitement began as a gold-
boom. It went on as a coiiipany-floating
boom. It is now becoming largely a bor-
rowed money and public works boom, like
those of New South Wales and Victoria in
past years. It will probably last a year or
two longer in its latest capacity, and then if
something not yet apparent doesn’t come to
its assistance it must go under.
* * * *
It would be absurd, of course, to profess
that the Forrest Government was wholly
responsible for the present position of things.
No one can draw a sharp line anywhere and
say that here genuine prosperity ends and
artificial inflation begins. No one can say
exactly how far it is safe to reckon on the
boom being continuous, so as to adjust the
commitments of .the State to the permanent
condition of things as they will be when
chaos subsides and some semblance of
definiteness sets in. Any Government would
be liable to blunder at such a time. But
the Forrest Government seems to have
blundered on a Napoleonic scale, and as-
sumed, not merely that the boom would
leave some permanent prosperity behind it,
but that it was all permanent. And, as a
result, Westralia is likely to make a historic
burst by-and-bye. At present it is enjoying
nearly three times as much revenue in pro-
portion to population as any other of the
Australias, and this glory is too incredible to
last.
Some More “Horrors.”
The series of shriek articles published by
Sydney Telegraph on the Horrors of Protec-
tion as exemplified in Victoria ended in shedding
something of a blessing on that much-abused
system. This wasn’t the result which the
writer intended to convey, but it was the one
which he arrived at all the same. Among many
other things, he attacked the social aspect of
the question, and demonstrated that a high
birth and marriage rate was a reliable test of
prosperity, and also proved by figures that the
rates in both Vic. and N.S.W. were declining,
but that in the Freetrade province the decline
was much faster than in its neighbor. He
alleged that the quantity of food consumed was
another infallible index, and that the best-fed
man was the happiest and richest and wisest,
and then he showed by his own tables of
statistics that the Victorian was the better-fed
person of the two. And, having got these two
disastrous facts off his chest, the Telegraph’s
contributor seriously entered upon the hard-
cash aspect of his story. This aspect consisted
mainly of two assumptions; first, that the
amount paid in duties to the Government was
added by the importer on to the price charged
to his customers—the public—along with about
60 per cent, traders’ profit on top of the actual
outlay, so that every shilling of customs duty
levied under the nefarious system of Protection
raised prices about Is. 7d. to the consumer;
and, second, that, as a natural consequence,
prices were that much higher in a protected
than in a Foreign-trade community. Then he
dropped into figures in support of his conten-
tion, and wrecked himself horribly in one act.
* » * *
The batch of figures in support of the first
contention read as follows:
DIRECT COST OF CUSTOMS DUTIES.
(Except Duties on Intoxicants and Narcotics )
Government revenue ... ... pqoo non
Gross profits of trades thereon ... ..' 540’000
Increased price charged by protected manu- ’
facturers 2 600 000
Importers gross profit on one-half thereof *260*000
Retailers gross profit on two-thirds thereof ... 664,000
Total cost ... ... 964 000
direct cost of protection to Victoria, therefore* iq
£4,964,000 a year, and of this enormous sum only £9OO *OOO
is reaped by the Government, while over £4,000 000 goes
into the pockets of private people. oes
This alleges as clearly as it possibly can that
where the Government gets £900,000 in duties
the people pay £1,440,000 in extra prices of
imported goods, and because they pay this
£1,440,000 they also pay £3,524,000 i/extra
prices on locally-manufactured goods. And
having laid down this mass of numerals with a
grievous thud, the Telegraph’s genius pro-
ceeded with another table of figures to call liim-
self an Ananias of the most foolish description.
*** * .
The second outbreak is a list showing the
alleged prices of various articles of food liu
Sydney under the blessings of Foreign-trade
and in Melbourne under the especial curse of
Protection. Taking the food aspect only, and
leavincr out some facts about white and gold
china "which makes the table run to more space
than is available, the document is as follows
This list, as a means of proving how the
average toiler suffers under Protection, is fool-
ishly incomplete. It says nothing at all about
mutton, beef, pork, sugar, tea, coffee, eggs,
fruit, cabbages, turnips, carrots, salt, locally-
made jams, and various other articles, which
make up the biggest part of the average work-
man’s food-expenditure. (In another part of
the statement, however, it appears that the
standard price of bread sold over the counter is
the same in both cities, and that potatoes are a
trifle cheaper in Melbourne.) Therefore, it is a
reasonable assumption that a lot of the above
articles are as cheap, or cheaper, in the southern
city, and it was therefore considered advisable
to leave them out as calculated to spoil the
argument. This, however, is all a side issue.
* * * *
The soulful aspect, of the case is that the
Telegraph’s list utterly demolishes its own
figures, and proves its statement about the cost
of Protection to be a foolish, clumsy fabrication.
Said statement reads that the £900,000 paid in
duties is passed on to the people along with
=£540,000 traders’ profits thereon ; in other
words, that every shilling duty costs the people
about Is. 7d. in enhanced prices. If this story
is demolished, then the whole yarn drops
through a vast hole into chaos. According to
the published price-list, 3d. per lb. duty on
cocoa only raises the price one penny instead of
over 43d. Threepence duty on raisins raises
the price lid. instead of over 44d. The
same duty on currants only raises the price
Id., 2d. duty on groats only raises the price
Id., and a duty of 2d. on sheeps’ tongues
only raises the price 2d. not as
alleged. A threepenny duty on cheese
seems, in one instance, to make the price less,
and a duty of Is. on matches only raises the
cost to the consumer by 9d. The fact that
locally-made matches are much cheaper still is
judiciously smothered. A duty of 2d. on mon-
key soap seems to raise the price Jd., and 2d.
per lb. on starch only makes it dearer.
These and some others to the same effect are
the Horrors of Protection person’s own figures,
so they are doubtless as good on his own side of
the question as he could make them. The
interesting result is that, whereas he asserts
that the importer charges the duty on to the
consumer with 60 per cent, added, he
only ventures to distinctly allege, despite
his great capacity for alleging things,
three cases out of 40 where that is done
and in 16 cases the extra price isn’t even equal
to the duty. Therefore, by the Freetrade
writer’s own showing the 60 per cent, is a fic-
tion, and the =£4,900,000 odd which he builds
upon it as the cost of Protection to Victoria is
a vision,, and the “ disastrous effects of Pro-
tection,” in this branch at least, are a premedi-
tated nightmare.
* * * *
Ihe inference, so far as the price-list goes, is
that Protection as an incidental means of pro-
ducing revenue, is rather a cheaper method
than most others. When the State takes <£l
from a Victorian citizen by direct taxation,
there is no possible means of denying that he
pays that <£l. When instead of doing so it
takes ,£1 per medium of a protective tariff, it
would seem that in a number of important
instances the Victorian citizen only pays 16s.
or 155., or from that down to 55., the re-
mainder being paid in the shape of reduced


prices by the foreigner who sent the goods
along, out of his desire to keep the mar-
ket against possible competition created by
Protection. At all events, the list of prices
makes a lamentable hash of the garbled statis-
tics which show the Victorian as being robbed
of something like £5,000,000 a year by his fiscal
policy. That theory is all built on the story of
how the duty is passed on with 60 per cent,
added, and, the author of it having knocked the
bottom out of his own chimera, the structure
comes down—thud !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Whisper from Bull-Land.
Hugh Watt, the British syndicator and ex-
M.P. —who alleges that Premier Kingston
didn’t throw him out of his office with con-
tumely, whereas Kingston claims that he did—
is again heard of. Watt is trying to bring
pressure to bear on the British Government to
have the Northern Territory erected into a
separate province, and as he doubtless has
influential boodle at his back the scheme may
not be so hopeless as it seems. Probably
it is as hopeless as it seems, but at 16,000 miles
distance there is no absolute certainty. At all
events, however hopeless it may be now, there
is always the possibility that, if the circum-
stances of the country remain as at present, the
scheme may become possible at a future day
when some more influential boodle accumulates
behind it. The Northern Territory, as matters
now stand, is an anomaly and a perpetual
danger. It was part of New South Wales, at
the time when New South Wales included not
only the present province but also Victoria,
Queensland and all of the continent except
Westralia and S.A. The separation of Queens-
land cut it off from the remaining territory of
the mother province, and it was abandoned
under circumstances that are still so very hazy
that no one really knows whether it is British
or cornstalk property. At all events, the
British Government lent it casually to S.A.,
which governs it, and is also partly governed
by it as it returns members to S.A. Par-
liament, and which stands the loss on its
railway and telegraph system, and is* respon-
sible for the accumulated deficit. Yet the
British Government refuses to allow its for-
mal annexation to S.A. ; it has separate
accounts, and is partly an individual province
bossed by a Government Resident and partly
a bit of the political system which centres
at Adelaide. It is distinctly specified that the
Imperial authorities may resume possession of
it, and this possibility has to be kept con-
stantly in view. S.A., at all events, isn’t
strong enough by itself to prevent the resump-
tion of the Territory, though an Australian
Commonwealth probably could remonstrate
with sufficient vigor if such a thing was sug-
gested. Now that the Rand boom has appa-
rently reached its limit, and the Westrahan
boom seems to be on its last legs, the desire for
a new country to exploit is much felt by J.
Bull, and the uncertain tenure of the Ter-
ritory suggest it as a field for operations.
Booming nowadays is done on a magnificent
scale and wants a lot of elbow-room, and the
Territory is almost the only empty place left
where a new Rhodes might start a Napoleonic
career of finance. Watt, ex-M.P., is the per-
son who has pegged out the Territory as his
especial stamping-ground, and on the occasion
when he alleges that he was not ejected from
Kingston’s office he was practically endeavor-
ing to arrange for its cession to an English
syndicate. If the British Government could
be induced to erect it into a separate province
of 523,000 square miles, with a population of
1159 Europeans and 3626 Chinamen, and with
practically no revenue and only one place that
can with any show of reason be called a town,
the chances are that any enterprising syndicate
could buy up its hopeless little Legislature and
almost all its population, and get for a moderate
outlay the right to do almost anything for
practically as long as it liked. Then the Terri-
tory would probably become an alien province
of black labor and Chinamen and leprosy and
semi-military dictatorship, and a thorn in the
side of white Australia for many years to come.
Whether Watt, ex-M.P., amounts to anything
it is impossible to say—most likely he doesn’t;
but, if present conditions endure, he or some
other boomer will almost certainly amount to
something in this matter sooner of later. The
Territory is such a spacious terra incognita—
such a magnificent place for a syndicate to
boom and lie about and sell mining concessions
in, and to trade off in agricultural allotments,
and to float companies about for raising sugar
and pearls and Cain and other things with nigger
labor, that the need of a central authority to
take a firm grip of it in the name of all Aus-
tralia is obvious.
Some Vandemonian Politicians.
The Braddon-Fysh Government appeals this
month to the electors of Tasmania, and so far
as appears at present it will be returned to
office very peacefully by the uncomplaining
tax-payer. Politics make very little row and
fury in Vandemonia. The Braddon crowd
came unobtrusively into office in 1894 when
Tasmania had the biggest deficit in proportion
to its revenue in the seven provinces, and was
in a bad way generally ; and the Government
and the province have stumbled agreeably
downhill together ever since. Retrenchment
was the especial whoop of the Braddon
Ministry, and as a retrenchment Government it
has been at least a partial success. It sacked
many uninfluential public servants and reduced
the salaries of others; especially it cut down
the wages of the railway employes to about the
lowest possible figure. In addition to this it
adopted the wise and business-like course of in-
creasing the land-tax from to Id. in the £
on capital values. The tax, however, was not a
graduated one but fell on the large and small
owner alike ; it was a mere revenue device, and
did nothing at all to break up the huge estates
with which Tasmania is encumbered. Also,
the Braddon Government levied an income-
tax of Bd. in the £ on incomes derived from
personal exertion and Is. in the £ on in-
comes derived from property. flhis dis-
tinction between personal and property in
comes was almost the only Democratic
feature in its record, and the fact that it drove
the income-tax measure through Parliament
showed that the Braddon Ministry had a lot
of reserve strength. In other ways its financial
policy consisted in making what revenue it
could out of Tattersall’s sweeps, in doing
deals with land-grabbing syndicates, and most
of all in making the accounts square by paying
current expenditure out of loans. In this par-
ticular swindle the Braddon-Fysh Cabinet is
the very worst in proportion to its circum-
stances in Australasia ; fully one eighth of its
ordinary expenditure is paid out of loans and
passed along to posterity, while even New
Bouth Wales only cooks its accounts to the
extent of about one-fifteenth. Probably the
best thing to be said for the present Tas.
Ministry is that, though it is financially the
worst in Australia, the Tas. Ministry which
preceded it was worse still, and that is only
negative praise. The latest news is that Brad-
don, probably with a view to the elections, has
weakened miserably on the only good points in
his financial policy. He doesn’t propose to
graduate the land-tax so as to do something
serious to the handful of big estates which block
settlement in Tasmania, and there is no State
Bank or Credit Foncier or Land for Closer Set-
tlement scheme, or other beastly revolutionary
device, in his political programme. But he
appeals to the electors of the Land of Lots of
Sleep with the assurance that :
Ministers regard it as incumbent upon them to favor a
reduction in the rates levied from Is. to 9d. in the £1 on
realised wealth, and from Bd. to 6d. in the £1 on personal
exertion, and to increase the exemption for the relief of
recipients of small fixed salaries. . . . The Govern-
ment proposes to realise a smaller revenue from the land,
both directly by a land-tax, and indirectly by an income-
tax.
Tasmania is still practically insolvent. The
public revenue is really .£60,000, or so, per
annum behind the expenditure, and it is only
by charging .£llO,OOO of the regular expendi-
ture to loans that any semblance of prosperity
can be kept up. There is an accumulated short-
age of about .£BOO,OOO, though half of it is
partially kept out of sight by judicious book-
keeping—a shortage equal to more than a year’s
revenue. The country wants its big estates
broken up so as to make room for population-
through want of population and goods to carry
the Tasmanian railways do little more than
paying working expenses and engine-grease. It
wants new roads, new industries, a new Upper
House instead of the present property-qualified
institution, and, above all, it wants to learn to
own itself and its own soul instead of being
owned and bossed by Melbourne syndicates.
And the Ministry goes to the country at this
juncture with a great scheme for letting all the
old evils remain as they are, and for reducing
taxation so that the already insolvent province
may be a little more insolvent still. And yet
Braddon is a comparatively active and pro-
gressive man, as active and progressive men go
in slow old Vandemonia.
That Blanket Contract.
The principal Freetrade organ in Sydney still
labors like a raft in a heavy sea to demonstrate
that the famous blanket-contract of the Reid
Government is not an infringement of its fiscal
faith. It is a difficult piece of work certainly,
but the organ toils at it incessantly. Its latest
effort was a leading article published on January
5, in which it wrestled with the matter as
follows:
The mere fact that the local manufacture of the articles
required has been made a condition of the proposed con-
tract is enough to cause those Protectionists who do not
look below the surface of the matter to hail the innova-
tion as a departure from the policy of Freetrade. That it
is nothing of the kind a moment’s candid examination
should be sufficient to show. The essential purpose of
Protection is to bring into existence local industries, as
the acceptance of such a tender as the Government invites
would also do, hut it is in the means by which this would
be accomplished that the difference lies. The Govern-
ment do not propose offering any subsidy whatever out of
the taxpayers’ pockets in order that the blanket-manu-
facturing industry may be developed. What their an-
nouncement conveys is that if local makers can guarantee
the supply of an article on terms as advantageous as those
offered by any others, they will be given the preference.
It is simply intended as a test which will prove whether
it is possible that Government requirements in a par-
ticular branch of supply can be as well satisfied here as
they can be abroad. In respect to blankets : if there is
anyone in New South Wales able to make them as good
and cheap as those imported, he can have an order which
should keep him going for a long time. The protectionist
principle is not introduced in such a proposal in the
smallest degree. That principle looks to the prohibition
of imports in order that manufacturers may have the
market to themselves, and, for a time at least, receive
subsidies from the public in the form of increased prices,
which they can command through the absence of com-
petition. What the Government offers is to guarantee a
certain demand to manufacturers who can compete with
all others. The Government, as a Freetrade Administra-
tion, are under the necessity of assuring themselves,
before any tender is accepted, that the tenderer is pre-
pared to give as good value as any other supplier who
might be contracted with.
All this is so clear that even if it were put
inside a barrel of tar and buried underneath the
sunken city of Atlantis it couldn’t be more
beautifully distinct. Still, it leaves the ends
of a good many loose improbabilities hanging
out. It is not much use to say that the announce-
ment that all foreign makers are shut out
merely “ conveys that if local makers can supply
an article on terms as advantageous as any
others ” they will get the contract; in any case,
that has been so all along, and everybody knew
it, and it didn’t need to be “ conveyed ” at all.
The idea of testing whether the State’s “re-
quirements can be as well satisfied here as
abroad ” by shutting out tenders from abroad
is an elaborately upside-down idea; according
to this narrative it is for the express purpose of
comparing the N.S.W. and foreign tenders on
absolutely equal terms that the foreign tenders
are banned altogether so as to prevent any
comparison. The Government, it is alleged,
does not “propose offering any subsidy out of
the taxpayers’ pockets” by paying a higher
price for local blankets than for im-
ported, and in proof of this it is stipulated
that the blankets in question must be locally
made ; the importer isn’t even allowed to put
his prices forward. The object of keeping the
foreign maker’s prices out of sight altogether is,
it appears, to make it quite clear to everybody
that his prices are not higher than the local
man’s, and that there is consequently no subsidy
to local industry, and no Protection. Further,
“ the Government, as a Freetrade Administra-
tion, are under the necessity of assuring them-
selves, before any tender is accepted, that the
tenderer is prepared to give as good value as
any other supplier who might be contracted
with,” and the simple and natural way to do
this is, of course, to entirely exclude the other
supplier, so that, even if he is prepared to give
better value, the fact can’t be made known.
The attempts to prove that the Reid Govern-
ment’s Protection isn’t Protection at all, but is
really a specially advanced variety of Freetrade,
are a soulful evidence of the advantages to be
derived by carrying a large hunk of logic uphill
and then letting it roll down again.

Only Sixpence.
Dear Bulletin, —Melbourne sentiment is
somewhat divided as to the brutal injustice or
otherwise of a Prahran (Vic.) magistrate who
gave a middle-aged woman seven days for the
theft of sixpence, but the tendency of the press
is to shriek severely and unanimously at the
Prahran beak. “ Nothing w T as previously
known against the prisoner,” and her sentence
at a casual glance seems very hard on a first
offender. I want to figure the matter out
quietly and without hysterics. The middle-
aged woman was not in pressing need of six-
pence. “She appeared to be slightly under
the influence of liquor when arrested” (which
suggests a personal expenditure on beer before
doing the deed), and by her own statement,
when pleading guilty, she went into a dairy-
man’s shop “ to make a purchase, and seeing
the money on the shelf was tempted to take
it.” The dairyman, witnessing the larceny and
being possibly as much in need of sixpence
as his customer was, offered to let her go
free if she handed over the loot, but she
denied the offence and maintained a death-
grip on the coin until he gave her into
custody. In short, she was a full - sized
thief, who had yielded to the weak temptation
of two threepenny-bits. A ,£lOO note or a
diamond ring on the dairyman’s shelf would
probably have gone where the sixpence went—-
into her pocket—and then she would have been
reckoned rightly punished with six months’ im-
prisonment at the least. She took all she could
see, and the fact that she took it on very
slight provocation—inasmuch as she saw only
sixpence, unless the beer doubled her
powers of vision—was the best possible proof
of her natural and full - blown dishonesty.
No woman could steal more coin than all the
coin within reach, therefore she was as large and
comprehensive a thief as she knew how. The
questions at issue are : Should punishment go
strictly and invariably according to the amount
stolen ? If the theft of, say, .£1 would carry a
sentence of 28 days, ought there to be 75 per-
cent. discount because the prisoner didn’t get
the chance to steal the whole pound, but took
all that was going ? Also, if, as is commonly
and rightly held to be the case, dire necessity
is an excuse for theft, isn’t the absence of any
necessity an aggravation of it ?. In short, are
there not cases where the theft of sixpence
might be worth even 14 days, to say nothing of
seven. In this case, I am with the Prahran
beak.—F.
A Very Small Matter.
The latest Australian shipping strike, which
raged for one consecutive day and then pro-
ceeded to taper off, was mainly about a matter
of somebody’s small dignity. Most of the
really important questions were in a fair way
for settlement before the strike began. A few
years ago, when the shipping business was in a
bad way, the wages of the marine engineers
were heavily reduced; now the boom in
Westralia has brought sudden prosperity to the
trade, but until the engineers kicked hard
there was no sign of their pay being restored to
the original figure. The shipping companies
were willing that their employes should share
their bad fortunes, but sharing their good ones
was quite a different matter. In addition to
the wages difficulty there were sundry
other matters of dispute, but when the
engineers, after some fruitless negotiation,
gave notice of their intention to start the
strike w'ithin 24 hours, the companies gave up
the most important points at issue and offered
to confer about the others. But they were
willing to do this on one condition only : that
the engineers withdrew their notice to quit,
and it became understood that there was no
compulsion in the matter, and that the com-
panies were simply conferring a boon in a lordly
and condescending fashion of their own, and
that the employes, w-ho hadn’t dared to ask for
it, w r ere receiving it with intense surprise and
gratitude, or something to that effect. The
trouble which led to the laying-up of 15 vessels
in Melbourne and to a serious dislocation of
trade, really came about because the marine
engineers declined to say that they hadn’t
given notice when every one knew that they
had, or to allege that they were receiving their
concessions simply because of the noble char-
acter of the shipowner, when everybody knew
that they were not. The respectable press
lamented a good deal over the small cussed-
ness of the men who were prepared to make
so large a trouble over so small a matter, but
there was a unanimous lack of any reference to
the baby dignity of the shipowners, who in-
vented and insisted upon the absurd condition.
The trouble presently simmered down, and the
question of dignity was unobtrusively buried
somewhere out of sight. It was, as the respect-
able press truly remarked, a very small matter,
and it would have been an equally small matter
if the companies, after conceding the more im-
portant points at dispute, had insisted on the
engineers turning three somersaults apiece in
Collins-street as an essential condition of peace,
or getting their hair cut sideways, or any other
grotesque trifle. Being a small matter, and a
very foolish one also, no one seems to know
exactly what became of it or where its grave is
located.
B. R. Wise, of N.S.W., was the first Federal
Convention candidate to issue an address to the
electors. The two main features of his pro-
gramme are that there must be no fiscal or
other local party politics in the Convention,
and that “ the delegates should enter the Con-
vention, not as hucksterers who are seeking,
each of them, to make the best bargain for his
own province, but as Australians.” In other
words they should cease drawing up elaborate
schemes to prevent each other’s province from
getting ahead and accept things as they
are. r J here is no end to the ways in which
each State can show that it will be robbed of
°i d - by the pooling of the various debts and
rates of interest and more or less profitable
railway and postal systems, and to the con-
struction of bottomless devices for portioning
out the surplus so that one province won’t be
benefited to the extent of only 2£d. while its
neighbor revels in 3Jd. It should be a good
omen that the electoral address of the first
Con vention delegate calls on the people of Aus-
tralia to abjure the penny-farthing aspect of
things, and think more about making a nation
than about the possible dropping of a copper
in the process. Provided B. R. Wise’s appeal
to the provinces not to huckster does not mean
simply that the other provinces are not to
huckster but to let Wise’s province have its
own way in everything, then the ex-Attorney-
General has struck the right key-note.
4.
A large case of false-swearing by half-a-dozen
policemen is just now agitating the public mind
of Maoriland. A well-known Auckland share-
broker picked up a lost child in the street, and,
taking it to a policeman, asked him to convey it
to the lock-up. This Robert refused to do,
whereat the other said he would take the child
to the station, and, at the same time, report the
“copper’s ” conduct. This threat incensed the
man in blue, for he also repaired to the watch-
house by a shorter cut, and, when the share-
broker arrived with the youngster, promptly
preferred a charge of drunk and disorderly
against him. The broker wanted to lay his
complaint, but was hustled from one policeman
to another, kicked down several stone steps, re-
fused an audience by the inspector, half-stripped
of his clothes, and generally rolled about, and was
finally locked in a cell. In between times the
inspector put his head in at the cell window, and
chaffed his prisoner, and made himself disagree-
able in his own small way. At the court, next
day, half-a-dozen police officials swore that the
prisoner was drunk, &c. One officer of a dis-
criminating turn of mind explained that the
culprit was “ mad drunk with whisky.” Against
this, an overwhelming mass of evidence proved
that the sharebroker was absolutely sober, and
he was at once discharged, and five of the
policemen were suspended from duty pending
a departmental inquiry. The inquiry re-
sulted in one or two being sacked and two
being “ invited to resign.” Strangely enough,
however, no prosecution has been heard
of so far, therefore people want to know if the
big man in blue is privileged to commit perjury
and conspiracy without suffering the same
consequences as other people. The policeman
is by habit and repute a hard swearer, and his
perjury is difficult to find out because the
average cop corroborates his brother cop under
all circumstances. If nothing particular is to
happen to him when he is found out then it
seems that a constable may be defined as a man
who is dressed up in a uniform and turned
loose in the streets with power to swear any-
thing with impunity.
Sydney Telegraph in its trade review pub-
lished Ist January, ’97 :
The great event of the year has been the removal of
duties, and at the time of the alteration in the tariff
opinions as to the future course of values were very
diverse. During the interval prices have so far recovered
that consumers have to now pay as much as and even
more than they did when an additional 10 per cent, was
levied by the Customs-house, which means that if the
duties current a year ago had remained intact, prices
would be fully 10 per cent, higher than they are at
present. This has been advantageous to importers,
manufacturers and consumers alike.
From the above, two inferences stick out con-
spicuously : (1) Prices have gone up under Free-
trade, whereas it was promised they would go
down ; (2) when prices go up it is “ advantage-
ous to importers, manufacturers, and consumers
alike,” yet the Telegraph has been publishing
a long series of articles in which it is alleged
that prices have gone up in Victoria because of
Protection, and describing the rise as fraud,
robbery, &c. Therefore, it seems that a rise of
prices under Foreign-trade is advantageous and
proves that Foreign-trade is right, while a rise
of prices under Protection is horrible and proves
that Protection is utterly wrong. How did the
Telegraph thunder-blast the Dibbs Ministry
on the alleged ground that its system sent
prices up and heaped a burden on the back of
the poor ! Why wasn’t it mentioned then that
advanced prices were “ advantageous ” all
round ?
The Melb. fly-flat stamp-dealer, who lately
started to swindle the Post-office department
by removing ink stains from receipt-stamps, can
thank the scandalous carelessness of the said
department for his present troubles. The pos-
sibility of working his simple little fraud—
which, by the way, was reported at head-
quarters by the Philatelic Society in the first
instance—should have been prevented some
time ago, long before the brilliant dealer had
found out the secret of converting second-hand
stamps into new. The Postal department
admits, quite casually, that it used to put
the old telegraph forms into a chemical
solution, for the purpose of separating
the cancelled stamps from the forms.
Which treatment was “ found to obliterate
the cancel marks on the stamps, and it was seen
to be unsafe to continue a system under which
the old stamps might be exploited in the man-
ner of the Webb case. The department there-
upon decided to burn the old messages.” But
the issue of fast-color stamps went on as before,
notwithstanding this suggestive revelation.
In point of fact, the present fraud was invited
by departmental methods, and it seems more
than likely that one of the postal officers put
the culprit up to it.
About the latest news from London con-
cerns a lecture on Australian Federation delivered
at the Imperial Institute by “ Major John C.
Neild, Commissioner on Charities for New South
Wales.” That is the tremendous blast of designa-
tion on the ticket of admission. Apparently
Neild is breaking his numerous titles and billets
gently to the British nation. If he came out with
the shattering information all in one lump the
nation would probably faint, and then before
water could be poured over it and the door-key
put down its back, some foreign Power might in-
vade the country and overturn the Constitution.
