Oscar Wilde’s drama, “A Woman of No
Importance,” staged at Sydney Criterion on
Saturday, has got the brand of failure written
large upon it. It is a ladylike failure of a very
able description, enshrined in a good deal of nice
furniture and some beautiful hangings, and a
great amount of expensive clothing cut low. It
has the same aspect of glittering worldly weari-
ness which characterises Wilde’s plays in general,
but the weariness is more tired and less brilliant
than usual. The first incident worth calling such
comes in at the end of the third act; until then
the production consists mostly of four ladies
sitting in chairs talking about morals. They talk
of very little else than morals, not as women who
haven’t any, but rather as people who are too
weary and too much jewelled and too bare
about the shoulders to care whether they have
any or not. The plot is only a streak alto-
gether. In the first act, Titheradge, a British
lord, whose conversation is one labored pur-
suit of the weary epigram, engages a nice local
youth (Edward Ferris) as his secretary, and the
four ladies converse a lot about morals. In Act
11. he discovers that the new secretary is his
illegitimate son, and the mother comes in and
listens, with an haggard eye, to the awful conver-
sation about morals. About the end of Act 111.
the new secretary (after a good deal of conversa-
tion about women and morals) finds the tired lord
trying to kiss his best girl—the only one in the
crowd who doesn’t talk needlessly about morals ;
and is only prevented from slaying him on the spot
by a shrieking woman, who tells him that the lord
is his own father. Then the curtain goes down on a
disastrous heap of morals, and in Act IY. the mother
refuses to marry the lord, and the son decides to
marry the girl who doesn’t talk about morals, and
the four ladies with the jewellery and the tired
conversation fade out of the story.
• * * »
There is very little incident, but a number
of people are told at considerable length about
various incidents which happened 20 years pre-
viously. A dozen or two of the epigrams are
brilliant, and the other 357 are merely rather
ordinary statements turned upside down and kept
that way with difficulty by a rope tied to their
feet. Hardly anybody makes one plain, ordinary
remark all through ; all the leading characters
are struggling desperately all the time to lift the
heavy epigram out of its bed and hoist it on top
of the conversation, and the result suggests brain-
fag and hard conflict. The author’s stock of in-
cident apparently gave out very early in his dra-
matic career. Now his best idea is to get two men
into a room and let them talk about women in
labored brilliances that have the footprints of the
gas-meter upon them to show how they were
struggled with in the small hours of the morning.
“ A Woman of No Importance ” is a clever play
of a kind ; it makes the auditor feel weary with an
expensive kind of weariness as if he had slept all
night in his dress-suit, and was a duke with
.£lOO,OOO a year, and hadn’t been properly in bed
for three weeks, and never expected to take any
interest again in anything as long as he lived.
Titheradge and Mrs. Brough do most of the dead
ljft in this play, and do it exceedingly well, and
Brough and Misses Hardy and Temple fill in some
smaller parts quite satisfactorily. Of the three
pew people, Mrs. Bracy is excellent in an old
woman part; Miss Florence Seymour is not excel-
lent so far, and Edward Ferris, who presents a
half resemblance to Charles Arnold, looks as if he
might be very good when he gets on to something
more congenial. At present there is compara-
tively little to say about him, except that he has
a tendency to look like Charles Arnold.
** * *
“ Tommy Atkins,” at Sydney Royal, goes
In its third week with as healthy a roar and bang
as on the opening night, and if it isn’t good for
several weeks longer it certainly deserves to be.
There are many things to admire in “Atkins,”
but what this paper most admires is the bad cap-
tain with three wives—his light-heartedness and
bis excessively beautiful clothes, and the way his
conscience doesn’t trouble him and the spectre of
his victims never worries him in the night. Pro-
bably the most attractive feature in the captain’s
character comes out when his second unmarried
Wife decides to drown herself and her infant, and
so remove herself comfortably out of his road.
Whereupon the captain considers the river
thoughtfully, and notes with his perfectly
honest smile that it is a very nice river,
pool and shady and picturesque and ferny,
and about the pleasantest river imaginable
for any man’s second wife to be,found in without
any trouble. Then he considers his second wife
with aq artistic eye, and evidently decides that
she will look a very nice girl to be found in just
such a river, so like a true gentleman he steps into
the woods that the lady may drown herself with-
out being embarrassed by his presence. There is
so much artistic feeling about Diver as he looks
round for a suitable place for his second wife to
drown herself in, so that her hair may harmonise
with the ferns on the shore when she is fished out
about the middle of next week, that the sinful
captain in “Tommy Atkins” deserves to be re-
membered as something above the average of his
kind. The ordinary dramatic captain doesn’t care
twopence whether his second wife drowns herself
in a nice spot or not; this one, as everybody feels,
would sooner shove her in at a nice place than let
her fall in naturally where the surroundings were
repugnant to his feelings as a gentleman.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) Crackles still
has another hat continually. Latterly, however,
his glory has paled somewhat before that of Law-
ton, who gets someone to throw dead rodents at
him from the wings, and grabs them eagerly as
they arrive, remarking “I’ve got another rat.’
The public hardly knows whether to pin its faith
most to the man who has another hat or the man
who has another rat, and, so far, these two intense
jokes constitute most of the verbal mirth of the
panto. The Lucifers, however, who never fade
and never get another hat, are a vast success and a
joy for ever, and when the male Lucifer kicks ten
feet high with both legs at once, and shoulders
arms with his leg for a musket, the public enthu-
siasm rises to fever heat, and even if Crackles got
other hats all at once he would pass unnoticed
at that moment of supreme contortion. The Bul-
letin, by the way, received a kind invitation from
the management to inspect Mr. Lucifer privately,
but it didn’t attend. It likes that gentleman
much in his airy moments on the stage,_ but the
undressed athlete in a serious frame of mind hav-
ing his chest-measurement taken with a tape
spoils the effect. The second edition of “ Sinbad”
is due on Saturday.
** * *
Charles M‘Carthy’s robust brogue is to
make its second essay at Sydney Lyceum on
Saturday in “ True Irish Hearts.” This produc-
tion is described as “ the great musical comedy-
drama,” but, as The Bulletin never heard of it
before, and the author isn’t mentioned, and nothing
is said about it having run even one half-night at
Jamberoo, let alone having made a furore in all
the leading cities of the world, this paper indulges
in no prognostications, and approaches the subject
in a perfectly unbiassed spirit.
** * *
. The Palace is mostly in a condition of
standing-room only, and not much of that. The
co. is decidedly stronger than it was at the open-
ing, Wilton and Stack, the Delevines, Knowles,
and Miss Antonio, the lady who makes music
upside - down, being a powerful combination.
Sydney Tivoli likewise keeps the wonted boom
going, and the very mild business doing at some
of the big theatres is calculated, by comparison, to
make Brutus and Hamlet and the Silver King
wish they had learned to support themselves by
their teeth from a nail rather earlier in life.
#* * *
No less than three cinematographes are
now pervading Sydney. Jimmy MacMahon’s
was the first in the field, and hangs out at its old
quarters opposite the Lyceum, where a new series
of pictures has been turned on. The “only
genuine original ” cin., at 478 George-street, has
also turned on a fresh series, and a third arrival,
“the latest and most improved” ’graphe, has
lately opened at 420 George-street. When this
paper has got rested a little, and its corn isn’t
quite so bad, it contemplates making another
serious examination of all three, and hearing each
manager’s unbiassed opinion of the other two at
full length.
** * *
‘ ‘ Matsa, ” at Melb. Princess’s, continues
to achieve eight performances per week without
betraying any signs of exhaustion. In point of
fact, the show went stronger than ever on Satur-
day night, when a reconstructed third act was
gratefully received. The change is a pretty vast
improvement on the original third act, inasmuch
as the new scene introduces more legs and dazzle,
also it provides a semblance of natural end to the
story, which used to finish like a nightmare in the
sky. Under present arrangements, the principals
have a chance to explain themselves before a
Khedive, or somebody. Their explanation is
feeble, and the interpolated potentate seems right
out of his element ; nevertheless George Lauri,
Florence Young and the rest deserve some credit
for trying to clear up the awful mystery of their
previous careers. Little Gulliver looms funnier
than before, Ivy Scott and Carrie Moore sing a
brighter duet, the boxing cats display more ability
at landing one another on the jaw, and the flying
angels are now well-nigh perfect in the poetry of
motion. The supers., disguised as wild beasts,
make a dumb appeal for further eulogy, likewise
the toothsome lady representatives of various
newspapers may be cordially referred to. Seeing
that one of them impersonates The Bulletin,
and does particular credit to her billet, the pre-
sent writer would like to know the state of her
feelings as regards the staff. This matter has been
agitating p.w.’s mind strangely, ever since Boxing
Night.
** » *
At the Bijou, there is a steady demand for
Frank Thornton’s production of the red-haired
she-male that went astray. “ Miss Brown,” roam-
ing at large among the lady boarders arid dodging
the swarthy embraces of the vision from Demerara,
is a humorous subject with shocking possibilities
in it. A comedy which offers plenty of scope for
the imagination, and sets people wondering
whether the seminary takes a warm bath on
Friday evenings subsequently to fixing up its hair
in plaits, was bound to succeed. It rivets the at-
tention of the audiences all round from the moment
when they learn that each young lady has a bed-
room to herself and that all of them are addicted
to a hopeless passion for the music-master, except-
ing pretty Miss Seabrooke, the boarder who ran
away to get married. In her heart the ghastly
piano-thumper reigns no more, but her maiden
companions are understood to be still dreaming
about the scarecrow when the curtain falls.
“ For England ” is doing the same as usual,
or even better, at the Royal. Two or three out-
side reasons for the rush to Bland Holt’s plucky
spectacle are mentioned. In some quarters it is
confidently expected to be a pantomime, whilst a
jingo five per cent, of the crowded congregation
takes the show to be a Heed that Won the Hem-
pire. “For England” gives full satisfaction to
all parties. The fox-hunt is more exciting than a
panto., and the bogus destruction of the Boers by
Walter Baker is just as pleasing to himpenal
sentiment as though it were founded on fact. For
the great mass of visitors the scenery, Zulu
Marks, and the hanging in store for Albert Nor-
man are three great sources of joy, and they go
home loaded with value for their money.
** * *
Her Majesty’s—otherwise the poor old
Aleck—was possibly struggling on when this
journal went to press. “Fun on the Bristol”
came to a bitter conclusion early last week, yet
the house resumed business on Saturday with
“The Temptations of a Nurse Girl.” The new
drama (suspected of having sprung from Ballarat)
did no justice to its title. The leading horror was
a comic Idiot, who hopped around the stage
stating that he knew what was going on, and the
duty, seemingly, of all the other men, who didn’t
know what was going on, was to let the aforesaid
Idiot knock them down. If they ventured to rise
they were knocked down some more. The Bul-
letin is not quite sure whether the Idiot stoushed
the Nurse Girl likewise, or merely rescued her
from temptation. Whatever may have happened
eventually,, the fiend with softening of the brain
was certain to boss the incident, but after waiting
a long time for the Temptations, the press with-
drew. Up to 9.45 p.m. there was no temptation
of any kind, save a bloodthirsty desire to throw a
brick at the company. Human endurance has its
limit.
** * *
John Coleman, Alice Leamar, Edgar Gran-
ville and the rest of them are upholding the
standard of Rickards at the Opera House, and
that reliable establishment looks prosperous. The
other night, on the occasion of the elements break-
ing loose, some rain came through Rickards’ roof,
but the stalls opened their umbrellas and stuck to
their seats ; which speaks well for the exhilarating
quality of the entertainment. The roof is now
watertight, and brollies may be left in the cloak-
room with impunity.
** * *
The show given by the Cat King, at Melb.
Princess’s is said to be much more surprising than
it appears to anyone who doesn’t know the facts
of the case, which facts, unfortunately, are not
suitable for bold advertisement. Seven of Clark’s
“ star ” pussies were stolen from his stock on the
steamer ; at any rate they were missing when the
boat reached Melbourne. The cats now playing
leading business are “understudies,” and the
trainer’s patient instruction is rapidly bringing
them up to the artistic level of their lost com-
E anions, Besides the seven cats, Clark found
imself minus a whole flock of trick canaries, also
he lost a couple of professional fighting cocks
under melancholy circumstances. The birds were
suffering from parasites in the feathers, and an
attempt at cold water cure by turning the ship’s
hose on them either washed them overboard or set
up congestion of the lungs aggravated by pleurisy,
or something equally fatal. After a pathetic
struggle for existence, the roosters were buried at
sea.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The people who have hopes about a speedy rise
in the values of Australian city property invited to
seriously consider these facts : The annual value of all
Sydney and its suburbs, according to the municipal
assessment, which bears a tolerably steady relationship to
the actual rental, fell from six millions in ’92 to about
five millions in ’96. The assessed value of Melbourne fell
from nearly seven millions to about five. These are re-
garded as abnormally low values, the unnatural result of
the bursting of the boom. Yet the assessed annual
value of all Glasgow is only millions ; of Manchester
and Salford about 3f millions. Both are larger than either
Sydney or Melbourne ; Glasgow especially is much larger.
They haven’t had any exploded hoom of recent years ;
they aren’t specially depressed ; in fact they are both
practically in their normal condition. Also they have
more industries and more visible means of support than
either Sydney or Melbourne. The value of money in this
country is steadily tending down to British rates,
and prices and wages are getting there also. There-
fore there seems every probability that rents, and
consequently the value of properties, will do likewise.
The inference is that the old figures were merely
boom valuations, brought about by the influx of bor-
rowed money ; that the present values, on the whole
are only the natural values, or it may be that they are
still somewhat above the natural values ; and that there
will be no rise (beyond what is caused by increase of
population) worth speaking of for years, unless it be
brought about by another borrowing frenzy, with a
worse smash and a greater depression to follow.'
The old inflated values were made up partly of
the price of land when it was raised to an absurd figure
by insane speculation ; partly of the price of bricks when
they were worth, say, twice as much as they are now ;
partly of the price of other materials when they were also
from 50 to 100 per cent, dearer than at present; partly of
the cost of labor when wages were vastly higher ; partly
of the interest on the money borrowed on mort-
gage when money was worth 10 per cent, instead
of five or six These things were all at boom valuations,
and out of them were built two great boom cities But
considering the circumstances of the case, there seems
very little probability of the old conditions coinim- back
again._ The chances are that affairs will simply "settle
down in something like their present state, and that the
existing order of things isn’t a depression but merely a
gradual finding of the natural level. At all events? when
it is remembered that even the present assessed values of
Sydney and Melbourne are greater than those of the
morfc populous city of Glasgow, and are verv far
ahead of every other British city save London ft is
difficult to feel quite sure that these values are ’abnor
nially low and that a rise must ensue shortly.

* * * *
New South Wales has increased its public debt
by £9,300,000 in the last five years. The Victorian debt
in the same period has increased by only £4,000,000. The
5U million difference fully accounts for the depression
being- more severe in one province than in the other. But,
if both were so lamentably depressed despite thisinflux of
borrowed money, where would they have been if there had
been no borrowing ? What an awful recovery these pro-
vinces will have to suffer when the swearing-off does
become inevitable 1 And the longer it is postponed the
worse the recovery will be.
* * * *
Victoria has at last got its factory-returns for
1895 into print. The figures show a big advance on last
year’s. The returns for five years are : 1891, 3320 fac-
tories, 53,525 employes ; 1892, 3055 factories, 43,921 em-
ployes ; 1893, the bottom year in Victorian industries,
2737 factories, 39,815 employes; 1894, 2675 factories,
41 273 employes ; 1895, 2804 factories, 52,095 employes.
This is an increase of 129 establishments and 10,822 em-
ploves in one year—the biggest single year’s increase on
the Vic. record, and one which makes up most of the lee-
way since the boom.
• * * *
A report of the last six years’ railway operations
in N.S.W., Queensland, S.A. and Vic. furnishes some mate-
rial for hard-thinking. Since 1890, New South Wales has
increased its railway system by 349 miles, Victoria by 471
miles, S.A. by 85 miles, Queensland by 194. The increase
of 349 miles in New South Wales has increased the total
capital expended on the railways by £5,083,577. The in-
crease of 471 miles in Victoria has increased its capital
expenditure by only £1,766,525. South Australia’s in-
crease of 85 miles has added £1,184,604 to the capital
invested, and Queensland’s addition of 194 miles seems to
have cost £1,341,039—at least, these are the respective
increases in the capital charged to the railways in each
province. There certainly seems to be an awful dis-
crepancy in the figures somewhere, seeing that 349 miles
of new line in N.S.W. have apparently added nearly three
times as much to the liabilities as 471 miles in Victoria,
and S.A. appears to have got through nearly as much
money for 85 miles as Queensland did for 194, but, at all
events, the published facts of the case are as above given.
Three of the four railway systems show an in-
creased loss. The Victorian loss was £251,000 more in ’96
than in ’9O. The New South Wales loss increased by
£68,000. The S.A. lines showed a profit of £175,000 over
working expenses and interest on cost of construction in
’9O, and a loss of £64,000 in ’96. The only improvement
is in Queensland, where the ’96 deficit, though it reached
the stupendous sum of £348,000, was still £102,000 less
than in ’9O. The four railway systems put together had
£450,000 more shortage in ’96 than they had six years
previously, which is about as depressing a state of things
as any pessimist could ask for.

Still another German fire insurance co., the
Anchor and Munich, has started business in Melbourne.
This makes the 50th foreign office, or thereabouts. When
is some consistent Protectionist going to move for Pro-
tection'for the Australian fire co. ?
* * » *
Dalgety and Co. (Limited), an institution which
seems to keep well above water even in the worst of
times, shows a net profit of £83,672 for the year ended
30th June, ’96. This is just about £220 less than last
year’s profit, and considering that the co, has first to pro-
vide interest on 12,800,000 worth of debentures it
is a very good result. Still, considering that it is
trading on only £1,143,000 of its own money and on
£2,800,000 of borrowed funds its position might he a
great deal better than it is; Dalgety and Co. is by no
means so top-heavy as some concerns in the same line of
business, but it would be a lot more secure if the
proportion of debenture money wasn’t so heavy. A
dividend of 8 per cent, absorbs £BO,OOO, which leaves
£3672 to the good, and if this rate of progress is main-
tained for 760 years the big institution will he able to pay
off its debentures.and trade solely with its own funds.
Then if something did happen to depreciate the assets 33
per cent, all at once it wouldn’t necessarily be insolvent.

The Mercantile Mutual Fire Insurance Co.
(Sydney) announces a profit for the past year of £12,137, a
handsome increase compared with £8265 forlastyear, and
£11,571 the year before. Also, there is a balance of £3BOl
from last year, making the total amount available £15,938.
A bonus of 20 per cent, divided between shareholders and
policy-holders absqrbs 18210, and £7728 is carried for-
ward ; so the co. ends the year with nearly £4OOO more
assets than it had at the beginning. The reserve fund
remains at £40,000 as formerly.
* * * *
The City Mutual Fire Insurance Co. (Sydney)
has a net profit of £3958 as the results of a year’s work,
against £5695 last year and £3320 in 1894. This is a con-
siderable falling-off ; still the results are good enough for
all practical purposes. Out of the profits and the £1478
brought forward from last year shareholders get a 10 per
cent, dividend (£1500); a 10 per cent, bonus to policy-
holders absorbs £1563 ; 1 1500 is added to reserve, making
it £13,500 ; and £873 is carried forward.
* » * *
The National Bank (Melbourne) announces that
it will pay off another instalment of its reconstructed 4J4
percent, deposits on 22nd April, making rather more
than half the entire deposits released. The balance is to
be released by instalments during the ensuing 18 months,
so by September, ’9B, the National will have definitely
gone out of the reconstruction business. The Bank of
1 ictoria also announces the repayment of another instal-
ment in April, and expects to pay off in full before the
end of 1898.
* * * *
The Victorians have just discovered, among the
other foolishnesses of their postal management, that the
designs and colors of their stamps are so much more per-
manent than the ink used for cancellation purposes that
it is quite possible to remove the cancellation marks with
salts of lemon or some other simple device. One indi-
vidual, who is alleged to have done a large trade in this
way, has just been captured. He says, among other
things, that the process is perfectly simple, and that he
believes several other people are at the same game ; also,
that he sold some of his cleaned stamps to the postal
authorities themselves without detection. He tried his
solution on the stamps of other provinces, but every-
wdiere except in Victoria it appears that the postal
officials have sufficient intelligence to print their stamps
in colors that won’t stand the cleaning process. Con-
sidering that it is only a few years since Victoria suddenly

* * * *
The recent seizure of “ renovated ” postage-
stamps, in Melbourne, has, it is rumored, led to the dis-
covery that, certain lawyers’ clerks have pocketed
sums bujdng the faked articles at 50 per cent, discount
for legal purposes. Considering that stamps up to £lOO
are frequently required in land conveyances, and for over
£lOOO for probate duties, there were evidently very large
possibilities about the business and room for a fine large
breakage of revenue.
* * * *
A Richmond (Vic.) man, last week, received
froin a boot-dealer a free £lOO accident policy as a bonus
on his purchase. A minute later, on the pavement out-
side the shop, he slipped upon a banana-peel, fractured
his skull, and died next day.
* * * *
Before the electric-light came to Launceston
(Tas.) the Gas Company used to be very independent.
Incoming tenants had to pay the arrears of preceding
tenants, or no gas, etc. The new editor of one of the
dailies got an astonishing bill for his household gas
at the end of his first month. At first he thought
it was a joke. When he found that £9 odd was seri-
ously demanded he forwarded a protest. Back came
the stereotyed reply, “Have examined the meter, and
there is no escape.” He replied, “ Apparently there is
no escape ; cheque enclosed.” Next day he telegraphed,
“There is an escape. I have bought kerosene lamps.
Cut off your gas.” One Launceston daily thereafter took
great interest in the lighting question, and ultimately
electric-light became an accomplished fact.
* * * *
A Melb. softwoods firm lately went under. A
German band that haunts the neighborhood lined up in
front of the warehouse the other day, and delivered the
“ Dead March in Saul ” as an appropriate Made-in-Ger-
many joke. The other warehouses of Cinders-lane are
touchy on the subject of insolvency, and when the band
came round with the hat later in the day, it found that
the usual silver encouragement was not forthcoming.
“ We must emphatically disapprove of any insult offered
to a highly-respected firm.”
* * * *
A few years ago Sydney money-lenders would
behave scornfully if asked to compile a bill of sale for
a less amount than £lO. Now six in one street do most
of their business in sums of 30s. and £2.
* * * *
The Melbourne agent for a boss British
firm of watch and clock manufacturers and im-
porters (from France), reports with sorrow that Germany
can now undersell both of those countries in certain
“lines” of the trade where they were formerly un-
rivalled, and supply equally good work. At this rate the
law compelling imported German goods to bear the name
of their birthplace will soon need to be withdrawn. The
brand “Made in Germany” wasn’t intended fora guar-
antee of excellence.
* ♦ * •
Only now, when Brisbane (Q.) City Council
loan indebtedness is mounting up into big five-figures, is
heard the harsh, John-the-Baptist voice of one alderman,
warning his mates to provide a “sinking fund.” And
this is only when the annual aldermanic elections are on.
One stumping alderman actually bragged to his public
meeting that, although the City Council had “struck”
the scavenging rates as usual for the past two years, the
citizens had not been called upon to pay a penny of them.
Which looks like a broad road to insolvency and repu-
diation.
* * *
Northward the sugar empire takes its way.
Mackay and Bundaberg are going the way Maryborough
went lang syne. That is to say, 15 years of continuous
cropping has exhausted much of the rich cane-land, and
planters are on the qui vive for fresh country. The Her-
bert, the Mosman, the Batavia will provide much of the
sugar of the future. When they are used up by the shift-
less methods of the Queensland planter the business must
move on again round the shores of the Gulf to the
Northern Territory or Gehenna or somewhere.
* * * *
An expert remarks that bogus bank-notes can
always be distinguished from genuine, as they are in-
variably smaller than the latter. The original steel
plates are drawn to allow for the shrinkage of the
transfer paper containing the bank-note imprint, and as
the counterfeiter must necessarily engrave his plate
just the size of the specimen genuine note he traces
from, a discrepancy in their size cannot be avoided when
the damped proofs are pulled and the further shrinkage
ensues.
* * * *
A div. ought to be due from the B.H. British
by March, but may possibly be deferred until the general
meeting, to be held in London about the end of May.
The co’s half-year ended on Dec. 31, and as the concen-
trators were started in June, the results of a full half-
year’s work will be shown. The operations throughout
have been conducted at a profit.
British B.H. Proprietary directors follow a
practice which some others that are building up alleged
reserves might adopt - with advantage to their share-
holders—set out as part of their balance-sheets the
nature of all their investments.
* * * *
Australia, and N.S. Wales especially perhaps,
hardly yet realises how great its B.H. silver-field still is.
Bendigo, Victoria’s present premier goldfield, produced in
1896 200,2130 z. gold, worth, say, £760,000. B. Hill’s
metal export was worth £1,850,000. Charters Towers,
Q’land’s premier field, not out 260,0000 z. gold, worth, say,
£1,000,000. Broken Hill last year produced a greater
value of metal than Victoria’s alluvial wonder, the Madame
Berry, did in all the years it was worked.
B.H. North shares, after a sharp advance, de-
clined on the temporary stoppage of the exploratory
working from the Junction North. Concerning which
exploratory drive, by the way, let not shareholders and
others when they read (in a North mine newspaper re-
port) that it is in “massive sulphides,” and so on, con-
clude that it is yet inside the North lease. So far as the
lode has been traced it is still far from the boundary.
* * * *
B.H. rainfall for ’96 was 6^in.—the lowest since
the silver-mines were opened. But station records for
the same district credit two consecutive years with under
4in. each 1
* * * *
When the ’9O maritime strike occurred the
Broken Hill mines were soon in difficulties from want of
supplies. As the B.H. miners were good contributors to
the strike-funds an arrangement was made (after three
weeks’ stoppage of work) by which B.H. supplies were
handled at the ports. Upon the outbreak of the
engineers’ trouble the managers lost no time in an-
nouncing that the mines would not be affected; and
shareholders were no doubt appeased. The B.H. Prop.’s
position is strong; it undoubtedly has a big reserve of
coal, &c., which it carefully maintains. But as to the
others there are doubts ; if they have substantial reserves
thev are very skilfully hidden—so skilfully that share-
holders are hardly justified in taking their existence for
granted.

made no profit.
• • * *
The chairman of the Sydney Harbor Collieries
Co., in London recently, said the co. had received extreme
and bitter opposition from the N.S.W. Govt. “1 do not
like to say too much, but when I tell you that there are
several gentlemen in the Govt, of N.SiW. who are very
largely interested in other collieries ad jacent to Sydney, you
can easily understand any opposition to our scheme that
has arisen.” He then foreshadowed the great things
coming out of this scheme. Perhaps so, but the present
prospects of finding unlimited quantities of good coal at
a depth of 3000 ft. do not warrant the risk of £200,000.
In the Vic. coalfields, it is the practice to keep boring
ahead to locate the seam, because of . the occurrence of
dykes. In this case two bores, heavily subsidised by the
Govt., were put down. One found good coal, the other
very bad. The common-sense plan before starting a
shaft, which alone will cost over £lOO,OOO, would be to
put down some more bores, and find out if the extent and
character of the seam warranted the larger expenditure.
By the way, what is the nature of the “ bitter opposi-
tion ” which the co. received from N.S.W. Govt.? All
that is known here is that the Govt, refused to hand over
one of the most beautiful spots on the harbor to be turned
into an eyesore. Probably that is what the complaint is
about.

Perseverance, Stuart-Town (N.S.W.): “The
tributors crushed 19 tons for 2Joz. to the ton. This mine
was recently floated in London for £112,000. The labor-
suspension is up about the middle of Feb., when it is ex-
pected the new co. will begin operations.” With all that
monish, we should hope so, but shall see. Many alleged
London co’s never make a start at all. One already in
the same camp probably wishes it never started, as it
would thereby have saved from £lO,OOO to £15,000. The
working of old, abandoned, and duffered-out N.S.W.
mines by English co’s is a sad spectacle.

Taylor’s Reward mine, N.S.W., now called the
Beacon : First crushing, 1030 oz. for a three weeks’ run of
a 10-head mill —about 300 tons. No time lost in the
erection of the mill, and the Exploration Co. will, it is to
be hoped, pull out well on this deal. Stated that it has
already got a bond on another mine in the same district—
the Coramba King—and is about to test it with a crush-
ing of 1000 tons. The owners’ faith in it must be a long
way above the average. The average N.S.W. mine would
be exhausted before the crushing was through.

Golden Gully after Xmas. Bull has had a ring through
his nose in this show. A waterjacket is going up on the
Ironclad mine at Cargo, and a sample of ore has yielded
soz. to the ton, besides copper. “As there is a large
body of this ore it is thought that under the smelting
process it will pay well.” One of the mines “ looking
very well ” is the Madman, and there is another of the
same sort doing very well with the poor old S.M. Herald.

* * * *
Clarke and Co.’s Investors’ Guide for Dec.
contains a special report on the W.A. goldfields by Claude
Maitland. He says of the mines of Ooolgardie, that but
for Burbank’s and a few others, the output would soon
be nil. The “few others” not specified. Burbank’s
itself seems to have been closed to his inspection. The
three big mines of Kalgoorlie—Boulder, Lake View, and
Ivauhoe—will, he thinks, be able to turn out 20,0000 z. a
month among them within the next 12 months. There
are 11 gold-producers, and others will be added shortly.
As in Ooolgardie, more attention has been paid to floating
blocks, many of which are of no value, than to bona fide
mining, and some shows even in the immediate vicinity of
the big mines are very disappointing up to date. The
number of co’s which must collapse from the want of
gold will be startling. The careful enumeration of all the
big yields and omission of all the duffers colors the
description.

only chance that remains is to prove statements by re-
sults.” Well, what other chance does any reasonable
mine expect ?
* * * *
The Great Fingail Reefs, Limited, capital,
£175,000, fully paid, is the latest Westralian derelict.
Originally the co. held 264 acres, but it sold 96 acres to
the Fingail Reefs Extended for £lOO,OOO in paid-up
shares, and 96 acres to the North Fingail Reefs for
£lOO,OOO of which only £2500 was cash. The North
Fingail prospectus contained five reefs 1 % to 4ft. wide,
according to Bewick, Moreing and Co., the monstrously
celebrated experts who reported on the property. The
Fingail Reefs Co. “ recognised ” they had four principal
lodes, one of which was worth gold per ton from a
width of sft. of solid quartz. At the meeting of the
G.F.R. Co. in London, on 27th Nov., the chairman, with
tears in his eyes, said the parent co. had given back to its
puppies all the vendor’s paid-up shares, and also to the
North Fingail Co. the machinery on its ground. As to
their own reefs, the G.F.R. chairman spoke to the effect
that they had all failed to come up to expectations ; in
fact, the show wasn’t worth working. So the Great
Fingail apparently sleeps with its fathers, or at least is
getting its clothes off with a viewjto doing so.

* * * *
The London chairman on the great future of
the Bulong Mining, Tramway and Ore Reduction Co.:
“We have four channels through which to earn money.
The Last Chance mine is situated close to others from
which very rich stone is being raised. As to the Queen
of the Lake, there is said to be a very fine outcrop of a
very strong reef which may be traced on the surface for
some distance. We have ordered a tramway four miles
long and a 20-head mill. You are aware that without an
adequate supply of water, batteries cannot be run, or
human life sustained. Happily, we are very favor-
ably situated. Although I hesitate to say our supply
is inexhaustible, Mr. Fearby says, ‘At the present time
there are millions of gallons of water on the surface.’ lam
confident that you will never be short of water for all
milling purposes, no matter what plant you may erect.”
Fearby doesn’t hesitate, and has proved himself a most
accomplished Westralian boomster. How much water on
the surface of the lake now ? This concern has a working
capital of £25,000 to work mines, build a tramway, and
run a public crushing plant in W.A., and the amount pay-
able to the vendors for their rights is £lOO,OOO in cash and
shares!
* * * *
From a reliable London authority to The Bulle-
tin : —“ A regular dry-rot seems to have set in over
Westralians. There are no phenomenally heavy declines,
but a gradual and persistent dropping away of values, and
no support is forthcoming from the big houses who
engineered the boom, and, some of them, the slump as
well. In most cases quotations are purely nominal, with
no dealings. The public are simply choked with shares,
both here and on the continent, and will not subscribe to
any new issue till they can relieve themselves of their
present holdings.”
* * * *
Westralian output for Deo. a long way short of
anticipation -29,6530 z. About half of the 10t—14,2170z.
came from Kalgoorlie mines, and nearly the whole of this
from three or four mines the names of which every fool
knows. A few months back the Perth Mining Journal
declared that the Dec. return from the Ooolgardie fields
alone would exceed 40,0000 z. Murchison put out 87640 z. ;
Dundas comes along this time with 16oz. The W.A. re-
turn for ’96 amounts to 281,2630 z., value £1,068,805,
against 231,5120 z. last year. Compared with a few years
ago, W.A. is putting out a lot of gold, but the reflection
that even last year’s gold-product does not represent 2
per cent, on the capital of the Bull companies working in
that country is enough to indicate that a day of reckoning
must be near.
* * * *
“ I am certain that fully 75 per cent, of the
English and foreign co’s working mines in Westralia must
shut down. Some will linger longer, but the chances are
that the majority will shut up before the end of this
year.” That is the declaration of one of the most influen-
tial mining men that ever traversed the Westralian fields.
Before leaving, he himself closed down six mines. He
admits that the two big auriferous belts at Hannan’s, so
far as they have been developed, are in many respects
unequalled in the world, but their richness will no longer
help the hundreds of “cats” surrounding. A big per-
centage of duffers is expected in every boom, but the pro-
portion of “cats” in W.A. has never been reached in
any other mining boom.

elpim !—J.G.
* * * *
From W.A. : “A phenomenal find of gold is
reported to have been made in the Mt. Margaret district.”
But the public have long wearied of phenomenal finds in
that country. No man should now attempt to sell a
phenomenal W.A. mine without having his life heavily
insured. Take the other Mt. Margaret phenomenal find—
the Queen of the Earth. They slept on that too long ;it
has been found to contain far more gold than nine-tenths
of the people who saw it expected. ’Tis said they have
taken out some £15,000 worth. The mine has been long
for sale in London, but it doesn’t come off; and the share-
holders get no divs. Bull remembers the manner in
which he was wheedled into Bayley’s after the gold had
practically gone, also the gold knobs of the Plunderderry
on which you could hang your hat, and the bitter disap-
pointment of the Wealth of Expectations, and, in the
midst of his weeps, remarks that he is not taking any
phenomenal mines at present, and probably his warning-
will descend to the third and fourth generation.

poorhouse.”

The miners working on a much-lauded W.A.
show are now told that their inability to see gold in the
6oz. lode stuff which they are shovelling out is due to the
fact that the precious metal exists in the form of tel-
lurides, “ which are not visible to the naked eye.” “ Tel-
luride ” is the newest excuse in the Golden West.

* * * *
A London director lately came out to inspect
his company’s outside show in Westralia, and after he
had seen the barrenness of the place, and the hopeless-
looking white quartz, and the dry dam and the waterless
battery, his attention was fixed on the solitary tree that
ornamented the lease. “ I think,” he said slowly,
“ you’d better take great care of that tree. Some of the
shareholders might want to hang themselves.”
* * * *
Of the half-score Victorian coal-mining com-
panies, only one appears to be doing much good, and that
one has a powerful daily “ organ ” to boom it, and a
practical monopoly of the Vic. Railway supply. The
crowds on the directorates of nearly all the Vic. coal cos.’
boards seem to have been unfortunately selected. Some
notoriously know nothing of the business. Some are
decayed boomers. Unless directors who know the busi-
ness are appointed, Vic. coal-mines, as dividend-paying
investments, look very black.
Long Tunnel (Vic.), 1750 tons, 1816 oz. ; South
Long Tunnel, 1750 tons, 510 oz. What a falling off is here.
Why? Madame Berry West, 2460 z.; Berry Consols No. 1,
2410 z. ; Berry Consols Extended, llloz. These alluvia)
mines have taken up the running of the old Madame
Berry, recently deceased.

Victoria Quartz Co. (Bendigo) looks well fox

* * • *
The once great Shenandoah (Bendigo) is all
awry—no dividends for five months from its section of the
deep reef, which, when it left the New Chum ground and
entered the Shenandoah, seemed to leave its gold behind.
This doesn’t put roses on the prospects of the Shamrock,
just ready to break the same run of stone on the other
side of the Shenandoah.
Bendigo’s economic mine is the St. Mungo. Past
year’s stamping a shade under 6dwt. to the ton, but the
co. managed to divide 18s. 6d. per share. Plenty of pay-
able stone still to stope. Ex-div.-payer Lady Barkly is
feeling her feet again. Below the 1130 ft. level the winze
is down in payable stone, though it will take the co. quite
three months to get it ready for effective working. The
Great Southern divided £5906 in ’96. Better prospects
for this year, as the stone between the 980 ft. and 1030 ft.
levels is improving, while the crosscuts give every pro-
mise of turning up trumps.
Ulster United (Bendigo) should, with luck,
shortly cut the Great Southern reef in the 900 and 1000 ft.
levels, and, on the opposite side, it may get the London-
derry’s patchy deposit.
The Londonderry (Bendigo) is resting once
more on its smellful, skyrocketty career. Had
but a small shoot of payable stuff at 1200 ft., and
the indications of getting more golden stone are
not too encouraging. The mine is the most specu-
lative, and the crowd which fools with it is the hottest
on Bendigo. Even in the best of times the Lon-
donderry was a spec, wherein tbe amateur speculator
almost always fell in.
New Moon (Bendigo) is steadily picking out
divs. from a mass of spurs 120 ft. wide and height yet un-
known. Stone crushes a shade over 7dwt., and heaps of
it in sight. Specimen Hill, New Hopeful, New Argus, and
the Virginia Tribute (whose term expires in July, before
which the co. expects to pay 4s. on each 24,000 share) are
also profitably working a big run of spurs. New Argus
has the pick of the formation, thickening to 30ft. in its
660 ft. level, and very payable. Of the rest, the Specimen
Hill gives promise of being the most profitable.
Catherine Reef (Bendigo) for the year —48360 z.
from 13,392 tons ; £5070 divs. Spurs at 870 ft. still carry-
ing gold, but the big reef there has averaged very low of
late. However, the co. has favorable country to develope
in the 1500 and 1600 feet crosscuts; the latter having
exposed an 18ft. reef showing the color. Koch’s Pioneer
will most likely be B.’s ’97 div. champion. Breaking a
strong reef at 1450 ft., with something like 600 ft. of it to
dig out before touching the north boundary; also, for
1000 ft. above, the ground is virgin, so the prospects are
good. Besides, at 1310 ft., it cannot be long before Koch’s
grips the Windmill Hill reef.
* • * •
New Red, White and Blue (Bendigo) has done
its 100th div.; total for seven years, £125,000, and only
one 3d. call made. Deeper stone is turning out even a
better average than heretofore.
« * * *
New Chum Railway, Bendigo’s great deep level
mine, has a great future both before and behind. Last
year’s crushings, 13,535 tons for 13,2730 z. 6dwt., is a fine
average fjAm a reef at 2878 ft. Having exhausted the
rich ston_morth, the co. is now opening out at 3000 ft. to
work the southern continuation to the Eureka boundary.
y * * *
At the Creenhill rush (N.Q.) nuggets are un-
known but slugs are common. Miner Hogan recently
came on the cap of the reef and dollied out 150 oz
cluding a beautiful 21b. specimen. One onlooker became
so excited that he rushed to town to spread the news and
came back to find pegs in everywhere and himself “ out
of it.”
* * * «
Thus the warden re the Etheridge (Q.) : “An
enormous area saturated with gold from the surface
downwards. Has produced for the last 16 years an aver-
age of 20,0000 z. of gold per annum, and can continue the
same with the present primitive appliances for the next
50 years.” Green Hills employs about 300 miners at
present. Ravenswood has suffered through the shutting-
down of the chlorinating-works. Clermont has turned
out 11,3600 z. for 11 months as against 11,9860 z. for 1895.

Victoria, Charters Towers : Fortnight’s yield,
185 tons, 4890 z. A consistent div.-payer for years with
at least two years ore in sight and shares at Bs. 9d. The
fickle public loves the desert mine where the veins are
uncertain, mining dear, and divs. few and far between.

Despite the coming fever season, N.Q. miners
are beginning to flock to the latest small El Dorado—
Membare, New Guinea. Forty left Townsville last week.

Dear Bulletin, —Re a recent par. Your fears
as to the number of men likely to be “ leaded ” in the Mt.
Lyell mine are groundless, inasmuch as the big mine con-
tains no lead. The sulphur question, however, is cer-
tainly getting serious. The killing of all vegetation for
three or four miles around is a small matter compared
with the effect of the fumes on drinking-water. The
sulphuric acid combines with the zinc on the iron roofs,
and fills the tanks with sulphate of zinc. A woeful
stomach-ache is the immediate result of drinking this
water, or tea made with it. Isn’t sulphuric acid a highly
marketable commodity? Anyway, for the credit of the
company, it is to be hoped they will adopt some means of
preventing their fumes from poisoning the whole neigh-
borhood, and compelling people to take their whisky
neat-.—R.
* * * •
Tas. ’96 gold-output : 63,0000 z., against 58,380
for ’95.
* * • *
S.A. had last year one div. from a gold-mine,
and that a little one—The New Milo, at Wadnaminga,
amount £620. Having next to no gold-mines of their
own, the S.A. people have a pretty fair whack of those of
the other provinces. The Barrier mines produced them a
pot of money, and, so far, they have had the best of the
W.A. boom.

COMEO
In one Australian city, at all events, Good
Templary has got down into a very unwholesome
state. The last few years have been full of
startling incidents in the history of the Noble
Order. About the first of the series was con-
nected with a Worthy Brother who was about to
be married to one of the ewe lambs of the flock,
when it transpired that he had a wife living in
another province. The timely revelation broke
up his vision of bliss and he dropped out of the
Order and departed for another town where he
became a lay preacher. Then he abjured lay
preaching and became a member of a festive local
society, and started to put up record drunks which
paralysed even the inhabitants of that much-
whiskyed region, and married a bush girl, and
was short in his cash, and became mixed up in an
entanglement with a strange woman, and finally
disappeared in the night taking almost all the
assets of the township with him. It was a his-
toric disappearance, and the Noble Order has
hardly recovered the shock to this day. The
father of the deserted bush wife pursued the
levanter, but fell off his horse en route and broke
his neck.
Then a dry-rot set in. One lodge secretary
stepped out into space one fine night, taking all
the assets with him and leaving his particular
Cold Tea fraternity stone-broke. Another diddled
his lodge in a more artful fashion. It was a
wealthy lodge, which owned its own hall, pur-
chased by public subscription in the old, roaring,
affluent days, and vested in trustees. All the
trustees died save one, and no successors were ap-
pointed. The solitary survivor sold the property
for £ 2000, and never fully accounted for the
money. It had been decided that the wealthy
lodge should admit no more members, but
simply proceed to die by effluxion of time
and by rigid expulsion of all members suspected
of giving way to der-rink, and then the last half-
dozen of the Brotherhood were to wind up the
lodge and divide the assets and go out of the
Good Templary business finally. The principal
asset was the £2OOO received on account of the
hall, which sum the trustee alleged was safely in-
vested on mortgage. The scheme of winding up
was carried out —save in one particular only;
when it was near time for the final closure the
trustee and the coin went amissing in the old
silent way, and there was nothing to divide. The
lodge closed with wailing and gnashing of teeth,
and the members never gathered together in
brotherhood any more.
Then notable lodges burst up in quick suc-
cession, and sold off the assets at a great sacrifice.
One Shining Light relapsed from the faith, and,
as he was a rich man, his defection was much felt.
Another disappeared over the horizon, closely
followed by his two wives and the police. A
venerable Worthy Brother, a Past Grand Ever-
somuch of great age and vast respectability, was
found by his wife hugging a Flossie in his own
office, and the good old lady assaulted him with
a brolly in the public street to the great scandal
of the Order. She also tore most of the apparel
off the Flossie, and thereby added more publicity
to the matter. And what with embezzlements
and bigamies and Flossies and rumors of Flossies,
Good Templarism in that city is now practically
extinct.

Dear Bulletin, —You say, “ Apropos the
eternal question of the police-court Bible as a
source of infection.” Well, bury the eternal ques-
tion once and for all time. Any witness may
elect to be sworn in the Scotch manner, which con-
sists in simply uplifting the right hand. It is
within everybody’s legal right, and those who con-
tinue to “ kish the buke ” any longer will do so
distinctly at their own option, and without any
maudlin sympathy from anybody.—X.

The pious Brisbane Telegraph doesn’*
publish the yearnsome advt. of the person who
wants to meet a lady with a view to “ matri-
mony,” &c., under a separate heading after the
fashion of the soulless publication that is run for
purposes of pelf. But the advt. isn’t refused ;it
goes into “Miscellaneous,” along with pills, loans
on good shecurity, horses, bricks, bicycles, kip-
pered herrings, and drinks, so it gets there all the
same. And thus does the Telegraph make the
best of both worlds and serve both mob and
gammon.
A widow living on a
small orchard not far from
Sydney lately advertised for
a husband. By ten o’clock
in the morning of the day
of publication a man, ar-
rayed in his Sunday suit,
was on the spot. At half-
past ten the man rushed out
of the house to bail up a
passing clergyman, and
offered him a pound to fix
things up then and there.
He declined on the ground
of unseemly haste, and the
happy couple adjourned to
the township half-a-mile off,
and were hitched at cut-
rates. Oil their return home
a group of smirking and ex-
pectant males, many of
them carrying flowers, sur-
rounded the cottage, and
the newly-married pair got
rid of them by referring to
a widow down the road,
mother of half-a-dozen chil-
dren, as the real advertiser.
A second marriage is antici -
pated.

Until the other day, when the sandy town
of Nevertire was blown off the face of the globe,
there was no record of a cyclone having struck any
Australian settlement in a serious mood. Any
number of cyclones have raged in this country,
but towns being few and small and far between it
was like gunning at a florin at two-miles range,
and the storm always missed its mark. Appar-
ently, however, things are improving. Just about
ten days after the successful removal of Nevertire
a hurricane practically removed Palmerston
(N.T.) from the map. That lone Chinese town
has hardly a building left standing. The gale
came on it from seaward, and swept away Ah Fat
and Hong Kee like Chows erected on the sand,
and a rushing cloud of dismantled shanties, Mon-
gols, blasphemy, Chinese literature, kitten soup,
and galvanised iron roofs has been blowing south-
ward towards the M‘Donnell Ranges ever since.
No one knows yet the exact amount of the dam-
age, but in a general way it seems that the only
town in the Territory may be written off as a bad
debt, or something very near it.

The cable-message in respect to the illness
of the Queens daughter, last week, was verv
awkwardly worded. It ran thus: “Princess
Louise unwell enormous.” But the last word was
part of a reference to an enormous gathering of
kahomedans at Bombay to pray for tie cessation
of the plague. The rest of the plague message
arrived later,just when the tired sub editor w!s
about to publish a statement that Louise had
swelled to six times her ordinary size.

Poor Governor Brassey’s life is a tempos
tuous one. Only the other day a demented artist
Miss KATHARINE HARDY (of the Brough Comedy Co.,
Sydney Criterion).
Photo., Bond de Co., Adelaide.
called round to die in Brassey’s arms, and this
trouble was hardly over before another arose in the
shape of a short, stout lady named Gallagher, who
claimed to be Lady Brassey, and insisted on going
in to embrace her husband and take her rightful
place by his side. She gave the guard outside and
all the flunkeys in the hall one day’s notice to quit,
and she sacked the policeman on duty, and wanted
to knock the aide-de-camp endways. Then there
was a great struggle and much display of stocking
before she was wrestled into a cab, and she had
hardly arrived at the lock-up before she sent notice
to magistrate Panton that he wouldn’t be required
any longer, and ordered the immediate disband-
ment of the Yic. militia.

“The influence of adver-
tisement upon the personal
appearance of business men
is,” writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent, “an interesting
byway of the fin de siecle
road to success. One patent-
medicine vendor, whose
N.S.W. trade alone is colos-
sal, -wears a moustache solely
in the interests of the litho-
graphed circular. Without
it his face would not be a
household friend among those
who don’t feel well. Facial
character often, like Damo-
cles’ sword, hangs by a hair.
Another head of a large Syd-
ney emporium dolefully des-
cribes how his very best girl
capriciously insisted on his
shaving off a distinctive
beard, which would have des-
troyed the utility of some
thousands of newly-ordered
“ blocks,” and how an at-
tempt to temporise with the
demand broke off a promis-
ing engagement. One Aus-
tralian politician at least, on
being approached on one
occasion as to the advisa-
bility of having his hair cut,
is reported to have stoutly
refused _ on the ground of
betraying the cartoonist.
This is a theme which in-
vites speculation. When
once a man gets up a dis-
tinctive advt. he becomes a
slave to his “ad.,” and it
owns him body and soul.

ARTHUR M. ADAMS
UNDER THE STARS.
Heart of my heart, when the dark
Creeps out of his cave in the mountain,
When Love lights a glow in the deeps of the park,
A sheen in the fountain:
While the stars whisper songs to the moon,
And tenderly tell their loves to her,
You’ll come to me silent and tremulous soon,
And at last I, your wooer,
Shall clasp you and kiss you, aflame
At the light in your low-lidded eyes, Love,
Till your swooning soul answers the passionate
claim
That in my heart cries, Love.
ESA KAROS.

* * _ . * * .
The Petone (M.L.) mystery is being
gradually sorted out. It started by the chopping
up with an axe by some person unknown of an old
inhabitant named Jones and his wife. The axe-
man did his deed at night and left absolutely no
clue behind him, and for a while the police went
round and arrested anybody who looked shabby
and friendless enough to fix a suspicion on to.
Then one Stephen Bosher, in a beery moment, told
Mrs. Bosher that he had a previous wife living,
and that lady told the police about mysterious
blood-stains on Bosher, and the force settled itself
Mrs. C. G. LANGE (Miss Christie Fuller).
Photo., The Falk Studios.
down in dead earnest to disentangle the problem,
which was something like this : Bosher couldn’t
be convicted except with the aid of the second
Mrs. Bosher’s testimony. As the accused’s wife
wasn’t a competent witness, the only thing
to be done was to find the first Mrs. Bosher, to
prove her identity and gaol Bosher for bigamy,
and then, having demonstrated that the second
Mrs. Bosher wasn’t Mrs. Bosher at all, to use her
evidence in the matter of the Jones assassination
if she didn’t change her mind by then and refuse
to give evidence at all. It was a long, devious
road that led to Jones’s alleged assassin, but the
police set their square-toed boots firmly into it and
plodded along. Things are now so far advanced
that Bosher has just started doing two years for
bigamy, and then, just when everything seemed
in order, the second Mrs. Bosher fell ill, and, un-
less she recovers, the question of Jones’s butchery
may never get into the courts after all.
In one Australian capital it will shortly be
necessary to execute a Minister’s rather nice-look-
ing daughter in the interests of peace, and pro-
bably her mother also. Since the plain old man
rose to his present dignity, the remarks “My
father, the Premier, you know,” and “My hus-
band, the Premier, you know,” are dragged in so
incessantly that nothing but bloodshed can ade-
quately meet the case. At one time there was an
idea that if the two ladies were severely admon-
ished and perhaps slightly dislocated on the rack
and then branded, it might be sufficient, but the
case has now grown too aggravated for anything
short of death.
Mrs. C. C. Lance, nee
Christie Fuller, who sang
with such success the con-
tralto music of “The Mes-
siah ” at Sydney Town Hall
on Christmas night, was born
at Port Elizabeth, South
Africa, but came to Mel-
bourne at an early age. Her
father, a musical amateur of
ability, was one of the foun-
ders of the first Melb. ora-
torio society. Miss Fuller
showed marked musical tal-
ent, and when only 11 years
old, at the performance of a
cantata at Prahran Town
Hall, stepped down from her
seat in the chorus and suc-
cessfully took the part of the
contralto principal, who had
failed at the last moment.
At 15 years Miss Fuller was
studying under Madame
Christian ; at 18 she made
her professional debut in the
Melb. Town Hall; and for
several years she sang and
taught very successfully.
Tripping then to England,
after a notable farfewell con-
cert,. Miss Fuller studied for
some time with H. C. Dea-
con, and before quitting Lon-
don gave several successful
matinees and concerts under
distinguished patronage. Ex-
hibition year found her once
more in Melbourne, receiving
a “ welcome ” concert on
landing ; and before return-
ing to England to resume
her studies she sang with
Santley in Melbourne and
Adelaide. Returning to Aus-
tralia in ’9O, by way of the
Cape, Miss Fuller undertook
a musical tour through South
Africa, followed by her mar-
riage and temporary retirement from the profes-
sion. Mr. and Mrs. Lance have recently settled
in Sydney, and Mrs. Lance’s beautiful voice—a
light contralto of conspicuously sympathetic
quality—which has only gained by rest, will doubt-
less be highly prized in the local musical world.
* * * *
At Cheltenham (Vic.) court, the other
day. Boy, 12 years of age, is brought up on a
charge of setting fire to his mother’s stable and
hay-shed. Pleads guilty. Doctor called as wit-
ness deposes that boy is weak of intellect. Police
withdraw charge. Chairman of Bench gravely
lectures imbecile (who is intensely interested in
watching the flies on courthouse ceiling) and winds
up with the sententious observation : “If you
look up the act, my lad, you will find you have
rendered yourself liable to 15 years imprison-
ment.” Law-bred irony, or simple, fat-headed,
ignorant J.P.-ism ?It was either one or other.
And the man who uttered it is the kind of being
who has the power to send intelligent persons to
gaol!
How is it that the alleged democratic Melh.
Age never buys a serial story which doesn’t deal
with the doings of members of the aristocracy? Is
it because David Syme knows his business better
than any outsider can tell him ? The typical ser-
vant-girl despises a yarn that doesn’t in its first,
or, at any rate, second chapter, describe how,
“ with these crushing words of scorn, the haughty
heiress of Hasheastle, drawing her queenly form
to its full height, swept proudly down the grand
old hall of her ancestors. ” Are the readers of the
Age —in the matter of literary taste —on a level
with the typical servant-girl ?
» « • *
A Vic. Western district man recently tra-
velled 50 miles to Portland to drown himself.
Undressing on the beach and about to commit the
deed, he noticed some girls bathing just near him.
He redressed, took the next train back to his
village, and was found two days later drowned in
an ordinary, common waterhole. He left word
that he preferred to die in the wide, green, breezy
sea rather than beneath the scum of the local pool,
but as a gentleman he couldn’t take his clothes off
and drown when ladies were present.
* * * * ->
111 Sydney the other day the police
besieged a weedy youth in a tree for nine solid
hours before they effected his capture, and it will
be a long time before the force gets its dignity in
working order again. The weedy person had
been stealing fruit, and being discovered he got
out on a high and very slender branch, which
wouldn’t support a “cop,” and hooted defiance.
For the best part of a day relays of police sat on
the grass through a series of tremendous showers,
waiting for him to drop off like over-ripe fruit,
while 400 small boys stood round and jeered. The
simple idea of burning sulphur under the tree and
smoking him out, or hanging a bottle of assafoetida
on the end of a long pole and holding it close to
windward of him and smelling him out, or shoot-
ing pepper at him and sneezing him out, never
entered, into the trap’s simple philosophy. There
were many opportunities for some highly strate-
gical policemen to distinguish themselves under
the circumstances, but all they did was to wait
and soak till the youth surrendered late at night
through rain and cold shivers and cramp.
* * * *
A Melb. lady placed herself under re-
straint, the other day, because she had got into
the habit of consuming 255. worth of laudanum
weekly.
* * * *
The very worst push at present raging in
Sydney consists almost entirely of clerks, who
wear store-clothes and straw hats, and storm the
town to the accompaniment of a specially hideous
battle-cry of great penetrating povver. Some of
them nearly blew down one of the suburbs the
other day, and, being captured by a cop, who took
his life in one hand and his revolver in the other,
they were duly fined by magistrate Isaacs 20s. each.
One pound for nearly wrecking three pubs, and
making the welkin ring with the horrid shriek of
the push seems a very moderate figure. Moral: If
you are a larrikin always wear good clothes and a
straw “cadey,”and then, instead of being an or-
dinary rowdy, you will simply be a misguided
young gent, giving vent to his overflowing spirits.
* * * *
“ We’ve been married 17 years,” said the
plaintiff in a recent Wellington (M.L.) divorce-
case, “ but we don’t live together now. I get on
as best I can in the house, and he bachelorises in
the cow-shed, and the cow lives in the garden.”
LOVE’S LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Too late—now every mesh is closed—
I see
Love’s not the game that it’s supposed
To be.
Some hours of ecstacy each week
We know ;
But ere we scarce have time to speak
They go.
And, though ’tis Heaven while I dream
With you,
By far those honeyed moments seem
Too few;
And all the dreary hours I spend
Away,
Long Hells with no apparent end
Are they.
My days of sport, my nights of fun,
Are o’er;
The pleasures that from life I won—
No more.
The girls and pals I was so proud
To see
Are now a brutal, soulless crowd
To me.
And, where my best-loved barmaid’s eyes
And bust
Once paced my pulse, I realise
Disgust.
I m wild when fellows say she “ain’t
Too bad ” ;
To-day she seems to me all paint
And pad.
And even liquor’s not a bit
The same ;
Gone are those times we were so fit
And game.
When “ straight from stable ” tips in luck
We took,
And deep in doubles gaily struck
Each book.
Gone are those dreams in dazzling tights
And hair;
Those willing, thrilling, killing nights . . .
Somewhere.
My old-time pleasure should I e’er
Regain
To steer from love I’ll take good care
Again.
sth Jan., 1896.
McG.
MAN.
A little birth,
A little cry,
A little youth,
Then, bye-and-bye ;
A little love,
A little weep,
A little pain—
Then one great sleep.

returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

BRISBANE.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Jan. 12, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
The match between N.S.W. and S.A., on the
Sydney Cricket Ground, attracted vast crowds of
women—especially on Saturday—and the new
stand was vivid with hats of the most glaring and
eccentric styles: there were rows and rows of
magenta roses and puce poppies and green daisies
all a-blowing and a-growing. The suburban girl
in her thousands was there, and the evolution of
her head-gear may be traced from the show in an
expensive shop-window, crossed by a flop and a
tilt copied from Mrs. Brown-Potter’s inspirations
in curves and angles, modified by the exigencies of
the finances of the suburban home and varied by
the taste and fancy of the amateur milliner. Even
a man-reporter was moved to mild wonder in a
daily by Saturday’s hat-display. Brilliant
chapeaux were made more conspicuous by the
manlessness of the ladies’ stand. Men members
sit coldly and proudly in their own stand, and their
best girls can only gaze wistfully at them across
the wire-netting which wards off the woman’s
quarters like a rabbit-proof fence. The place
looks like a synagogue on Saturday mornings.
One or two members of the local team in
celestial blazers were adorning the ladies’ stand
during the afternoon. The crush in the tea-room
was evidently unexpected; there were not too
many waitresses, and customers had to sit
patiently hoping for a cup of flabby cold tea or
sloppy coffee. In the matter of dresses, black-
and-white effects were among the smartest.
One girl, with an amateur reputation as a prima-
donna, showed some white lace over black
silk, and a bewitching hat, all black feathers
and red crown and diamond buckles. A married
ex-Melb. belle was also in black and white of a
confused pattern, and a hat like a stra w-thatched
hut. A popular warbler clothed herself appro-
priately in canary yellow. Two fair-haired sisters
had almost invisible bonnets, and proved them-
selves thereby up-to-date, for the tiny is now
toney in millinery. Grass lawn costumes of the
early spring—soiled by three months’ wear and
tear—have evidently “ gone back ” on their owners
in the wash-tub, for they have all disappeared
from best-frock festivals. A little French woman
created an atmosphere of Paris by a judicious
blend of apricot and black.
Mrs. Silas Harding, widow of one of Vic.’s
wealthiest squatters, met with a sudden death
from a carriage-accident, at Dunkeld, Vic., on
Saturday. She passed through some romantic
episodes in her lifetime. As a young girl she
earned her living in many humble ways, and finally
as canvasser to a jewellery firm she captivated old
Silas Harding, then a widower, who married her.
At first the greatest diplomacy was needed to
screw any money even for her personal wants out
of the veteran. But he died and left her im-
mensely opulent. Slighted by the local first
families, she blossomed out among the Melb.
Nicest by acquiring the gorgeous stucco mansion,
planned and oecupied for a brief time by M. H.
Davies, at Toorak. “Bracknell” has brought
bad luck to both its proprietors. It seems to be
a boom-cursed place. Perhaps it’s the moulded-
iron staircase with crimson plush hand-rail that
blights its owner. Mrs. Harding entered into her
domain by giving a large garden-party there last
Cup time; everybody went and very few knew
their hostess by sight.
Tram-ticket poker is it favorite game among
Melbourne city-going clerks. They bet on the
numbers on their tickets if the figures read any-
thing like a good poker-hand. The enterprising
sharper sometimes unlawfully gets the stakes and
the “ kitty ” by keeping about him a lucky ticket
and producing it at the right moment. Is there
any game in this world at which man doesn’t ring
in a cold deck on his fellow-man ? I have heard
of a person who used to induce guileless sports
to bet on two flies sprinting across a window-
pane, and who found out a way—l forget what it
was—of making the other man’s fly run “ stiff.”
Selling seats by auction has been seized on as a
money-making scheme by the church as well as by
the stage. The sittings in a newly-erected chapel
were thus sold, and bidding was brisk for pews in
remote corners, while those just in front of the
pulpit went cheap. Worshippers now feel that
they are not trying to get saved “on the nod,”
but, having respectably paid their way, are free to
turn out any celestially-minded deadheads who
may wander in unwittingly and endeavor to find
peace out of hymn-books and pew-cushions paid
for by somebody else.
A party of Yankee enthusiasts calling them-
selves the “ Theosophical Crusade,” hired the
Protestant Hall two nights last week, hung some
gay bunting around the walls, placed pot-plants, a
piano, and other obstructions on the platform,
and proceeded to expound their views from among
the ornamentations to a room-full of disciples,
who were all soulful-looking. The men wore
swallow-tails and long hair, and the women bare
necks, glasses, and short hair. The lady-crusaders
were bright and cute : the men looked intense and
yearning with a great sorrow welling up in their
eyes, as if at the callousness of the outer world in
not receiving with joy the universal-brotherhood
theory.
By way of showing how to love everybody with-
out reference to sex, age or color, the leader of
the movement, Mrs. K. A. Pingley, explained
that this crusade had nothing to do with the other
branch of the P.S., which was a fraud and a delu-
sion, and denounced the other leader, Mrs. A.
Besant, and all her works. Only half the society
Bets itself, figuratively, to hug the entire
universe. The bogus half is willing to hug
everybody, but the real half is only prepared
to hug everybody except the bogus half.
This is practically what the audience gathered
from the speakers, and then the room
ceased to vibrate with the ’Murkan twang, the
audience arose with a tired sigh and departed, and
the cold gloom of midnight settled down once
more on the Hall.
A discussion on “Flirtation” is now languish-
ing in the columns of Sydney D.T. On this point
the women who write don’t know, and the women
who really know don’t write. For which their
fellow-flirters are doubtless grateful. Very few
women, even of genius, possess the immoral cour-
age of Georges Sand, who used to call round in a
cab for her young men, take them off with her to
Italy or Spain, and then make a novel and plenty
of money out of the incident. But when these
learned ladies lay down hard and fast rules to
guide a frivolity-loving public, can’t you _ tell
they’ve never “ been there ” themselves ? Flirta-
tion is all right as long as neither flirter nor flirtee
is in earnest; in which case it becomes rather too
exciting to be pleasant. Many an engagement is
a flirtation doing penance*
The woman who both knows and writes is some-
times inconvenient. One with a vast field of
reminiscence has lately been attending swell
London weddings—whereat she always cries—and
has been corresponding about them freely in the
newspapers, giving personal recollections of the
bridegroom when he was young and frivolous, or
recalling mental pictures of the thrioe-wedded
bride’s No. 2, and “somehow during the service
his face seemed to come back to me so plainly and
distinctly.” Pleasant for the bride !
This week, at the Cri., the gowns are of the
greatest importance, whatever the woman may be.
Even “The Woman of No Importance” herself,
Mrs. Brough, expresses just the right shade of
sorrow in her snuff-colored frock with lace soften-
ings. A woman who had been led astray by any-
body of less rank than a stage lord should wear
seal-brown with a mere touch of frilling at the
neck. If she is abjectly repentant and left penni-
less, her costume should be of unrelieved black.
The first scene looks gay enough for a garden-
party, and the harmonious blending of the wearers’
frocks proves that somebody must have passed a
sleepless night planning to have five women on the
stage together in colors that don’t clash. Miss F.
Seymour pursues Lord Illingworth in magpie silk
much be-chiffoned and a feathered hat ; she
mingles blue and green in a low-cut dinner-
gown.
The most affluent - looking gowns are ap-
propriately worn by Emma Temple as the Yankee
heiress; in No. 1 she seems to have shipped
it green, as Kipling kipples prettily in the
ballad of the Bolivar, m billows of green
glac4 silk tulle and embroidery; No. 2, a pink
evening frock, with the latest thing in sleeves,
stands out well in an artistically stage-managed oak-
room with crimson lamp-shades ; No. 3 is white
and simple and manages to combine a loose blouse
front with a neat waist-line. Miss Kitty Hardy
makes her best point with a brilliant red sunshade
on a cream-and-red creation. Mrs. Bracy is
tremendously awe-inspiring as a British matron in
red, and the other dowager frivols stoutly in the
person of Miss Watt-Tanner.
Crowds of Sydney theatre-goers now arrive at
places of amusement per wheel. The bicycles are
deposited in various spots around the building.
In the Palace vestibule they sometimes surround
the walls. The dress-as-you-please regulations
enable the cycling woman to adorn the d.c. in all
varieties of garb except evening dress. The wheel
is not built to display a girl’s upper half to advan-
tage. To look graceful on a bicycle a woman
must only end in revelations, like Lord Illing-
worth’s “ book of life.”
An invited few turned into the Salon Cinemato-
graphe on Friday noon, while Mr. J. M‘Mahon
explained his new pieces, just arrived per R.M.S.
They are very Frenchy, and show all sorts of
things that you needn’t go to gay Paree to see
now you can see them in Pitt-street, including
horseless-car racing and Moulin Rouge dancing
(expurgated edition).
“ An Angel in Muslin ” (F. R. Peel, Sydney), is
an air that ought to become popular, for it is fairly
catchy and is wedded to immortal verse by G.
Darrell, who must have had it very badly indeed
that time. Paling and Co. publish “ The Merry
Brewer of Bundaberg ” as morceau de danse, and
exhibit on the cover a photo, of a bearded gentle-
man as a guarantee of the statement. He looks
jolly enough, but a rather substantial morceau to
tread the mazy. “ Hyacinthia ” waltz, by C. E.
Mason (Nicholson and Co.), makes its little appeal
to the Nicest by being dedicated to Hon. Margaret
Brand. “Ariel,” by Florrie Banbury, waltzes
ambitiously into four flats —too many for the
average amateur.
The new “ mind-cure ” is now expounded in a
book called “ Practical Metaphysics for Healing
and Self-Culture. ” It is rather tough reading, but,
boiled down, the meaning seems to be that health
is either good or bad, as thinking makes it so. If
you steadily make up your mind not to have a cold
in the head, then you won’t annoy your friends and
family by sneezing and snuffling at the breakfast-
table ; or you can conquer an obstinate gumboil
by abstracting your mighty intellect with a deter-
mined wrench from your mountain of cheek ; or
you need feel no grief and pain after eating a
Sydney peach, if you only think hard enough that
the green ball of concentrated ache is a soft and
luscious sun-ripened fruit. There is really one
way in which “mind-cure” would supply a long-
felt want —that is, by diverting people’s attention
from their own particular aches and pains. Listen
to the conversation of a tram or carriage full of
women. Nine out of every ten are talking about
their health or want of it.
A tombstone dance is the latest style of social
upheaval. In a N.S.W. country town the
parishioners of the leading church were exercised
as to ways and means for cleaning up dilapidated
grave-fences, straightening old tombstones, and
regilding the faded “Hie Jacets ” in the local
cemetery which had fallen into disrepair. The
elderly parson’s youthful wife suggested a sub-
scription dance, which turned out a great success,
and left a margin for renovating the family vaults
of the township, after deducting exes, for claret-
cup and ham sandwiches.
Earl Russell probably holds the world’s record
at the moment in the matter of an objectionable
mother-in-law (he has just got Judge Haw-
kins to accommodate the lady with eight
months’ gaol). A local suburban bridegroom
showed a stern determination to refrain from try-
ing to break it, by having a type-written docu-
ment drawn up previous to his wedding, properly
signed and witnessed, whereby the bride’s father
and mother bind themselves to assert no claim to
enter her husband’s house.
