My Melbourne correspondent writes :
“ The first meeting of the Austral Salon for ’97,
which took place on Thursday, served as a fare-
well to Miss Nellie Veitch, who received from the
members gifts of barbaric gems and gold, much
effusion, and a silver m£(tch-box. Quite a large
number of intrepid males lurked in the back-
ground of the proceedings, one of whom was bold
enough to emerge from his stronghold and uplift
a very excellent tenor voice in a couple of ballads,
of the kind which are marked “four shillings on
the cover, and which, for some mysterious reason,
one always gets for two shillings or else three-
pence. R. S. Smythe extended his travel-limits
that evening, and visited, for the first time, the
lair of Melb. ’s intellectual womanhood.
“ The streets of this city have lately presented
an unusual and striking aspect, by reason of the
numbers of young men, obviously opulent and
cheerful, who spread themselves over the land-
scape and give to this Adamless Eden a decidedly
festive appearance. At the theatres, hordes of
them with much-creased dress-clothes and with
faces showing a good brick-dust tint above their
boiled shirt-fronts, clatter noisily out between the
acts, and become sleepy and inattentive before
the entertainment is over. Shipping-lists, showing
that over a thousand men are leaving the golden
West almost every week, give an explanation of
the influx.
“Society has not left this dusty city for the
mountain or sea-side in very large crowds this
year. A great number of the ton are still mer-
cifully left with us, and graciously condescended
to shed a faint, diluted lustre on each day of the
cricket-match, and to lend something like dis-
tinction even to the bicycle races at St. Kilda. A
field of raoing cyclists on a fine day, in their gaily-
colored silken jackets, rather pleases one’s aesthetic
taste, but the cyclist per se is an unbeautiful ob-
ject. Parsons, ui his neat “ suit of sable ” and
his compact, nuggety figure, sits rather grace-
fully and makes a pleasant picture while
racing. The invalided Bilmartin spread-eagles
all over his machine, and is not pleasant
to watch from a feminine point of view. Porta’s
f raceful fashion of entwining silken scarves round
is waist and bewreathing his face in smiles
whether he loses or wins makes him popular with
girl spectators and might be imitated advan-
tageously by other riders. The new English rider,
Relph, looks careworn and anxious over his race,
like one who regards it as a most serious and im-
portant thing.
“The shipping strike caused a wild exodus of
humanity in print frocks and flannel suits from
the fashionable seaside spooning resorts. On the
Sunday, when the Ozone went for what was
thought to be its last trip, the boat was rushed by
a crowd of about 2000 returning holiday makers,
with luggage attached, and a considerable number
of these were people who, in the ordinary course,
would prefer death to travelling on the Sabbath.
“ Miss Alice Muskett, the sister (not the
daughter, as sundry papers misstate) of Dr Mus-
kett, after touring Holland, has decided to settle
for another year in Paris, where she will probably
start a studio ‘on her own.’ Miss Muskett, by
birth a Melb. girl, owes all her art training to
Sydney.

Dear Bulletin,—You sav, “the man who
goes out cychng in a top-hat probably has but one
hat. More probably he is afraid to give himself
away in any other style of headgear. With many
men, the top-hat is absolutely essential to a
character for “ respectability.” Substitute for the
orthodox pot a soft felt or a straw (which is the
severest test of all), and those wooden-faced repre-
sentatives of a particular commercial school would
be undone. You remember the ponderous Patri-
arch in Little Dorrit.” When his long hair was
clipped short, children fled from him in horror
There are many heads of city firms whose appear-
ance would get them six months, as the savins
goes, if they were compelled to wear a flat-rimmed
boxer or straw. They arise at banquets and attri-
bute their precious “ success in life ” to all sorts of
causes save the real one—the top-hat—but they are
quite conscious of their weakness, nevertheless
Hence the dislike of city potentates in generai
for all men who go about their business in head
gear better suited to the climate. They feel them-
selves mocked and put to shame.—Clan Alpine
Bimerah Station (Q.) has just put up a re-
cord in Australian artesian boring. The bore has
gone down considerably over 4000 ft., and, having
thus established a record for depth, is still descend-
ing in search of the yet-unfound water. The bore-
casing is sin.
Quite a large number of contributors to
Australian Xmas numbers were surprised to re-
ceive a small shoal of begging letters immediately
after their tales appeared in print. The late
Lady Brassey reports, by the way, that the
amount requested from Lord B. by begging letter-
writers in the course of a year or two was over
i 11,000,000. In the present case the inky mendi-
cants evidently formed a very erroneous idea of
the amount of wealth which streams in upon the
author of a Xmas tale. As a rule, after he has
mollified his hash-tower woman with something on
account, the sum that remains is about enough for
one drink —a very ordinary drink, too.
Captain Goddard, a patriarchal mariner
who retired from the sea the other day, is cele-
brated as having voyaged from London to Sydney
regularly for 41 years, without once being led
astray into visiting other ports. Every year since
1855, this veteran has steered some sailing vessel
or other into Port Jackson and out again, and in
all that time he has never lost a ship, or met with
a serious accident, or cost the underwriters any-
thing. No other shipmaster has haunted Sydney
so persistently. Captain Goddard is aged 61, and
was born on January 26th—that great occasion
when the first Australian was officially lagged—
which circumstance established a connection
between him and this country from the very
beginning of his existence, and now he proposes
to settle down in Sydney and die there comfort-
ably when he happens to feel that way.
The Mundic Miner, Georgetown (N.Q.)
reports the doings of a recent church raffle up in
that forlorn region. “ One of the features of the
show is a bridal bed warranted to hold two. The
married ladies are contributing towards the ex-
pense of getting up this haven of future blissful
memories. The mode of disposal is original, for
no one is allowed to buy a ticket except bachelors,
and each one must get a spinster to throw for him.”
The soulful hug of the church comes out strongly
in this idea. Nobody except a church would think
of offering a bridal bed as a prize and raffling it
under such blushful conditions. But the taber-
nacle always has an eye to business. The winner
is almost bound to marry the giggling maid who
threw for him, and then the church will scoop in
the fees. No ordinary person could publicly win
a bridal bed without being harried into matrimony
very shortly afterwards.
A tragic love-episode, which reads like a
slab from some short French story, ocourred in
Little Lonsdale-street, Melb., last week. In that
fragrant locality lives one Ah Hing, a cabinet-
maker, and his frugal residence has been shared
for the last five years by a white girl, now 23 years
old. Until six weeks ago Ah Hing and his inamo-
rata lived an ideal existence. They loved each
other with the fidelity of a Romeo and a Juliet—
until the adjoining Chow brought home a big, fat
helpmate of about 40 summers. The Amazonian
proportions of his neighbor’s lady appealed to Ah
Hing, and very shortly his own girl caught him
making violent protestations of love to her through
a crack in the back fence. Mrs. Ah Hing that
ought to be broke her heart there and then over
her faithless Chow. She drank sixpenu’orth of
belladonna, then told her erring lord all, and lay
down to die. But in the face of this awful situa-
tion Ah Hing repented ; he burst into tears and
begged forgiveness at his idol’s feet, promising
never again to look at the huge creature next door.
Then the police came, and the girl was stomach-
pumped at the hospital and afterwards locked up
for attempting suicide, while her loving idolator
wept all night outside the lockup, and filled the
air with heartrending cries for his “ beau’ful Maly
Ann.” Even Little Lonsdale-street has its
romance now and then.
Spectre of my weak-willed, lost, soiled heart
Silent glide in the gondola, ashen, empty as thou
art,
A skeleton shame; a memory adrift on a sunless
sea,
Where slowly wanders the sluggish tide totvards
Infinity.

THE OLD LIFE AND THE NEW.
Sir Turner, now you knighted be, -
No more a democrat,
Those pants, so baggy at the knee,
That ancient honoured ’at;
Those awful boots and greasy ties
Henceforward we taboo ;
Their part is played, the Queen has made
Another man of you.
Shut your eyes, and droop your head.
Whilst we whisper—“ Turner’3 dead.”
Sir Turner, tilt a topper bright
Upon a haughty brow,
And point your “ patents ” left and right
(Bid Chirnside show you how);
And sell the famous suit of tweeds
For ninepence, to a Jew ;
Your title needs, in duds and deeds,
Another man of you.
Fold your hands upon your breast,
Like a pilgrim gone to rest.
Sir Turner, get with smarter togs
Dear Duncan’s dreamy smirk;
Devote your time to rolling logs
Instead of vulgar work.
Lay in a stock of Tory fibs,
And swear they all are true.
God save Her Nibs, who nobbled Dibbs
The way she nobbled you.
’Twas, alas ! a painful end.
Good-knight! dear, departed friend.

It will be interesting to note if Turner’s
luck goes out with his knighthood. So far he is
recognised as about the most successful Premier in
Australia ; he has steered the country through the
V°. rst years of the depression when Patterson,
ohiels and Munro all failed ; he has wrested more
concessions from the House of 48 Landlords than
any of his predecessors managed to do, and he got
them, not by any sensational row, but by silently
pegging away and wearing down the fossils with
sheer exhaustion. In the regular order of things
iurner should now fall over some unexpected
disaster and go right under politically; or he
should be found out in his hitherto concealed
iniquity ; or he should get swelled head and begin
to foregather with Sargood at champagne banquets
and maunder about the British Flag. If nothing
dreadful happens it will be directly contrary to all
the traditions of K.C.M.G.-ism.

Butcher-evangelist Varley has left these
shores for America. The beef-prophet dropped a
good deal of money over the Searchlight, that
vigorous little paper which ruined itself by damn-
ing everybody in the first issue, and so leaving
nobody to damn in the second. Also, some
more of the nose-blood money of sectarianism
went in trying to galvanise into new life a mori-
bund tabernacle at East Melbourne, and on the
whole Varley has been doing badly of late.
The champion economist of the Vic. public
service is a big-salaried telegraph official, whose
daily dinner consists of a half-dozen sandwiches,
which he usually produces each morning from
his coat-pocket wrapped in newspaper.*- Some-
times in a moment of abstraction the great man
leaves home without his lunch, but no sooner does
he discover the fact than a telegraph-boy is re-
lieved of duty and despatched to his boss's
suburban residence for the sumptuous repast.
The hero of this yarn is a firm supporter of the
vote for thrift. Thrift mostly consists of carrying
your food in your tail-pocket.

The gruesome account of the rescue of a
man named Hugh Williams from a derelict the
other day recalls the extraordinary train of cir-
cumstances connected with that name as chroni-
cled m the Admiralty Records. The first instance
harks back to 1684, when the Menai was lost, the
t° i^oJ being a man called Hu S h Williams.
Jin 1785 a schooner went ashore on the Isle of Man.
1 he brokers dLsgorgcd only one man, and he was
entitled Hugh Williams. In 1820 a picnic boat
was run down on the Thames, and out of the 25
corpses resulting only one cheated the coroner by
coming to life again. It was another Hugh
Williams And in 1889 a Leeds coal barge foun-
dered with nine men on board. This time there
were two Wilhamses (both Hugh) in the case, and
both were fished out, wet but spry. The talis-
manic virtue of a caul appears to sink into insig-
nificance compared with the advisability of being
baptised Hugh W. previous to entering upon a
maritime career. Possibly Jonah’s real name was
W llliams.

A certain well-known Vic. squatter told
the following recently at a fat-man dinner-party :
I once played a good joke on a yokel that I
met out on the run. ‘ Any sport, mate, ’ said I
quite friendly like. ‘Only a few mushrooms,’
said he.. I admired his gun, and he let me look at
it. ‘Nice cut of a dog,’ said I. ‘Best in the
land, he said, ‘ been offered a tenner for him.’ I
pulled the trigger and his dog was cold meat,
then I covered him with his own gun and made
him dance on the mushrooms, and with a
shot over his head I started him on the fastest
run for the fence that he ever did in his life. By
Gawd you would have laughed.” And then his
fellow hogs did laugh unanimously.

In Adelaide a disgusted community backs
Governor Tough Fowl Buxton as the most utterly
desolate of Australian public orators. There are
some others who elocute as badly as Buxton, but
for a complete want of anything to say, combined
with a tendency to state in sepulchral tones that
two and two, my deah brethren, are generally
supposed to make—ah—fou-ah, Buxton has no
equal.

There has been a good deal of doubt during
the last fortnight as to the actual whereabouts
of the Premier. When anyone inquired at the
Treasury they were told he had gone to Victoria.
It seemed to be a point of honor with the offi-
cials to be mysterious as to Mr. Reid's exact
whereabouts and the length of bis holiday. He
did manage to let himself be seen at the inter-
colonial cricket match in Melbourne, and then
vanished into obscurity again. The “Argus” has
discovered him, or at least one of its correspon-
dents has. This is what they say about him:—
“Our Lome correspondent telegraphs that Mr.
Reid, Premier of New South Wales, is at
present staying at the Grand Pacific Hotel there,
and has booked' his rooms for some time. Mrs.
Reid and one of his two children, who accom-
pany him, have been ordered to the seaside for
a change, and Mr. Reid has chosen Lome, where
he has stayed before, and which is a favorite
placs with him. to afford them the change de-
sired.” >

for more than two years. If sumo surprise is foil at the distinguished honor conferred upon him, it arises solely from the fact that he has given no indication of an
ambition for personal distinction other than that which necessarily springs from the important position he holds. Nevertheless, his many friends will sincerely con-
gratulate him upon this proof of Her Majesty’s favor. "— ARGUS.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Vic. State Land Bank, the second
institution of its kind in Australia and the third
in Australasia, opens for business in a few weeks.
S.M. Herald, as a Foreign-trade paper,
rises continually to point out that Foreign-trade
makes better and cheaper goods than Protection.
This is theory. And the paper the Herald is
printed on comes from New York because Pro-
tection makes better and cheaper goods than
Foreign-trade. This is practice.
The syndicator is very much abroad in
S.A. The scheme for a land-grant transcontinen-
tal line is never at rest; and now John Moule,
ex-Works Minister, desires to plank down a de-
posit in connection with a scheme for a land-grant
railway from Port Augusta to Esperance Bay
(W.A.), and is very wrath with Premier Kingston
for declining to even look at his proposals.
Vic. Opposition is now only a plaything
for fifth-rate Scotchmen. Old Scotch Gillies
dropped the sceptre and it went to Scotch
MTntyre, and Scotch MTntyre now thinks of
passing it back in an off-hand manner to Scotch
Gillies again. The Australian element in the
party counts for very little, and Duncan and Jock
and Jock and Duncan settle things between them.
The Maoriland election trouble is simmer-
ing down gradually. At a northern police-court a
man and his wife were run in as long as ten days
after the poll for fighting about the rival candi-
dates, and a policeman was prosecuted for illegal
arrest of both parties. Also, a Wellington State
school-teacher was called upon by an infuriated
parent to “please whack Smith’s children for
throwing stones at our children all'along of poli-
tics, they being for Mr. Stout while our family is
for Mr. Hutcheson.” The teacher declined to
“ whack Smith’s children,” and a complaint was
lodged against him, but he was duly exonerated.
They take their politics rather seriously in Maori-
land.
In Sydney, last week, a particularly brutal
rowdy who had deliberately jumped on a man’s
face when he was down, was severely lectured by
Johnson, S.M., for his fiendishness, and sentenced
to two months’ gaol without the option. “ Subse-
quently, on the intervention of Mr Dalrymple, ”
Johnson, S.M., gave the prisoner the option of a
£5 fine with 21s. costs. The scandal of magistrates
reducing sentences to oblige some irrelevant person
who gets up in the Court requires to be suppressed.
Either the first sentence was altogether wrong, in
which case Johnston doesn’t understand his busi-
ness, or else Johnson is a weak respecter of per-
sons who diverts the course of justice to oblige
Dalrymple, whoever Dalrymple may be.
The two Dederers, recently convicted of
the murder of an Asiatic hawker in a back-block
region of N.S.W., were almost wholly illiterate.
The two boys charged with the murder of Phillips
at Cassilis (N.S.W.) are also almost wdiolly devoid
of education, and if they should be convicted their
case, like that of the Dederers, will be set down
by the usual illogical parson to “ our Godless sys-
tem of education.” Such cases always are so set
down, and herein the parson’s want of logic is
more than usually glaring. The apparent trouble
in the Dederers’ case was that they had missed the
blessings of Godless education —to all intents and
purposes they had no education at all—and if it
was their illiteracy which led them to crime then
the inference is that a reasonable amount of God-
less education would have saved them.
A. Heron-Wilson, M.L.C., of Queensland,
on a recent visit to Sydney, was interviewed by
the Herald on the subject of Federation. The
following is an extract from the hyphenated one’s
account of how the Brisbane Government feels on
the subject:—
The bulk of the Queensland people are not in favor of
Federation. . . . Queensland knows what she has
attained through her independence, and fears that by
Federation she may give up a great deal of that inde-
pendence. . . . With regard to Central and North
Queensland there is no doubt a number of people would
go for Federation on any ground to enable them to get
the separation from Southern Queensland they have been
asking for so long.
The beautiful logic of it. South Queensland is
against Federation lest it should lose part of its
“ independence.” Its independence consists
largely in refusing Central and North Queensland
their independence. Brisbane politicians so love
and value independence that they are willing to
burst up United Australia to get it. Yet when
the North asks that it should also have this in-
alienable blessing of liberty the answer is that
Brisbane will see it dashed first. Evidently the
liberty which the South is so afraid to sacrifice is
simply the liberty to refuse liberty to the North.
Consequently the South is against Federation
because it wants independence, and the North is
in favor of it because it also wants independence
—of a different kind.
Brisbane (Q.) Telegraph complains that
the term “Westralia” is “newspaper patois.”
Yes; it is Bulletinese. And it is “going to
stick,” just as “ Maoriland ” has done for the
islands awkwardly christened “New Zealand,”
and as “ province ” has done in place of the Hing-
lish grotesquerie, “colony.”
Yic. Civil Service numbered
25,981 employes in 1891, and only
20,489 in 1896. The number of
State school teachers has increased,
but in every other department there
has been a huge decrease.
A DAY OF MOURNING.
“ If my first wife had lived, we'd have been
married twenty years to-day .”
“ My dear, you cannot deplore her death more than I do!”
Vic. Legislative Council, with its usual
desire to monkey with things for no visible reason,
exempted butter-factories from the operation of
the Factories Act. The butter-factory employe is
probably the hardest-worked and worst-paid toiler
in the province, and strange stories are in circula-
tion about the foul and germy places wherein
Victoria’s glorious cow-fat is constructed. By all
accounts, the butter-factory wants regular inspec-
tion quite as much as any other industry, but
Sargood and Job Ham apparently like their bull-
grease dirty rather than otherwise.
O! SOLITUDE WHERE, &c.
No. 1. — African Explorer (soliloquising):
Well, here I am in the pathless jungle,
where very likely the foot of man has
never trod before !”
The present N.S.W. Customs and Excise
Act provides for a protective duty on beer; the
local article pays 3d. a gallon while the imported
brown ruin is charged 6d., so the Protection
amounts to 3d. a gallon. This matter has often
been ventilated, yet there is no stated intention
of amending or repealing that Act. The doctrine
of Freetrade seems fuddled on the question of beer.
If the Westralian railway authorities can’t
run the railways to the satisfaction of the public
and prevent numerous accidents, they can cer-
tainly lay claim to being the inventors of a most
novel system of settling claims against the depart-
ment. If an importer of pdU de foie gras is a case
short and makes a claim for his loss, the railway
authorities, having a large stock of chairs on hand,
save any bother with the pdtt de foie gras man by
handing over a couple of chairs to satisfy his claim.
The chair man in due time discovers he is short
and makes a claim which is duly settled with a
cheese or two, and the cheese man is pacified with
a present of somebody’s lost luggage, and so on.
Now that P.M.G. Duffy has started on a
six weeks’ trip to Maoriland, a Vic. Cabinet
committee will “look into the administration of
the Postal Department.” The committee must
prepare itself for shocking sights and awful
smells.
Still the cry goes up in the Maoriland
Tory press that “the days of Sed-
donism are numbered.” Consider-
ing that the Stagnant Party’s
minority now looks like 15, it has
evidently learned to be thankful
for very small mercies.
In Tasmania, since the retrenchment
disease set in, the same man fills many parts. The
Usher of the Black Rod is also Clerk Assistant to
the Leg. Council and secretary to the Minister of
Lands, and follows the musical profession in his
spare time. Another man is associate to all the
judges, Curator of Intestate Estates, chief clerk in
Bankruptcy, and a boss pedestrian as well. As even
these economies failed to square the finances, the
Braddon-Fysh Gov. knocked off taking the Age
in the Parliamentary reading-room on the score of
economy.
No. 2.—800 k Canvasser (springing apparently
out of the ground) : “ Allow me to take your
name for ‘ The Complete African Traveller,’
now appearing in monthly parts and sold
only by subscription, containing maps, sur-
vey-charts, portraits of explorers with a
record of their discoveries, views of principal
cities, and a in short, it is a complete and
exhaustive guide-book to every square inch of
the Dark Continent. It is to run through
seventy-five parts, but as it is to be paid for
on delivery you won’t feel it. What name,
please ?”

hold on to the Premiership ; the followers don’t
want him to hold on because of their love for him
but “the party” is afraid to risk what might
happen if he resigned Who is to succeed him?
l ozer ? Tozer is disliked and distrusted by nearly
all sections, and hated and abhorred by the Labor-

Labor voters. Tommy Byrnes ? He is disliked
by many, and has been “ chucked ” by the metro-
pohs ; he is regarded as an upstart and dicta-
tonal, and his anti-gamblmg bigotry has classed
him with the hypocrites. Also, with Nelson
away, Byrnes and Tozer, according to nresent
hghts would be flying at each oth*er?td
For the rest, there is a choice of “impossibles”
or noodles Nelson himself has filled present
ambitions, has wealth enough to « see him out ’’
and wants to “quit work.” He would like the
Presidency of the Council, but Palmer wont

During a recent all-night sitting in Tas.
Assembly, Attorney-General Clark took off his
boots and dreamed in his socks on a bench, and
Gill, M.H.A., snored heavily beside him. Some-
one called the chairman’s attention to the row,
but he refused to interfere. “ The. remarks the
hon. member who is asleep,” he said, tartly, are
quite as much to the point as those of the hon.
member who unfortunately remains awake.”
Fully £288,000 has the Q. Govt, spent in
trying to make Terrible Townsville a port, and at
present one arm of the breakwater is in ruins and
passengers are placed on coasting steamers by
tenders, barges, punts, and similar primitive appli-
ances. And poor little Bowen, with its glorious
harbour, the true port of the North, is peacefully
slumbering a few leagues down the coast, waiting,
waiting, waiting for the death of the log-rollers or
the Trump of Judgment.
Premier Reid has been again gazetted as
Solicitor-General of New South Wales, in the
absence of Attorney-General Want, who is some-
where in Tasmania or Maoriland. But Solicitor-
General Reid is also absent in Victoria, where, it
is said, he proposes remaining some time. In the
absence of both the A.-G. and his understudy, who
refuses to prosecute the influential offender or
pursues Crick with a warrant ? Does the alleged
offender wait until Jack Want is done fishing, or
what happens ?
In the stormy days down south, when the
Vic. revenue grew shorter every half-year, the
Sydney morning journals pointed to each decline
as another of the inevitable results of Protection.
Now Victoria has a big increase in revenue while
N.S.W. has a heavy fall, and the same papers say
that but for Protection the Vic. increase would be
much larger than it is, and that the way the pro-
vince bears up against the evils of a nefarious
fiscal system is really wonderful.
Opponents of Vic. Factories and Shops
Act, including even some local employes, are
raising a doleful prognostication that wages will
be brought down because more hands will be
needed. The fallacy of such ideas appears when
it is remembered that every extra employe required
tends to raise the wage for the whole body by
making men scarcer. Within certain limits the
more work is got out of the average toiler the
lower his wages will be; if he does two men’s
work then he leaves the other man unemployed,
and the other man is driven by necessity to un-
dersell him for the sake of employment.
Only a few people remarked that the
energy died suddenly out of the Vic. postal enquiry
just when some revelations about a certain secret
society seemed imminent. Experience shows that
the Ancient Buffalo or Cheerful Cherokee in office
generally helps on his fellow Buffalo, and lends a
willing ear to his complaints, and winks at his
misdeeds, and supports him through thick and
thin against the man who isn’t a Buffalo or a
Rechabite, or whatever it may be. Yet this
aspect of the matter is seldom inquired into,
probably because the Order is so widespread that
the inquiry as often as not is conducted
by some of its members.
The Victorian idea of levying a
duty on imported coal has roused the
Sydney Freetrade press to ask with indig-
nation if this sort of thing promises well
for Federation. The Bulletin reckons
that it does, if the proposal is only carried
out. Supposing Victoria levies a duty of
2s. 6d. a ton on coal which is N.S. W.’s
chief export to the adjacent province,
then N.S.W. has an inducement towards
Federation worth about <£loo,ooo a year,
seeing that Federation will abolish all
interprovincial duties. If Victoria doesn’t
levy the aforesaid duty, then the induce-
ment is entirely absent. The one thing
that would do more for Federation and
unity and brotherhood than a 2s. 6d. duty
on coal is a ss. duty. Also, as a means
of pushing along the great cause of nation-
hood a 7s. 6d. duty would have valuable
points about it.
Even the Vic. House of 48 Rich
Landlords moves a little now and then.
It amended at least half the valuable pro-
visions of the Companies Bill out of exist-
ence, but the clause prohibiting directors
selling their own properties to their own
companies got through. Considering that
a whole lot of the most awful boom
“banks” were simply formed to “take
over ” the boss director’s property at his
own valuation—the boss director conduct-
ing . the negotiations with himself and
paying himself out of the depositors’
money—this at least helps to close up
one huge swindle. Twelve months ago a similar
proposition was put before the House of Sar-
good and Cornelius Job Ham, and bounced out
with ineffable scorn. Turner’s policy of pegging
away with Job-like patience isn’t wholly without
its reward.

hcation The title of the book will be ‘Vive-

Charles Hardie Buzacott, formerly of Rock-
hampton (Q.) Bulletin, Brisbane Courier, and
half-a-dozen more white and other colored ele-
phants, has now become part owner of Rockhamp-
ton Argus, and has changed the name of that
Ttf " and " SO d P ap f, r to the d aily Record.
1 he Record now goes in all it is worth for Central
Queensland separation, and its remarks concerning
South and North Queensland are about equally
forcible. Considering how much Buzacott must
have learned concerning the many boodle Govern-
ments of Brisbane during his sojourn on the
Courier, and also considering how many things
he judiciously didn’t mention concerning these
Governments in his Courier days, he should have
a lot of awful truths to relate now that he has trot
away from under the Ministerial shadow.
A policeman may look a large, imposing
mass of brawn and a tower of strength in an
emergency, but in some circumstances he is as help-
less as a child. About 1 a.m. the other morning a
Melb. publican s wife had a disagreement with the
old man and speedily reduced him to subjection
with a stick. There were only the two of them in
the house, so when he was suppressed she had
matters m her own hands. Then she solemnly
took off all her clothes, and, going into the bar
proceeded to smash the stock-in-trade generally!
Ihe husband fetched a policeman and gave him
the needful instructions, and the officer marched
into the ruined premises to take the devastator
into custody. He took in the situation and fled
covered with blushes, and flatly refused to go in
again. Then for 20 minutes or so the constable
stayed outside, and implored Mrs. Public-house
through the keyhole, to dress herself like a good!
reasonable woman, and come out and be arrested ;
and the lady laughed and smashed things joyously
and swam in grog. Finally, an elderly married
constable was procured, and he ran in backwards,
carrying a counterpane, and effected the capture!
If that married constable hadn’t been available it
would probably have been necessary to swear in a
posse of special policewomen for the occasion, or
else to lock up the bar and starve the lady into
her clothes and a more moderate frame of mind.
Apropos Butler, is there any real reason
why a Sydney detective should be sent to London
to obtain extradition papers in the case, or is he
simply having a fly-round for the good of his
health ? Some years ago a- N.S. Wales public
servant cleared to ’Frisco by sailing-ship with 800
sovereigns, proceeds of a cheque which he had
been given to change. By the first American
mail-steamer detective “ Joe ” Camphin was sent
to intercept the fugitive. Joe arrived at the
Golden Gate many days before the sailing craft,
and captured the levanter without any of the elabo-
rate British frill adopted in the present instance.
Another instance, more-or-less to the same effect:
Many years ago, when Thackambau reigned in
dusky glory in Fiji, a Melbourne man, of Hebrew
origin, fled from that city, leaving many heavy
creditors lamenting. Fiji was then the refuge of
such folk, and Melb. merchants determined to stop
the game. They chartered a special steamer —the
Balclutha^—and sent over private-detective Otto
Berliner, with a Victorian warrant, backed by
Chief Justice Stawell. Berliner waited upon the
king (most of the white people sided with the de-
faulter, and rendered no help), and, after a lot of
persuasion, got him to “back the warrant” in
certain royal hieroglyphics. Armed with this
document, Berliner hunted amongst the islands
until he found his man. A certain amount of
loose shooting ensued, but, aided by a couple of
half-naked native constables, the ’tec got his man
to Levuka, then on board the steamer, and brought
him back triumphantly. The delinquent went to
Pentridge for a long term, and intending ab-
sconders had to look for another haven.

dose of alcohol has sometimes sent me to sleep. A

and all preparations of opium, ditto. Subcutaneous in-

Re other physiological eccentricities : I know a patient on

Wooden legs somehow do run in families.
In the British Medical Journal (Sept. 26) Dr.
Spurway gives the fracture record of a single
family in Tring, Herts. J. C. (great-grandfather)
broke his ribs. T. C. (grandfather), between 2£
and 16 years old, broke his right thighbone once,
both bones of both forearms once, both bones of
left shin twice, both bones of right shin three
times—ls fractured bones in less than 14 years ; a
few years back a cow gouged his left eye out, but
bar that and a slight twist of one arm he is, at 65
years, hale and hearty. J. C. (father) up to date
has broken both bones of both forearms, both
bones of both shins, both thighbones (10 fractures)
and dislocated an elbow. Of his six children,
four have gone in for the fracture business up to
date. M. C. (16 years) has broken both bones of
right arm in two places, both bones of left forearm,
ami left upper arm in two places—B breaks. H. C.
(15 years) scores fractures on both thighbones,
upper arm and a toe—4 breaks. H. 0. (12 years)
records a broken collarbone only, apparently
having not yet taken to the business seriously.
E. C. (a 14-year-old girl) is top-notcher of this
generation, having broken both bones of both fore-
arms, both collarbones, both thighbones, left
upper arm twice, right upper arm once—ll
broken bones. If she lives to the allotted three-
score and ten she should score 55 fractures, and
certainly should have a memorial tombstone
erected by the local surgeons—unless she is a lodge
patient.

drought ever known.”

It was on the Warrego and they were talk-
ing of rain—they had just had lin. 30 points in a
quarter of an hour, but the man from Carpentaria
was equal to the occasion :—“ When I was out
Thursday Island way,” he said, “ several of us
were out in a boat when it came on to rain. It
took three of us to keep the boat baled, and in less
than 10 minutes you could get a pannikin of fresh
water from the top of the salt.”

A travelling Australian to The Bulletin :
The Anglican parson, or some of him, seems to have a
real good time in this world. I stayed nearly five weeks
at the lake of Lucerne, and a fortnight at the Lago
Maggiore, and used the lake steamers nearly every day.
It was never my luck to travel on them without meeting
C. of E. parsons. Often there were half-a-dozen aboard.
At Stresa, about the prettiest place on Lago Maggiore, I
counted at the best hotel (they always go to
the best hotels) one short of a dozen. There were al-
ways two or three in the train. Well-clad, well-fed, and a
darned good hamper whenever the train didn’t stop long
enough to get a square feed at a station. And apparently
not one of these fire-escapes was a teetotaller. Now, I
hope lam not an irreverent man—l have all my life tried
to believe in something, and, being unable myself to find
a soul-satisfying creed, envy even the superstitious street
beggars whom you see prostrating themselves before the
stone saints in the cathedrals. The smug, matter-of-fact
worldliness of these clergymen astounds me. The audacity
with which they wallow in luxuries and cast to the winds
their Saviour’s doctrine of self-sacrifice is absolutely won-
drous. If I could only believe as they profess to believe,
how gladly would I try to live on apples and maccaroni,
and how small would be my concern about the shiny
seams of my old coat! Great God ! what a field is there
everywhere in the world for the man who Believes and is
therefore prepared to work for others without thought of
the morrow !
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, I ancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-nnle
long.*
When chloroform was first brought into
British surgical practice fifty years ago, an en-
lightened clergy protested against its use in mid-
wifery cases, as being an attempt to evade God’s
curse upon all daughters of Eve. According to
the good book (which is the secret of Hingland’s
greatness) a woman is doomed to take her interest-
ing events as they come, and every shriek uttered
under the circumstances confirms the terewtb of
the Scriptures, even as a rainbow in the sky
proves beyond all doubt that the Lord gave Noah
a promise not to drown the world any more. This
was quite clear to the parsons and ladies of 1846,
consequently chloroform for child-birth sufferers
had to be justified by some sort of antidotal quo-
tation from the Book. Dr. Simpson reminded the
church that Adam was put to sleep by his Creator
prior to the removal of his historic rib, and al-
though this was rather irrelevant reasoning,
seeing that Eve wasn’t apparently put to
sleep when little Cain became due, it suf-
ficed to satisfy religious scruples in general.
Or, rather, it gave the hardshell believers
pause until their Gracious Queen settled the
question by graciously consenting to be anaes-
thetised at the birth of number eight baby. Then
the archbishops and bishops felt sure that God
doesn’t care whether a female on the verge of
maternity has a dreadful time or otherwise.

Factors of Longevity.
Can one predict the probable duration of life?
Inherited tendencies, habits, occupation, obser-
vance of sanitary and hygienic laws, and care to
promptly avert the first approach of symptoms of
disease, have a direct bearing. A long and active
life is attainable to most of us. If the liver shows
signs of torpidity, or the kidneys are allowed to
become weak, no time should be lost. Warner’s
Safe Cure has proven curative even in advanced
so-called “ incurable cases.” If used at the incep-
tion and approach of the disease, a few doses
effects a cure, enlivens the liver, checks the diffu-
sion of bile, and stops the irritation of the kidneys.
This is not alone the claim of the vendors. It is
the testimony of those around you who have tried
it and who know. Mrs. C. W. Richards, in a
letter dated Dec. 5, 1896, Hotham Hill, Murphy-
st., Melbourne North, writes : “ Some time ago I
suffered from jaundice and liver trouble. Having
consulted medical men and tried several remedies
without success, I made up my mind from what I
had heard about Warner’s Safe Cure to give it a
trial, and am pleased to say that after taking
three bottles I was completely cured, and have
much pleasure in recommending Warner’s Safe
Cure to any one suffering as I did.”*

The heroes of the cricket-match—S.A. v.
N.S.W. —won among them 218nobblers of Wolfe’s
Schnapps—the proprietors of that noble beverage
having offered 6d. per run for all over 50 made by
any individual player in an innings. In the re-
sult, Garrett scored the cash equivalent of 81 nob-
blers ; Kelly, 58; Giffen, 54 ; and M‘Kibbin, 25.*
The Boston shower-bath falls like three
inches of rain on* the dusty and undusty, and is no
less grateful to the red pores of the Coolgardian
than to the black ones of the man who voted for
Tommy Byrnes at Warwick. It is the ideal bush
bath, since you merely insert the end of a pipe in
a bucket of water and work the handles and the
machine does the rest, while the water can be
saved in case it is the only bucketful about and
the subject wants another bath to-morrow.*
The Fowler bicyles now on view at the
Australian agency, Messrs. A. Hoffnung’s build-
ings, 171 Pitt-street, Sydney, represent the latest
scientific development in bicycle construction.
The Fowler truss frame is an ingenious innovation
for strengthening the machine while preserving its
lightness ; further strengthening is in the special
reinforcement of the fork crowns. While the
stability and wear of the machine is thus ensured,
its colorings and finish amount to little less than
art. Another important element is the perfection
of the running gear ; the Fowler being called
by its riders the easy-running bicycle. It weighs
just over 201 b., is fitted with Dunlop tyres, costs
£22 10s., and is guaranteed for 12 months. The
Fowler is shown to good advantage at the com-
modious premises at 171 Pitt-street. Here, too,
all bicycle sundries and necessaries are plenteous,
varied, and up to date. One particular welcome
contrivance is the safety oiler which, costing Is.,
will prove a perfect boon to the Australian world
a-wheel.*

At Poverty Point.
Cabled news that the British Govt, is much con-
kerned about a leakage of secrets from the British naval
and military departments didn’t arrive in time to serve
as an advt. for “ One of the Best ” at Melb. Royal. But
the wily Bland will be able to collect some pars on this
subject from the London papers, in readiness for the next
time Cosgrove sells “plans of the fortifications” to the
French agent in the top’at. Meanwhile, the capture of
the unpatriotic person who is revealing things would add
several hundred pounds-worth of topical interest to
Holt’s property.
It is interesting to hear that “ Skipped by the Light of
the Moon ” caught on around the English provinces, ana
that George Walton intends to send two companies out
with it next season. All these years has our old friend
Gourlay been wearing the piece threadbare in Australia,
when he might have Obadiah Dingled to his great profit
beyond the seas. “ Skipped,” when one comes to think
of it, was the first of musical, farcical comedies, horn
somewhat before its time, and never treated here on the
smartest musical comedy system.
Hairy little Zelman, who batons the orchestra at
Flemington races, is said to know more Arabic than any
other man in Australia.
Mahlstick : “Through a compromise with Mrs. Grundy,
some high-dried lady beef is supposed by the Melb. art-
Btudents to be a Trilby.”
Eight performances a week of “ Matsa ” is unprece-
dented in Australian records, but at Drury Lane, London,
the panto, is given 12 times a week.
Melb.: “There is a Sleepy Hollow called the Athenaeum.
Entering it, you find a tableau of Rip Van Winkles, som-
niferous over all the papers approved by Rip Van Winkle-
dom. It is 20 years, or may be 30, since any new paper
came out according to Athenaeum ideas.”
Melba wrote lately to a Melbourne friend lam just
off to America. I may go to South America from May
till August next year, for which I shall receive £40,000
—not bad for an Australian.”
The “ Chinatown ” song,.“ Cynthia, Cynthia, I’ve been
thinking,” has for air an old students’ song, beginning . •
“ Professor Martin was a nice old fellow,
Full of cant and piety.
Till one night he fell a-kissing
Of his servant fair and free.”
The Chinese are solid on the realistic drama. An opera
performed recently in Castlemaine (Vic.) had one scene
wholly devoted to a confinement case, the hero being
born (he had to grow up gradually in the next 63 acts),
with much travail. After the event the happy, heavy
father seized the infant, a fully-dressed wax doll with
long red hair, and waved it triumphantly amid much
Chinese applause.
Honolulu has adorned itself with a big opera house, so
the beautiful city of leprosy and revolution may become a
one-night stand for companies drifting backwards or for-
wards between Australia and San Francisco.
Sims Reeves, according to the latest reports, is timed
to reach Australia next February. The old tenor is
accompanied by an able concert-party who supply all the
music while the veteran remnant steps on the stage and
gains applause for his past achievements.
“ Matsa 1’ will probably be produced in Sydney at
Easter.
Oscar Wilde in “A Woman of No Importance”:
“ What can be more dreadful than the spectacle of an
English squire out fox-hunting ?—the unspeakable racing
after the uneatable.”
“ Matsa ” has no real claim to be called a pantomime.
There isn’t a fairy queen in it.
Fanny Liddiard talks of “ Chinatown ” Hoyt sending a
company out to Australia every year, which is a rather
appalling possibility. One hopes, at any rate, that his
companies will not be invariably described as “ Hoyt’s
Comedians,” as though the boomsome manager had
bought ’em at auction.
THE OLD GIRLS.
Where do the old girls go
That we knew in our younger time?
Are they single or wed, alive or dead?
Or gone to another clime ?
Or are they marching to and fro
In the Xmas pantomime ?
Bland Holt had to negotiate a lot ere he got the Melb.
City Fathers to let him take a nightly lease of Little
Boiirke-street in which to fix up the hunting-scene of
“ For England.” That riotous horse-delirium rages over
the best part of a block, and threatens every night by
misadventure to trample underfoot the local Chow and
his Maly Ann.
Melb. Aleck stages the “Temptations of a Nurse Girl.”
Reminds one of Baron Von Mueller’s burst of sympathy :
“ Dem poor nurz gurls in de Vidzroy Gardens, mid de
berrambuladors, and all dem naged statoots aboud.
Pantomime being apparently extinct in Melb., someone
has been rummaging the books of the Royal to find the
subjects done there. They include “Aladdin,” “Arabian
Nights,” “ Babes in the Wood,” “ Cinderella,” “ En-
chanted Island,” “Forty Thieves,” “ Froggee would a-
Wooing Go,” “Giant Killer,” “ Golden Island,” “ Goody
Two Shoes,” “Gulliver,” “ Hey, Diddle Diddle,” “ House
that Jack Built,” “ Humpty Dumpty,” “ Jack Horner,”
“ Lalla Rookh,” “ Magician’s Daughter,” “ Riquet,
“ Robin Hood,” “ Robinson Crusoe,” “ Sinbad,” “ Sleeping
Beauty,” “Tom the Piper’s Son,” “Twinkle, little Star,”
and “ Dick Whittington.”
N. : “ After two nights of ‘ Fun on the Bristol,’ at Melb.
Aleck, a notice on the door said the show was closed
through ‘ the indisposition of Mr. Cosgrove.’ A previous
closure at the Bijou was through ‘ the indisposition of
Miss Luella,’ as a precisely similar notice on the door
stated. However, it seems Miss Luella and Mr. Cosgrove
are not too indisposed to fight it out in the law-courts.
Jennie Lee, playing “Jo” at Drury Lane last year, wore
the same ragged knickerbockers and wielded the same
broom as a quarter-century ago.
Melb. All Nations Carnival, Exhibition, results in £2200
loss. The Hebrews are hit badly, and Attorney-Gen.
Isaacs is the main cause.
Fanny Liddiard returned from America by the Alameda.
Miss Liddiard has a beautiful Yankee accent now, and it
is a pleasure to hear her express through her adorable
little nose how glad she is to be back.
Four theatrical companies have gone smash in Broken
Hill within the past three months.

further, and say she is better than Alice Leamar. A.L. is
vulgarity combined with cleverness—distinct talent, I

aged parts and shows very obtrusively. In this connec-
tion, by the way, what becomes of infant prodigies when
they grow ? ”

Ireland as a violiniste next month at Belfast Philharmonic
concert.
Melb. : “ Will South dead ? R.I.P. He was clever and
straight. I have seen South play a large-toothed Adelphi
villain one night, a juvenile lead the next, sing in bur-
lesque the third, and do Rufus Dawes the fourth. At
E resent the profession suffers too much from the one-part
reed, said one-parter being commonly, from his own limi-
tations, only too apt to .accept sweat-wages —whence
managers of a certain type grow fat, while the earner
thins, and his co-earner gets even more thin, or doesn t
earn at all. Several years’ close intimacy—bar some few
brilliant exceptions—makes the writer think the Eliza-
bethan classing of the actor as ‘ rogue and vagabond
not so far out after all.”
Johnny Sheridan was engaged to play Widow Twankey,
in “ Aladdin,” at one of the London suburban theatres
this Xmas.
The burst-up of “ Fun on the Bristol,” at Melb. Alex-
andra, is said to have been owing to the issue of a writ in
connection with a previous production of the Widdy at
another theatre.
The artistic advance of Carrie Moore (who has been
growing taller all the time since last Xmas) is very notice-
able, in contrast with little Ivy Scott. The latter, being
one of the small and early sort, seems to lose ground as
she gets older.
A hypnotic trance exhibition put up not long ago at
Chester (Eng.) had a bad termination. The professor put
his subject to sleep and announced that he would remain
absolutely unconscious for seven days. As usual, he in-
vited all and sundry to inspect the patient as much as they
liked, and a party of sceptics took up the contract. They
arranged themselves in relays so as to keep a constant
watch on the slumberer night and day, and they played
nap in his room for eight solid hours without him turning
a hair. Then, with the hope of getting at him through
his thirst, they drew sundry corks. Next they sang comic
songs with a view to making him smile, but the subject
was Scotch and showed no sign. Finally they sat round
and told undressed stories to each other, and, towards
the end of the twelfth hour, the sleeper suddenly got up,
and bashed the narrator of the moment on the nose, and
proceeded to sweep the party off the premises. The pro-
fessor burst in just then with a revolver and threatened to
shoot all and sundry, and the subject scrambled hurriedly
back into bed and apparent unconsciousness. Finally a
policeman dragged the committee of inspection off the
professor, and the difficulty ended in the police-court.
Nigger-humorist Horace Bent was once knocked down
by a cab in Castlereagh-street, Sydney, but escaped quite
uninjured. Rising, he ran for four blocks in vain pursuit
of the cab, which was making a hurried retreat. “ You
needn’t be so anxious to have him arrested,” said a friend
when Horace gave up the pursuit; “ you’re not hurt.’
“ I didn’t want to arrest him,” replied the breathless
Bent; “ I only wanted to ask him what he would charge
to take me to the hospital.”
Alma Grey’s sister, Pearl, lately touring N.S.W. as
Topsy in “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” is only nine years old.
Alma herself is seven.
An English dramatic critic suggests that the “legiti-
mate” drama dropped out of fashion along with legiti-
matedrinking. In the palmy days of “Hamlet” people
swilled quarts of stout, port wine and gin and water in
which there were “infinite potentialities of tragic emotion.”
Now they sip claret, and prefer light entertainments.
Mrs. Kendal wrote an insulting letter to a newspaper-
man who had passed remarks on the neglected condition
of her brother, Tom Robertson’s, grave. The postscript
was particularly painful—“ If you publish this letter, send
me a copy of your paper to Royalty Theatre, Glasgow,
where I shall be next week (otherwise I should not see it),
when one of my servants will send stamps for same.—
M.K.
“ The Mikado ” is far and away the most valuable Gil-
bert-Sullivan property. The Jap. opera was lately per-
formed for the thousandth (not consecutive, of course)
time at the Savoy Theatre, London, and immediately
afterwards the right to produe-e it in Italy was sold for a
term of five years. And what a future lies before it 1
Half-a-eentury hence “The Mikado” will convulse
civilized Japan with laughter every evening until further
notice.
Has it ever occurred to the average business manager
that the morning advt. of Such-and-Such Theatre which
chronicles “ Phenomenal success” is an awkward insult
to the show or the public or hitnself or the performers, or
somebody 1 If the show is a good one then the success
isn’t “ phenomenal,” and if it isn’t a good one the B.M.
should have known it beforehand, and the terms of ex-
clamatory surprise in which his Monday advt. is generally
couched are a tacit acknowledgement of his astonishment
that the first-nighters didn’t arise and denounce the
entertainment with much blankiness.
When Lola Montes went to flog Seekamp, a Ballarat
editor, the editor’s wife larrupped Lola. Thk Bulletin is
now collecting stories of actresses who went to horsewhip
the editor and were whaled themselves.
Cockaigne : “ In London there was once a piece called
‘The Manager,’in which the entire ‘behind scenes’ was
revealed to the audience. For a whole hour all the clock-
work and mechanism of a spectacular play is now showed
gratis nightly, in Little Bourke-street, behind the Melb.
Royal, and the wholesale exposure quenches curiosity.”

money is not on.” Of late the outside punters have paid
very little attention to tales that trainer Power didn t
fancy his mount. O’Trigger for the Bagot Handicap, for
instance, was a pretty general public belief, in spite of
the prevalent stable whisper.
* * *
Treason, a sweet filly by Trenton-Sedition, won
a little event at Moonee Valley (Melb.) last week, and one
of the dailies “explained” the sudden improvement on
Treason’s running of the previous week by the fact that
she had then been rather short of work. The filly s lack
of condition accounted for her starting at any price on
that occasion. Seven days later she had “come on to
such an astonishing degree that she went to the post a
red-hot favorite. Treason’s dam, by the way, won a New-
market Handicap.
* * * *
Queensland form must be bad indeed for the
crack sprinter. Archie’s Daughter, after running in-
differently at the Randwick meetings, was unplaced on
Saturday last in a Welter at Canterbury (N.S.W.); whilst
another champion, Babel, who was an utter failure when
in Sydney, has been equally unsuccessful in his Mel-
bourne trip, as he was a good last in a Welter at Flenung-
ton, after running nowhere in the Standish Handicap.
Yet these two animals have carried thumping weights in
watch-breaking times in their own province. So much
for the time-test 1
* * * »
Snapshot, the winner of the Perth Cup, seems
a general-utility kind of animal, as last year, after running
second in the Cup to Inverary, he was pulled out the next
day and carried list. 61b. into second place in the Hurdle
Race.
* » * *
The M.L. two-year-old Gold Medallist (Medal-
lion—Corolla) is evidently “ something superior,” as he
not only easily won the Great Northern Foal Stakes at
Auckland, beating his more fancied stable-mate, the flying
Multiform (Hotchkiss—Formo), but he afterwards suc-
ceeded in winning the valuable Royal Stakes, in which he
defeated the crack three-year-old, St. Paul (St. Leger—
Satanella).

On New Year’s Day two much-fancied candi-
dates for last Melb. Cup were returned winners— viz.,
Damien, at Sydney, and Broken Hill, at Adelaide.
Doubtless when betting commences on next Melb. Cup,
these two will once more help to fill the bags of Mo & Co.
* * * *
Mathematically, the odds are 3699 to 1 against
anyone naming winning double, Newmarket and Austra-
lian Cup—and yet Ikey Mo, in his generosity, offers the
munificent price of 100 to 1. It is nearly that odds against
anyone, at this stage of the game, spotting the winner of
the Newmarket alone, nevertheless the fatuous punter
helps to fill the bag by backing his fancies at the above-
mentioned ridiculous price. No wonder Ikey drinks
“ fizz ” whilst the punter contents himself with tangle-
foot, when things run to it.
* * * *
That many a boundary-rider and stockman be-
strides an undiscovered Oup-winner has ofttimes been
said, and, to give support to the saying, every now and
then a red-hot provincial is sprung on the metropolis.
The latest wonder brought to light is Merloolas (pedigree
unknown), who recently won the double at Charters
Towers, defeating, amongst others, the sensational V.R.C.
Leger winner Cabin Boy. His party has entered the
aged gelding for Newmarket and Aus. Cup, so it will soon
be seen whether he is a second Bungebah or a fraud.

whispered, The Skipper journeys on to India, she will
have all her work cut out to win next year.

At a Vic. suburban race-meeting an amateur
iockev with more than the usual want of amateur ability
anded a nice little bet with a prominent cash bookmaker
over the race immediately preceding the hunters steeple-
chase in which the amateur was to ride. On applying to
the Man of Yells for his money, he was asked to wait for
it until after the next race. The punter consented
readily enough, the bookie being rich and solid “ Wot
did you balance him for, Ikey?” asked an astonished
brother in bags, when the creditor was out of hearing
"strike me, Sammy; he’s going to ride in the next and
might get killed. He can’t ride,” replied the long-nosed,
long-headed gentleman of odds.

Somebody remarked that £ £ Smith’s win with
Argent at Flemington ought to be recorded as an item of
financial news. £ £, whose colors had almost dropped
out of recollection, put £4OO on his horse, and when the
coup duly came off, £ £’s hat flew into the air. Rejoicing
wasn’t the word for £ £’s demonstration over his own
victory. He fairly jubilated.
» * « *
The Wellington Park (M.L.) yearling sale this
year was a very successful one. Twenty-five youngsters
fetched 2725 guineas, thus averaging 109 guineas. A full
brother to Daystar (Castor— Cissy) topped the list, being
sold for 440 guineas, but perhaps the best bargain was
secured by Mr. Mossman, of Queensland, who bought the
brown filly by Cuirassier—Ouida (dam of Strathmore,
Hazel, etc.) for 290 guineas. She should turn-out a flyer.
“ Donovan delighted Australia ” ran the words
of a London cablegram, intending to indicate that the
first three horses in a recent race were Donovan, De-
lighted, and Australia. A sagacious Australian cable-
interpreter padded it out to —“ O’Donovan Rossa is
delighted with the manner in which the Irish Home Rule
proposals have been received in Australia.”
• * * *
The editor of one Australian sporting paper
habitually climbs on top of a horse from the wrong side.
The editor of another once explained at great length to
his readers where and how to shoot lyrebirds at a time
when these birds were protected under a heavy penalty.
* * * *
Snipping from a Melb. report of the Vic. v.
S.A. match —“Just as Harry went in there was a rumble
of thunder, and rain commenced to fall, and as the
thunder increased and a rain storm was impending, they
left the field for a time. The rain proved to be but light,
and did not last more than five minutes, but for all that,
the cricketers remained for over twenty minutes in the
shelter of the pavilion. When they did take the field
again they had only ten minutes play before the luncheon
interval.” What fun for the spectators !

* * *
At the recent Melb. cricket-match there was a sud-
den and deep pause ; the band played, the game stopped,
and every groveller that wasn’t bald uncovered. A ton of
pins falling could have been heard. Brassey had arrived.
Then a wretch with no soul and a trumpet-voice roared
out loud enough to be heard outside the ground : “ What
rot this is. Play on.” They did.

* * » *
All this newspaper talk re Capting Trott’s
action in the matter of the “follow-on” rule is very tire-
some. The rule was there, anyway, and Trott served two
cricket purposes by taking advantage of it. He saved his
side from the possibility of being beaten by the weather,
and he ensured a couple of innings for each side, which
means a long game. The great aim of interprovincial
cricket is a long game. Outside of the newspapers there
is very little furious rivalry between teams whose leading
players have been members of the same Australian Eleven.
* * • •
The S.A.-N.S.W. cricket-match, played at
Sydney, was won by N.S.W. in one innings, with 11 runs
to spare. S.A. went in first and shaped very badly
against Howell’s bowling, making only 159 on a perfect
wicket. Next day, Saturday, the big audience of 15,000
people helped to brighten the play, and N.S.W. piled on
runs till the last man retired at 420—though Jones’s
thunderbolts had commenced the innings with 4 wickets
for 9. Garrett and Kelly were the big N.S.W. scorers,
with 131 and 108, and M’Kibbin shaped well for a useful 75.
S.A. did a little better in tbe second innings,
but not much ; and the 250 scored were not sufficient to
send the N.S.W. batsmen out again. In spite of a lame
leg, Giffen managed 104, but, taken all round, the crow-
eaters gave an unsatisfactory exhibition, and Jones was
the only man who showed something like first-class form
in any branch of the game. The present makes the fifth
consecutive win for N.S.W.
* * * *
The veteran cricketer, Tom Garrett, has taken a
hand in more interprovincial matches than any other
player, past or present. The last match played by
N.S.W. against Victoria completed his 31st appearance as
a representative of the former province. He thus beat
Blackham by one match in first-class interprovincial
cricket. Tom, like his sturdy old father, takes a lot
before “ getting knocked out.”

Vic. Chief Sec. Peacock distinguished himself
on Boxing Day by getting on the track While the Chines
bicycle race was proceeding. One of the competitors hit
Peacock with a bang that made him think the united
factory proprietors had fallen upon him, and the cvclfst
and the politician skidded along the track together , ? n a
shrieking heap of dilapidation. Peacock disenga-ed him.
self and arose with his tattered coat-tails and sundry
spikes tied weirdly round his head and laughed his awful
kookaburra laugh, and there was an immediate rush o
competitors to mend what they imagined were a series
of blown-out tyres.

* * * *
There is some talk of having a Bicycle Casualty
Ward opened in connection with Melb. Hospital Bikes
are the cause of more than half of the street accidents
that happen in Melb. nowadays. It is about time that
an Act was passed by the Parliaments of the provinces
declaring the bike to be a lethal instrument.

Boidi’s estimate of Porta’s present form makes
no allowance for the young man’s improvement during

* * *
Boxing, which appeared to be on the up-grade
in Frisco, has had a sad set-back over the Fitzsinimons-
Sharkey affair. From the accounts of the fight it
appears Fitz. played with the sailor for seven rounds, and
then sailed in during the eighth and knocked him out with
a left hook on the jaw and a right body blow, whereupon
the referee gave the fight to Sharkey on a foul. The
matter was at latest in the law courts, Fitz’s party having
stopped payment of the certified cheque for £2OOO which
represented the stakes. Australian Billy Smith, who
trained Sharkey, turned “ dog ” on his party and gave
some very interesting evidence in the courts, which, if
true, shows the whole affair to have been a conspiracy to
rob the auburu-haired one of the stakes. Fitzsimmons’
"'ail of “ I have been robbed,” when the referee gave the
fight to Sharkey, is stated to have been most pathetic, as
anyone who remembers Bob’s struggles in Sydney for
anything from a crown upwards may imagine.
Billy Smith swore in court that Sharkey told
him after the fight that Fitz. could beat Jim Corbett in a
couple of rounds, as Fitz. could “hit like the kick of a
mule.”
* * * *
On Saturday M'Murtrie’s 22-footer, Effie, con-
tinued her winning career by annexing the Johnstone’s
Bay Sailing Club's 22-footer race, off scratch. Effie
started with a small suit of sails, but even that handicap
could not hold her back, and she made the third of an
unbroken list of wins. Some score of years ago Joe
Donnelly built for Mr. S. H. Hyam a 22-foot skiff, named
the Effie, but better known as the flat-iron, which com-
pletely ran away from everything of her class, or out of
it, and it looks much as if Joe’s last creation is going
to take after the old-time craft in performances as well
as name.
* * * •
Wray, the sculler who went to England with
Stanbury, was an unsuccessful competitor in a sculling
handicap on the Tyne, won by W. Haines. The johnny-
know-alls used to say that Wray was very little, if any,
behind Stanbury .as a sculler, and as Wray has now
challenged Harding to row for £2OO a-side on the Thames,
there is a chance of learning how good he really is.
* * * *
In England they are disinclined to credit
Roseingrave’s Australian jump-records. The young man,
before he left ’Ome, was reputed to have done big things
in the wilds of Connemara, yet could not come near them
even in Dublin. In Melbourne, he could not do nearly as
well as in Sydney, and in Sydney he did nothing very
extraordinary. Roseingrave’s biggest jumps appear to be
done in remote places where no one in particular is about,
and they are not so much seen as heard about afterwards.
When Happy William and Converted Jane
give forth their “testimony ” at the street corner,
most people take it for the spontaneous outbreak
of the soul. It looks like that, but it isn’t. The
testimony, in most cases, is elaborately and pain-
fully rehearsed beforehand (the writer lives next
door to a tambourine temple and speaks with
authority). Far into the night sometimes, in that
house of din next door, does Converted Jane
struggle with her part without salary or liquid
refreshment, making alterations and additions at
the bidding of the stage-manager, adding lurid
experiences, and working up her attitudes and
her tears and the set of her poke bonnet and her
hair. It is a hard, nigger-driving contract, and
by the time the wretched, ill-fed Jane has got her
little tawdry speech all right she is ready to drop
from sheer exhaustion, and is almost inaudible.
Even the sewing woman or the waitress hasn’t a
much harder life than Jane when Bhe is in her
novitiate.

Inarticulate, bubbled in gasps of death.
S® S *P ope , d down lower ; no sounds came now,
Hut the blood-foam lay on his cheeks and brow.
Macalister’s camp lay by north and west:
was a four hours’ ride to a man hard-pressed.
wn thr f, e hours ’ sun had not fired the air
There b blood-branded came staggering
The horizon was nigh, for the noontide glowed
hen Macalister’s party for Murgill rode.
But the black creek timber showed over the plain
xtre Macalister tightened his bridle-rein
And looked out hard on a spot that he knew
W here the pme-ridge bristled against the blue.
Then, or ever a man of them turned or spoke
1 here crept through the pine-trees a wisp of smoke.
Kach thought of a woman, what fate was hers ?
And handled his rifle, and fleshed his spurs.
Through the crashing scrub, up the clogging sand,
Ihey rode like devils with heel and hand.
Through the choking smoke and the fire-drift hot
Ihey flashed like madmen who heeded not.
On the ashes of hope, in the grip of fate,
Ihey halted like mortals, just all too late.
There were wild, swift seekings through crackling
flames,
There were anguished shoutings of tender names.
When the mother and girls were together laid
There were deep oaths pledged to be fully paid.
The boy stood out in the blackened ring
And the men looked down on him shuddering.
His father’s blood on his brow was dark
As a fire-brand’s blaze on a sapling’s bark.
And his lips were sealed as by deadliest drouth
With the kiss of his mother’s fire-charred mouth.
Baptised in blood and confirmed by fire,
His whole soul dark with one fell desire.
Two days passed by ere they rode again,
The boy in the van of a grisly train.
But no books tell us what then befel,
For the tale of that riding were bad to tell.
And bad and sad is the whole tale known
Of the boy who led it from shout to groan.
Of the boy and the man as he still appears
In the reddest page of the Gulf’s worst years.
They blacken him still in their cackling talk—
The pitiful, preaching new-chum folk.
But some men listen to all they say,
And stand by him still in the back-block way.
“ For I say,” says Macalister, bursting through
All the babble and prate of the half-baked crew,
“ That if ever a mandate to be obeyed
On the soul of a mortal man was laid
“ ’Twas on him, and ’twas sealed in his life’s
eclipse,
On his father’s blood, by his mother’s lips.
“Those black, scorched lips, could he feel their
kiss,
And ever his life’s sole purpose miss ?

The days of the prophets ye preach about,
“ His oath would have held till his course was run
And his hate kept hot, as it ought to have done.”
FRANCIS MYERS.
Earthworms are proved by experiment to
carry up tubercle bacilli from buried tuberculosed
flesh. Tuberculous meat was buried at the bottom
of a deep flower-pot, in which several earthworms
were kept. Six months later the worms were in-
oculated into guinea-pigs, which promptly suc-
cumbed to tubercle. The bodies of dead worms
also contained bacilli abundantly. Another reason
for cremation.
The recent wreck of the Delmira off the
Maoriland coast should give another fillip to
nautical superstition. The vessel was originally a
Spanish slave-trader, and on nearly every voyage
in her later years she encountered bad luck of
some sort. Last trip but one the Delmira was
months overdue, the skipper’s wife and the mate
were seized with typhoid, the crew took scurvy,
provisions ran short, and finally a serious breach
occurred between skipper and men. The sale of
the old slaver’s bones realised exactly one guinea.
The “ cocaine bug ” or “ cocaine worm ”
otherwise a hallucination in cocaine-taking cases,
which makes the expert think he is full of
worms which crawl out of the pores of his skin
and out of clothes worn by himself and others
(but, curiously, not out of freshly-laundered clothes
or clothes not in use) —must now be supplemented
by “ Magnan’s symptom,” a symptom in advanced
cocainism in which the sufferer fancies that his
skin is full of sand, crystals, or more or less
rounded pebbles, all of which are continuously on
the move. Medical cocainists fancy they are full of
wriggling microbes. Even continued use of cocaine*
dressings in surgical cases may produce these'
horrors.
Dear Bulletin, — In reply to “ X.Z.,” who
asks whether cannibalism survived in Scotland 1
till the days of Queen Bess, etc. After the English
invasion of the 14th century, the inhabitants were
reduced to such straits that cannibalism became
rife. In the commencement of the 17th century
the cannibals were exterminated barring only the-
notorious human-flesh-eating family of Sawney
Baine, consisting of eight sons, six daughters, and
32 grandchildren, who lived on the coast of
Galloway. They were all wiped out by the gory
arm of the law save one infant girl. But before
she reached her twelfth year the inherited craving
for man-meat broke out, and having ferociously
bitten a boy playfellow and eaten the piece ex-
tracted, she was burnt to death for the crime of
cannibalism. In the forests of Brazil the descen-
dants of the deserters and stragglers of the old
Spanish conquerors are eating human flesh to this
day. — Bony.

-Now that doubts are expressed whether
the supply of india-rubber can keep up with the
manufacture of pneumatic tyres, Australian
Govts. would do well to foster the cultivation of
the caoutchouc, or india-rubber tree. Much of
the world s india-rubber is derived from Borneo,
and experiments have already proved that the
plant will thrive in the warmer parts of Australia.
North Queensland would lend itself specially to
the industry; so would most of the Northern
.Territory and Northern Westralia. There are
immense possibilities in the business which should
be worth all the profits of the struggling butter-
trade twice over.

A religious paper reports that “Some-
where on the west coast of Africa there stands to-
day a native Kerristian church (erected by the
converted blacks at a cost of .£2000) close to the
site of a heathen temple which had walls mainly
composed of human skulls.” How very sad ! The
owners of all those skulls were perhaps honest
wooden-god-fearing creatures who went to Heaven.
And their descendants, for certain, are mean,
psalm-singing crawlers, who, having only an ir-
rational “ belief ” to set off against the heavy
debit of civilised vices that follow in the Bible’s
track, will probably go—where missionaries go.
Dear Bulletin, —Being connected with an
Italian firm of standing, I have necessarily to
give what assistance I can in the way of explain-
ing any little matters which may not be perfectly
clear to the understanding of the Italian com-
munity here. The following will show one
side of the business in the matter of the registra-
tion of birth of an Italian child: —
Copy of Official Memorandum from Registrar of Births
and Deaths. (Exact transcription):—
x O.H.M.S.
No. 1/b. District Registry Office,
7th July, 1896.
Memorandum
from District Registrar at
To Guiseppe .
Sir, —
I have to invite your attention
to the fact that either the particulars of the Certificate
of Birth received regarding your son Alberta or those of
your “previous issue” are most erroneous, and to the
penalty for a false statement see last clause of forms of
Certif. of Birth (£lOO and 3 years’ gaol).
In 1892 you and your wife are described as 29 twenty-
nine years of age. Your wife’s maiden name Gorge (in
reality the wife’s name was Giagi.)
In 1894 your ages are both 30 thirty years
her name Gaigan.
In 1896 your ages are both 31 thirty-one years
her name Gallagher.
In 1892 your marriage is dated 1888.
In 1894 „ 1886.
In 1896 „ „ „ ~ 1888.
Those facts speak for themselves, and prove either gross
negligence or wilful falsification.
In last certificate tendered the ages of previous issue
are not given. I have written Angelina 7 years, Emilio
2 years.
If that statement—your own ages as 31 years each —or
any other statement made in the certificate of birth of
your son Alberta be incorrect, then be good enough to
fill up the enclosed “ amended ” statement correctly,
making the information, if correct, of your birthplace
Walli di Signori and Josnelo, and place of marriage
Sorbo biano, as it will save too much alteration in my
register, as those particulars are also differently given in
previous information, but may be only a variation of
geographical description, and so may still be correct.
By the time I had finished the interpretation of
this awe-inspiring document, and thoroughly
explained the terrible clause in certificate of birth
referred to, the miserable Guiseppe quaked with
fear. He said, “I am a poor mana, and never see
one hundred pounda in my lifa, but you tell that
Govment Magistrato I can give hundred pounda
sweet potatoes, suppose very good all same. Per
Baccho ! ” I continued to explain that if the
money were not forthcoming he (Guiseppe) would
have to go to gaol for three years, whereupon
Guiseppe :“I think very good. Mr. you
write Magistrate and say I no time go gaol, too
much worka slaughteryard. I no belonga this
countree ; no understands. Grazie !! you tell
that Goment Magistrato gobloh ell! ’ ’ —Vlako.
Many a traveller approaching the mining
township of Lionsville, Solferino (N.S.W.) gold-
field, has gasped in astonishment when the
battlements of Ugilbar Towers came in view.
Ugilbar is certainly one of the finest homesteads
in Australia, and, although in the heart of the
bush, all the surroundings and appointments
savor of life. A large staff of servants is required
to keep the place in order, although the members
of the household are few. The house has been
built many years, and was only compiled after
long delay and many tiresome law-suits. The
labor was done by Germans, brought straight from
the Fatherland, who had signed an agreement for
a certain time at the appalling rate of £8 per year
and tucker—with no prunes in the cabbage-soup.
Finding, after their arrival here, that they were
working at far below current rates, many of the
kraut-eaters deserted their employment, thus
causing the importer a deal of trouble. The
owner of the place—he took up the virgin country
near 50 years ago—recently died, and, in accord-
ance with his dying wish, an old aboriginal who
had been all his life on the run, followed the coffin
as chief mourner. The deceased, in his will, made
provision for the nigger during his life. So Queen
“ Wittoria ” will not be bothered with appeals to
“ gib it blanket ” from that quarter.

The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
