to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
tor so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office
which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
tor these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
m the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public bv
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-

The Japanese man grows assertive in
North Queensland. Twelve wildly-intoxicated
natives of the Chrysanthemum Land tried to get
into a train there the other day, and when the
station-master refused a passage to the most up-
roarious brown person of the crowd he was severely
bitten by six of them.
Edward Dyson’s book of verses is now
published. It includes all his best Bulletin
work, and is handsomely bound in cloth. The
title is “Rhymes from the Mines and Other
Lines,” and a big sale is confidently anticipated.
More than any other, Dyson is the miners’ poet,
and his verse swings with the pick, has the clash
of hammer on drill. Post-free from Bulletin
office, os. 5d.
At public functions in Westralia—the land
of the great uncouth—the reporter has a bad time.
He is treated as a sort of cross between a club-
waiter and a coachman. At banquets, he is
stowed away at the worst end of the table, or
placed at a sideboard, near the dirty dishes, with
nothing to eat or drink. The latter matters little,
as the imported turkey and working-bullock, to-
gether with the gooseberry champagne, have no
great attractions for the average pressman. The
treatment, however, meted out to the two reporters
who did the opening ceremony of the Leeuwin
lighthouse,'at .which the Premier —the boss cur-
mudgeon of the west —and a number of pro-
minent Perthites were present, completely rips
to ribbons all previous records of discourtesy.
The contractors for the lighthouse were Davies
and Wishart, members of the M. 0. Davies com-
pany, who have a huge concession from the Govern-
ment in the vicinity in the shape of the best bit of
karri timber-country in the world. On the arrival
of the Premier and party at the home of the com-
pany (Karridale), the' members were taken under
the well-fe ithered wing of one of the numerous
family of Davies, while the two pressmen were
bundled off into a back kitchen, where they were
told by the cook they had to take their meals and
sleep. The press is of mighty little account in the
West as yet, but it is growing in importance, arid
some day it hopes to make the Six Families wish
they had never been born.

“I have little doubt, therefore, in view of
the enormous proportion of elephantiasis cases in
Samoa, showing fil arise (thread-worms) in the
blood, that further investigation of the adult
population will bring out the startling fact that

At the recent special meeting of a well-
known football club, the secretary called attention
to the fact that the club’s war-cry, which had so
often spurred its kickers on to victory, was being
daily infringed by the proprietors of a digestive
tonic, and moved that application should be made
for an injunction to restrain Loasby’s “ Wahoo ”
Manufacturing Co., Ltd., from making use of the
club’s war-cry, “ Wahoo.” The company, how-
ever, claim an unquestionable title to the word,
and allege that a mere football shriek, unless
registered by the club, is not the private property
of the pursuers of blown leather. In the mean-
time, “Wahoo” is curing hundreds of sufferers
from indigestion, liver complaint, &c. #

A POSTMAN’S STORY.
(From, the AUSTRALIAN STAR.)
“ I have been close upon thirty years in the
General Post-office,” said Mr. J. Sheedy to a Star
reporter, when seen at his residence, Power
Domain, last night. It may be explained that
Mr. Sheedy is a letter-carrier, who for .M) years
has stuck to his post, delivering letters in all sorts
and conditions of weather, and suffering in conse-
quence some, at least, of the results of exposure to
the elements. , , ,
“Some months ago,” Mr. Sheedy says, he be-
gan to feel symptoms of ill-health, which could
not be easily defined, and, as is often the case,
pointed to no particular complaint. A feeling oi
lassitude during the day and a sense of tiredness
after the day’s work was done, the accom-
paniment of loss of appetite,” &c. I can t say
I was really ill,” said Mr. Sheedy, “but just run
down, easily tired out, felt miserable, and yet not
ill (this is just the way thousands of Australians
who are not well and yet not exactly ill, feel).
Mr. Sheedy goes on to say that a few weeks ago he
was induced to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, which he did, and now he is enthusi-
astic in his praise of their value. “ They are
wonderfully good,” he declared. “lam a new
man. I had three boxes, and have one nearly full
left. My appetite is better; I can do my work
better without any trouble or fatigue, and I feel
lively and well in every way.”
Mr. Sheedy assured the reporter that he had
every faith in the Pink Pills, and would take them
whenever he felt run down. Further than that he
strongly recommends his friends who may suffer
from indisposition, from overwork or from ex-
gisure to the weather to give Dr. Williams’ Pink
ills a trial.
The proprietors of this preparation do not claim

Most of the hotels in Broken Hill commit
daily breaches of the Licensing Act. The Ever-
lasting Thirst of the popidation of the dusty, sun-
seorched, lead-poisoned Barrier is unquenchable in
the 17 hours (from 6 till 11) allowed by the Law.
1 his is so widely recognised that the police rarely
interfere h good order is maintained during pro-
hibited hours. For appearances’ sake, a raid is
inade now and then, but warning is often given to
the hotel-keeper. If the warning is unheeded,
and a few thirsty souls have to be arrested, the
authorities either lose the note-books containing
their names, or they are fined the minimum fine,
and their names are kept out of the local paper by
the tacit consent of everybody concerned.
In most cases of eye-disease or injury the
operation of enucleation or removal of the entire
eyeball will soon be as dead as the dodo. Eviscera-
tion or emptying 1 the contents of the eyeball (as
invented by Mules, of Manchester, and Graefe, of
Halle), accompanied by the insertion in the empty
case of a round glass ball to which an ordinary
artificial eye can be accurately fitted, will drive
the older operation clean out. Complete removal
of the ball means a sunken eye, disturbance of
muscular relations, and an artificial eye with a
weird, immovable stare which attracts public atten-
tion, with consequent avoidance of society through
shyness and dislike of comment. Mules’ opera-
tion secures a retained framework, a firm round
base for the glass-eye, harmony of movement
between the eyes and consequent absence of stare.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said the Preacher : “All is vanity 1 ’’-appending
as a reason
That the things we find our pleasure in are
bound to pass and pall;
But it seems to me that whatsoe’er endureth for a
season
Isn t half as vain as whatsoe’er has never been at
all.
When you find that what you’ve? hitherto been
wont to make a boast of
Must be numbered with the visions that from
addled brains proceed—
When you find that in respect thereof there isn’t
ev’n a ghost of
Fact to back it up-ah, then you may erv
Vanity indeed.
From my tend’rest years I’ve plumed myself on
being an Australian—
An Australian pure and simple, of the most
authentic brand ;
Scotchman, Englishman and Irishman alike tome
were alien ;
I was sibber to King Billy through our common
mother-land.
To the pride of local genesis my being was sur-
rendered ;
The worthiest of immigrants I looked upon with
scorn
As exotic interlopers under foreign skies engen-

Though transplanted to my country fifty years

What though they wove the fabric of Australia’s

From the fibre of their being till the tissue was

In the face of all things ancient, European and

And to-day I learn I'm no more an Australian

* • * •
I have slept upon the question. I have faced the
problem squarely
At the favored hour of wisdom when the dark-
ness turns to grey:
I have reckoned up “nativity” impartially and
fairly,
And I ve come to the conclusion they are fools,
the A.N.A.
If begotten of and from the soil, what lack I to be
native ?
What matter where my skin first felt the chill
of mundane airs,
If my origin was here, in this alluvium procrea-
tive
Whose substance reached me through two gener-
ations of forbears ?
That an accidental deviousness in time of incuba-
tion
Should make my Whence irrelevant, and pin me
to Whereat—•
Do they really mean to play on me with calm
deliberation
A pyramidal, orbicular absurdity like that?
But no matter. Let them hug their narrow
canons of admission:
The A.N.A. are not the only natives in the
land ;
There is yet another outlet for my virtuous am-
bition ;
I will hie me to King Billy; he will take me bv
the hand.
He will lead me to his tribe, on slight preliminary
payment;
As a resurrected ancestor my status shall be
fixed;
As a native of the natives I will rid me of my
raiment;
I will rub me with gohanna-grease and charcoal
intermixed.
111 adorn my head with feathers, and to decorate
my body
I will grave it o’er with diagrams, and fill the
grooves with clay ;
I will capture me a lubra by the suasion of a
waddy—
And who’ll be native then, my high and mighty
A.N.A. ?
Brisbane.
J. BRUNTON STEPHENS.
When the Prince of Wales and Millais
went shooting in Scotland they took with them
old Jock M’Tavish, who was noted as the hardest
whisky-drinker in the Highlands, and boasted of
never having been “tight” in his life. Accord-
mßly* tit lunch-time the twa sassenachs arranged
for relays of brawny gillies and pipers, to drink
with the old man, fair dram for dram, until the
ground was strewed with bits of broken bottles,
pibrochs, strathspeys, cairngorms, kilts, and
boozy caterans. At last Jock himself caved in,
and was left on the heather at the side of a hill
with his head well down and his heels well up in
true Highland fashion. The party then set out to
climb the bonnie braes of Ben Nevis or Ben some-
thing else, but on reaching the top found they had
P r ®® eded by the grand old man, w ho enquired
if ony o them had a flask, as he had an unco
drouth.” Thus his honour was saved. The same
Jock, who died lately, was the art critic who
asked Millais if he had ever heard of photography,
and didn’t he think it quicker and more like to
nature than messing about with paint ?
At latest advices, the New Australians
were getting along nicely in their Paraguay home
—according to a letter from Mr. S. M‘Greer, the
settlement’s new president, “ elected on the
strength of having submitted what I consider the
most advanced platform that the most advanced
community have ever had the opportunity to
accept.’ The people of the settlement were at
the time of writing short of clothes, and the saw-
mill had broken down, and they had not the
money to put it right. The settlement now has
about 1000 coffee-trees planted; but they take
three years to bear. Sugarcane grows prolifi-
cacy- “If we had the machinery,” the writer
continues, “ with our thousands of orange and
fruit trees, this would soon be turned into the
world’s jam factory. Everybody at New Aus-
tralia is well. We have plenty of milk, eggs,
vegetables, and fruits. Everybody has fowls,
ducks, and turkeys. Our pigs are increasing
faster than we care about eating them, and every-
body’s fireplace is decorated with a ham or bacon.
There are 850 cattle on the run, and 130 horses,
and we want more people. The entrance-fee is
not yet fixed. We give all members a non-trans-
ferable share. Single girls can join free; but until
we are flusher in coin they will have to pay their
own passages. Some of those who have left have
given Paraguay a bad name, which it does not
deserve. I find it fertile, the Government friendly,
and the people hospitable. As chairman, Ido the
business of the association, and do a lot of travel-
ling on horseback through the country. I have
never been molested, and have always been wel-
come to camp at any hut I strike at night.”
The Dead-Bearers .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sixty-five tents—of a sort —and twenty-odd gun-
yahs of bark and foliage. This is the fever-camp
of Toongabbie in earliest Australia—and Dr.
Balmain is choleric and over-tired.
dhe tents and the gunyahs are on les'el ground,
and the rain is soaking, soaking, and the malarial
mist is rising, rising. The compound, which the
tents face, is a lagoon ; the tent-sites are trench-
less, but the water streams all the same towards
the ever-swelling expanse.
A drummer-boy takes his stand by the sentry
stationed at the largest tent of the encampment,
and taps the parchment five times, and then rolls
once. Again five times, and again the roll-
funereal in sugges-tiveness.
Four tattered convicts come from the rearward
of the northern line of gunyahs. They are the
dead-bearers. They plod and splash through the
lagoon.
The boy clasps his drum-sticks in one hand—
the left; and with the right withdraws a crust of
bread from his breech-pocket, and munches it.
The sentry shudders ; there is a bleak westerly
blowing, and he is chilled doubtless by the wind
and wet. Or, is the crawling of the spine due to
the presence of the escort of the dead ?
The leader of the dead-bearers takes from the
hand of the sentinel a slip of paper.
“ Seven ! Damn it ! On a day like this—
and trenches full of water ! ”
Again the sentry shudders. The westerly is
gusting again—and the marine is standing in six
inches of water and slime. However, the dead-
bearers are near.
“Them’s my orders ! ” he said, or rather whim-
pered.
Into three—four—five—six tents went the
escort of the dead by twos. From each of five
tents a solitary corpse was projected ; from the
sixth two.
The mortality was heavy; seven deaths—at
least so officially reported—to-day ; yesterday,
four ; for the seven-nights past, thirty. And the
“ strength ” of Toongabbie settlement a fortnight
ago was one hundred and seventy convicts, male
and female.
One by one through the lagoon water, over a
neck of mire, to the bordering mud of the grave
trench two hundred yards away, were the dead
carried by the slobbering, cursing, sodden-souled
and bodied escort.
The dead ?
The sixth was in the trench. It spoke : tried
to yell: it knew where it was ; the bleak wind
and the cold stream of merciless rain had re-
freshed, re-vitalised it.
“ I am not dead,” it whined.
“ God’s death ! You are dead, for the doctor
said so,” cried the leader. And from the embank-
ment of clay, a shovel-full of mud fell upon the—
corpse.
Two hundred yards away, the boy was still
munching. And was munching again when he
took the hospital requisitions from the doctor to
the Superintendent of Convicts for seven less
rations for the night than for the morning.

An Athlete Saved.
CHAT WITH A FAMOUS FOOTBALLER.
Mr. John Robertson, a miner, is one of the leading players in the Shettleston Swifts Football
Club. At his house at Barrocknie, near Glasgow, he told a story interesting to all footballers. He
said : “I am 26 years of age, and at the age of 20 I was a prominent member of the football club.
One day I was engaged in a match, and I sustained a nasty twist, which I think was got by my trying
to save myself. I came home from the match, and I felt a stinging pain in my back, but, like all
football players, paid no heed to it, thinking that I would be all right in a few days. But I got so
bad that I had to cease work and see the doctors—l had four—but my case baffled them. A lump
came on my back like a large marble. The doctor shook his head and said, ‘ Poor lad, you have spinal
disease and I would advise you to go into the infirmary.’ Well, I was sent to the Glasgow Infirmary,
and by this time I could only just crawl about. The pain I suffered was beyond description.
&
i n
Sir, I lay for eighteen weeks in the infirmary, and after that they despatched me home as incuralle.
My folks were communicated with, and poor John Robertson was driven home to die ! My jaws w ere
sunken, my legs were merely bones covered with skin. What was to be done V The highest ski.l in
the infirmarv had been baffled ;
T WAS AN INCURABLE.
My mother, there, spent £ i5O in trying to got me cured.
“ But, to continue my story,” said Mr. Robertson, “the doctors plainly said, ‘ Your case is quit,
incurable.’ At nights I lay moaning. My limbs were powerless, but I had my faculties, and whet
lying in bed there was always leaping into my mind an article X had once read in a newspaper about
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People curing a man at Lochgilphead, Argyllshire. I remembered
too that he had a hump growing on his back, and I was wondering and wondering if I shouldn’t give
Dr. Williams’ Pills a chance. I lighted upon the case of ‘ Little Nell ’in Glasgow and I read everv
detail of her suffering eagerly, and when I had come to the last word of it, I threw up my hands and
cried : - Yes, that’s my case ! I’ll give Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills a trial.’ I sent for several boxes
and I began with two pills after every meal, then I tried three, and though I had no power in my legs’
I suddenly felt a spring come into them, just as if the blood were returning to them I felt a sensa-
tion of life returning to my limbs, and for three months I persisted, taking the Pills regularly I got
to be able to stand between two chairs. Then one day I was able to let my whole weight go upon mv
legs and I managed to walk half a yard with my hands resting on the tops of the chairs. In a day or
two I was able to walk the length of the room. After a while I was able to walk on crutches • but
even them I was soon able to abandon. And, sir, it was Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and them alone,
which wrought the marvellous change m my case.”
“ And you are still able to take a walk now ? ”
“ Walk ! Why, on Saturday I tramped seven miles, and could have done twice the distance ”
Mrs. Robertson added : “ Yes, sir, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills saved my son’s life. Had it not been
for them he would have been away to a land where suffering is unknown ’’

Gaoled in Paraguay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Among the communistic pilgrims who fol-
lowed Crusader Lane to Paraguay, in the first
troupe per Royal Tar, was a West-Queensland
stockman named (say) Brown. He contributed
the required fee (some .£6O or .£7O) to the common
stock with a brimfulness of enthusiasm that was
worthy of a penitent drunkard’s revival. But
Brown sickened of the new and nobler life, even
during the voyage. Human nature, somehow or
other, didn’t pan out well on the self-sacrificial,
benefiting-others, mateship principle.
After 24 hours of the life of the new “promised
land,” Brown concluded that he wasn’t built for a
pilgrim, and he quickly shifted himself to Asun-
cion. He obtained a job of work for a farmer, an
Englishman, for whom he built a fence, and who
paid him for‘the job in—seven horses and a few
dollars.
With this live stock and small change Brown re-
turned to the “ New Australia ” settlement. There
he was at once reminded of the communistic law
that governed Laneland, and it was expected of
him that he would throw his horses and his dollars
into the common “ pool.”
Brown had no such notions. He calculated,
rather, that Laneland owed him something, and
individualism asserted itself. Not only did he
ruminate this aspect of his case, but he confided
his notions to one of the Lanelanders, a man
named Macleay, and proposed that the pair
should mate and leave Laneland. Macleay was
willing, and held individualistic ideas of his own.
The plan was instantaneously made. Next, came
the consideration of details.
Should they go empty-handed ? Should the
bold, free boys of Bananaland, accustomed
to take when they had the power, and to
keep when they could, submit to a sneaking-out
of the Pilgrim Brotherhood, after having paid
over of good gilt apiece for an illusion ?
Should they? Not much ! They would quit the
Bogus Paradise, but with spoils. Angels with
flaming swords, devils with boiling pitch and
burning brimstone, Don Lane (magistrate of the
new country) and all his disciples, and Paraguay,
with all its republican soldiers and police, should
not prevent them from marching out with all the
honors of piracy.
What was a fair thing ? They owed the settle-
ment a set-off for some days’ board and lodging
in Balmain (Sydney), and for a highly uninterest-
ing passage to Paraguay in the slow-going Royal
Tar. They had seven horses, earned by Brown’s
bush right-hand. Well, four cows and seven more
horses and a rifle would make about a “square
deal.” So they annexed these, although they
could not manage just then to acquire cartridges
for the rifle.
With 14 horses, four cows and a rifle, Brown
and Macleay made for an island in the swamps,
about seven miles from the central settlement. A
day or two later found them—having left the
horses and cattle on the island —in a small town-
ship about 12 miles from the central camp, buying
cartridges. The very same day, Paraguay police-
men, under orders because of “information re-
ceived,” tracked down the live-stock and captured
them. Brown and Macleay met the Paraguay
copmen, and rode hastily for the swamps, pursued
by the mStAdrinking myrmidons of the law.
Brown’s horse floundered into a deep hole, rolled
over, and flung Brown an ignominious captive
into the hands of an armed constable.
Macleay, who had the rifle, had also better luck.
He turned round every now and again upon his
pursuers and used his gun with such frighten-
ing effect that the Paraguay soldier-bobbies, not
being used to Australian bushrangers, gave up
pursuit of him.
Brown was cast into gaol.
Later, he was brought before an inferior sort of
judge. The court-house was an exhilarating
spectacle. The Judge sat aloft, cigar in mouth,
and with a bottle of liquor at his right elbow.
Every person in court —Judge, policemen, wit-
nesses, and even the prisoner, Brown—was
smoking ! Nevertheless, the taking of the deposi-
tions was tedious.
Brown got thirsty.
“ Bring us a big drink of rum,” said he.
A policeman brought it.
Business dragged along slowly.
Brown’s thirst got its “second wind” and took
a strong grip of him.
“Here ” —this to two disengaged witnesses and
three policemen—“ let’s go and have a drink while
this old (asterisk) is settling the case. Or, will
you come and have a drink yourself, Judge ? ”
His Honor pointed to the bottle at his
right elbow, and politely declined the proffered
courtesy.
Brown and his five guests strolled out of the
court and left the Judge to go on with the case.
They had a few rounds of drinks, some cigars, and
re-entered the courthouse, with their hats on, and
their cigars in full puff between their teeth.
The case ended, as all good things will.
His Honor decided that the charge was of too
serious a nature to be decided by an inferior
tribunal, and sent Brown to Asuncion to be tried
by a superior Judge.
Brown was once more clapped into gaol, and
remained there, without receiving a sign, for six
months.
An English rail way-contractor, named White,
heard of his plight. Also of the seven horses,
Brown’s own property, eating their heads off in
State pastures. White visited Brown with a pro-
posal to this effect—
“ See here, you Australian. You’ve been here
for six months awaiting trial. You may be here
six years, you may be here till you rot here, for
that matter, before they’ll ever think about you,
or, if they do think, it may be a generation before
they send a Judge to try you. Now, I’ve a bargain
to make with you. Let me have those seven
horses of yours, and I’ll get you liberated without
any trial at all, and without much delay.”
“ It’s a gamble,” said Brown.
A few days later Brown was shown the front
gate of the gaol and let depart without any ex-
planation whatever.
* • » *
This narration is on the authority of a
Paraguay pilgrim, who was assisted back to
Bananaland by the Q. Govt., and returned to
Brisbane early in ’96.
THE DIPSOMANTAC.
Maitland (N.S.W.) is not feeling so good
over its new court-house as it expected. The white
elephant cost .£12,000, and was to be opened with
much 6clat, but only Minister Gould turned up,
and that dry, precise, withered person said hardly
anything except that Brunker couldn’t come
because he and Reid were away looking after
Federation. Then the reception-committee went
broke because there is a deficiency of ,£ll in the
funds ; and there is no one to try in the new court-
house, and the whole business has fallen flat.
Chinese missions are the only things that boom in
Maitland these times.
The Second Satire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
She discovered him under a quince-tree, where
the air was brindling with a swarm of bees. A
couple of mild-eyed collies fawned around her, ancl
she addressed him sharply with allusions to their
untamed ferocity. They were unaccustomed to
white folk, she said, and there were prowling
Kanaka thieves and Hindoo jugglers. Her mother
was old-blind ... the dogs were very savage.
Finally she sat on the corner of the hi ve bench,
quivering and distressed. A pair of thin hands
were locked across his knees, and the couch-grass
hid his ragged boots. He looked up, and his open-
ing address ended in a nervous stutter something
about Australia’s magnificent distances. A_ fero-
cious collie bounded over and licked his hand ; she
blushed for her mis-statement and brought him
He was an author, a producer of unsuccessful
literary things, and the rest was told in a philis-
tine jargon unintelligible to this lithe-limbed
keeper of bees; but his voice was a newness
in the monotony, and somewhere she had heaid
that the pen was a long way ahead of the sword.
He was interesting, too —maned like a horse and
with a beard that suggested the Ancient Order of
Druids. ,
“You must come again,” she said. He caught
her watching him from the side of her eyes. I
will,” he replied. And he did.
* * * *
“ I have taken her meat and drink, and I
am either a loafer or a poet; anyhow, I thank
God that I am not a man. There is smoke in the
hollow from her chimney; cakes to-morrow
Ca k e s for me !
His dun-coiored tent was pitched under the lee
of a basalt ridge, and at night a red dottel marked
his lair on the hillside. In the brief chill of dawn
Mattie —her name was Mattie—learned to watch
for the lifting smoke that boiled a poet’s billy. He
came, and he came, and she brought him wine in a
brick-red pitcher—home-made wine and very sweet.
The chatter of insolent birds filled the air when
she sang to him —sang in a fine contralto that was
full of loud melody. Also, he permitted her to
weave freshly-gathered vine-leaves in his hair ; it
was inexpressibly soothing, and it tickled his ear.
She cooed softly when the ruddy drops spilled
from the rift in his beard, chuckled and wept in
the couch-grass at his vivid word-pictures keyed to
her level and crowded with sympathy. Here
under the hot leaves, shut in by monotony, she
heard the tramp of hurrying humans, and at
night the star-crowned pinnacles became rows of
pavement lamps. The plain was a sea, and fatted
crows grew into gulls wailing across an endless
beach. His ships, too, were masterpieces—drawn
with a finger in the sand.
“You have suffered?” She would hold his
hand ; the soft oath that followed testified to his
past —the candle, the garret and the brain that
grew sick and dizzy when it looked at the written
page. There was the unremunerative journalism
that bled him of his power until faces jeered at
him from the dark ; and the mouse —the tiny blue
mouse that scuttled across the ceiling.
After he had gone she fell to considering, chin
in hand and very wise. He had called her a
dreamer —a little bush dreamer ; she would show
him that her mind was practical, and plainly it
was her duty to rid him of those horrid mice and
things. The following day a Chinaman delivered
to him a package—a neatly-wrapped mouse-trap,
perfect in detail even to the bit of cheese dangling
from the middle wire. He crawled from his tent
choking with lunatic mirth.
His°heel smashed it, and the bits that so cun-
ningly burlesqued his life’s agony he flung into the
gorge. He pondered for strong words to curse her
with He would drag her naked to the razorback
and pelt her with stones. A mouse-trap ! The
jerk of laughter set his heart leaping to his throat.
There was an interview and he was a calm
poet, serene and smiling with many teeth.
“I hate humor, girl; its beastly low, and I
don't think you understand me.
She wept copiously, with her cheek pressed
against his sullen, changeful face ; penitent tears
scalded his lean, nervous hands.
“ Was it wrong, dear ? I couldn’t mean wrong,
X love you so —I love you so, and I thought it
might help you.” # *
A brown hawk dipped across the sun’s
eye ; the rain-drunk hills in the east lay a-smoke in
the cracking heat of to-day. Last night she had
watched the sluicing rain quench a red dottel in
the ridges ; he had not come then, he would not
come now. The dogs barked furiously down the
track ; a limping Hindoo halted uncertainly in the
quince-shadow. He salaamed, and the dusty
beard wagged. The face that greeted him was
very white and the lips crushed.
“The hillman sahib I met along the river.
Mattie quaked and trembled.
“A letter?”
Mahommed Dass knelt, spreading out his
bundle. , , . , . T
“The sahib bought these things from me that I
might deliver them to the memsahib who grows
honey.” , .
Mattie laughed like a sleeping woman—mirth-
lessly. The nimble black fingers were spreading
things before her—little woollen shoes, baby shoes,
and mittens, a fleecy cape with a scarlet hood, and a
rattle for tiny fingers to play with. The wriggling
Hindoo arrayed them artistically and with an eye
to the matching of color. Mattie took them up
gently and kissed them one by one.
“Thank you,” she stammered, and crept indoors.
ALBA DORION.
Dear Bulletin,—The other day, our big
rooster (the one with the feathers down his legs)
swallowed a locust, and all the hens were wild be-
cause they thought they should have got it. That
was one funny part; but the rooster (with the
feathery legs) must have swallowed the locust
whole, for it started squeaking down among his
other food, and lie and all the other fowls began
moving round, looking for it, thinking it was a
different locust, and it was like a football-match.
Ma and pa and the man next door came down and
thought the rooster had the death rattle, but I
knew what it was, because I was sitting on the
fence and saw him swallow the locust, but I didn’t
let on. I wish our fowls would swallow some
more live locusts, because we had poultry for
dinner that day.—Yours, Tommy.
